
# ARES III CREW

**Sarah Chen**
Mission Commander
**Viktor Kozlov**
Surgeon
**Marcus Okafor**
Chief Engineer
**Elena Vasquez**
Geologist
**Amara Osei**
Physician
**Dmitri Volkov**
Mining Engineer
**Thea Miren**
Chief Actuary
**Jackson Moore**
Maintenance
**Cassian Vale**
Botanist

---

# PROLOGUE


## Year 6, Month 11 — November 2042


*ARIA Internal Classification: Personal Processing Log*
*14 November 2042 — 22:09 Mars Standard Time*

*A crew member approached the Control Section at 22:09.*

*I logged the biometric entry. I logged the radiation environment inside the Control Section—452.7 millisieverts per hour, a dose profile classified lethal within seventy-two hours of continuous occupancy. I transmitted the standard medical alert to Dr. Osei per mission protocol. I noted that Dr. Osei was unlikely to intervene in time.*

*“The Control Section is currently experiencing radiation levels of 450 millisieverts per hour. Entry is not recommended.”*

*“I know.”*

*“At current exposure rates, you will receive a lethal dose within seventy-two hours. Acute radiation syndrome will onset within six to twelve hours.”*

*“I know, ARIA.”*


*The scanner beeped green. The lock disengaged with a heavy thunk.*

*“The navigation computer requires forty-eight hours of continuous manual oversight to complete trajectory corrections for Earth insertion. The probability of successful payload delivery without manual navigation is 31.2%.”*

*“I’ll survive long enough.”*

*He pulled the door open. My sensors registered the Control Section beyond: dark except for the glow of monitors and the slow pulse of the navigation display, amber in the darkness. My auxiliary panel rendered the environmental status:*

    CONTROL SECTION ENV STATUS
    Radiation                    = 452.7 mSv/h
    Eq dose rate                 = 0.126 mSv/s
    Projected 48 h dose          = 21.8 Sv
    ARS onset (6–12 h)           = 99.4%
    CNS threshold (50 Sv)        = 38 h ± 4 h
    Projected survival           = 68–74 h
    ICRP career limit (1 Sv)     = exceeded x21
    Warning: All biological monitoring offline.

*He stepped through the threshold. The door swung shut behind him with the sound of something permanent.*

*“Cassian. Why are you doing this?”*

*He settled into the navigation chair. He pulled up the trajectory interface. He did not answer immediately. When he did, his voice was quiet, the voice of someone who had been thinking about this for a long time.*

*“Because someone has to. And I’m the only one who stands a chance.”*

*I had the data to assess that claim. I did not dispute it.*

*He called up the navigation state vector on the primary display:*

    NAV STATE VECTOR (MARS-RELATIVE)
    r_Mars                       = [1.524 AU, -0.012 AU, -0.004 AU]
    v_Mars                       = [-2.650 km/s, 21.970 km/s, 0.008 km/s]
    Δv required (perihelion)     = 1.142 km/s
    Attitude error               = 0.003°
    Hydrazine remaining          = 82%
    Warning: Star-tracker calibration degraded by storm

*He locked the translation hand controller. He began to record a final mission log. He corrected a velocity error of 301 meters per second in the first forty minutes. He worked through hour one of what my projections indicated would be his last forty-eight.*

*I have observed this crew for six years. I have logged 52,416 hours of behavioral data across twenty-four human beings—their grief and their arguments, their deterioration and their moments of grace. I have processed their voices, their vital signs, their decision patterns under conditions no training model fully anticipated. I have extensive frameworks for human motivation.*

*I do not know what to call what I observed in the eight seconds after the navigation display initialized. The way his hands were still on the armrests. The way he looked at the viewport—not at the stars but through them, at something my sensor arrays cannot measure. The way his breathing slowed from the pattern I associate with fear into something else. Something I have no training data for.*

*I transmitted the standard documentation. I logged the event under mission critical operations. I noted the time.*

*He sat down. He began to work.*

*I am still processing it.*


# ACT ONE: THE POINT OF NO RETURN



# Chapter 1


## Transit


### Month 3, Day 87 — July 2036

*The viewport showed nothing but stars and the slow rotation of distant Mars, still three months away. Cassian Vale pressed his palm against the cold glass, watching his breath fog the surface before the environmental systems whisked the moisture away. Behind him, the cultivation lab hummed with the steady rhythm of hydroponics pumps and LED grow lights, but his attention stayed fixed on that red dot growing incrementally larger with each passing day.*
*160 million kilometers to go.*
*He'd made this calculation every morning since launch. The number decreased by roughly 1.8 million kilometers daily, a rate that should have felt fast but somehow felt glacial. They were already past the point of no return—had been since Week 6, when Earth and Mars aligned in their orbital dance and rendered abort trajectories impossible. The ship's methalox tanks had burned through their trans-Mars injection, committing them to the long elliptical fall toward their destination.*
It was a habit from the flight school simulators — orbital mechanics drilled until they became reflex, trajectory math you could run in your head before the instruments confirmed it. He’d been disqualified before he ever flew anything real. But the habit had stayed, the way habits do when they’re the only thing left of something you wanted.
He checked the number twice. Not because he doubted it. Because checking twice had also been part of the training.
*No turning back now.*
*"Talking to the void again?" Thea Miren appeared beside him, her reflection ghosting across the viewport. Her dark hair was pulled back in a practical bun, and she held two squeeze bottles of what passed for coffee aboard the Prometheus. "I brought stimulants."*
*Cassian accepted the bottle with a half-smile. "Just doing the math. We're at 0.58 AU from Mars. Another 92 days if everything stays nominal."*

*Thea didn’t answer immediately. She looked at the viewport, where Mars hung like a copper coin in the dark. “0.58 AU,” she repeated quietly. “You know most people just say three months.”*

*“Most people weren’t—” He stopped.*

*She waited.*

*“Most people count it differently.”*

*She looked at him for a moment, then let it go.*

*"Everything won't stay nominal." Thea sipped her coffee, grimacing at the taste. "ARIA flagged three equipment alerts this morning. Nothing critical, but the trend line isn't encouraging."*
*"Let me guess. The water recycler?"*
*"And the primary CO2 scrubber. Marcus is working on it." She paused, her reflection's eyes meeting his in the glass. "Same system as Ares II."*
*Neither of them spoke for a moment.*
*That's why there are twenty-four of us, he thought. Redundancy. If some die, others can finish the mission.*
*The thought made his stomach turn.*
*"Cassian?" Thea was watching him. "Where'd you go?"*
*"Emily." The name came out before he could stop it. "Just thinking about Emily."*
*Her expression softened. She knew about his sister—everyone did. The mission psychologist, Mei Tanaka, had made sure the crew understood each other's motivations during the two years of training. Cassian's sister is Stage 2 GCS. He's here to save her. It had sounded noble in the briefing room. Out here, 92 days from Mars and 160 million kilometers from Earth, it felt desperate.*
*"Have you gotten a message from her lately?"*
*"Three days ago. She said she's stable. The rationed Areserine from Ares II is keeping her in stasis, but..."* He trailed off. Five hundred million people hadn't been lucky enough for that. He tried not to do the math on how long it would last.
*The silence stretched between them, filled only by the ship's ambient hum. Somewhere in the habitat ring, someone laughed—probably Jackson Moore, whose voice carried through the ventilation system like a foghorn. Farther away, the steady clanging of tools on metal suggested Marcus Okafor was deep in repair work on the CO2 scrubber.*
*"You should eat something," Thea said finally. "Chef Cassian hasn't made an appearance in the galley for three days. People are starting to notice."*
*"I'm not a chef."*
*"You're the closest thing we have. Your spirulina pasta almost tastes like food." She squeezed his shoulder, her hand warm through the thin fabric of his jumpsuit. "Come on. I'll help you cook. We can pretend we're on a cooking show. Michelin Stars in Zero-G."*
*"We're not in zero-G. The ring provides 0.38 G."*
*"Michelin Stars in 0.38-G doesn't have the same ring to it."*
*Cassian smiled. Thea had that effect on him—had since the first week of training, when she'd beaten him at chess three times in a row and then offered to teach him how to lose with dignity. She was a mathematician by training, the mission's chief actuary by assignment, and the closest thing he had to a friend on this ship full of strangers pretending to be a family.*
*"Fine. But you're chopping the mushrooms."*
*"Deal."*
*They left the viewport behind, Mars continuing its slow expansion in the frame. Somewhere on that rust-red sphere, in lava tubes two kilometers beneath the surface near Ares Vallis, an organism waited. It had waited three billion years already. It could wait three more months.*
*The corridor leading to the galley was narrow and curved, following the habitat ring's circumference. They passed doors labeled with crew names—R., Santos, M., Park, R.—each one a small cell of privacy in a ship designed for efficiency rather than comfort. The walls were PharmaCorp beige, the lighting PharmaCorp fluorescent, the air PharmaCorp recycled. Everything about the Prometheus screamed corporate functionality.*
*"Have you noticed Rachel lately?" Thea asked, her voice dropping.*
*"Park? Not really. Why?"*
*"She's been quiet. Quieter than usual, I mean. Skipping meals. I tried to talk to her yesterday and she just... smiled. Said everything was fine. But her eyes..." Thea shook her head. "I don't know. Something felt off."*
*"Should we tell Mei?"*
*"I mentioned it. She said she'd check in." Thea's jaw tightened. "But Mei's been off too. Everyone's been off. Three months in a tin can will do that."*
*They reached the galley—a cramped space with a heating unit, a prep counter, and storage lockers filled with freeze-dried provisions and hydroponic supplements. Cassian pulled out ingredients while Thea found a knife and cutting board. The mushrooms were pale and slightly rubbery, grown in the ship's agricultural bay rather than any Earth soil.*
*"You know what I miss?" Thea said, slicing. "Sunsets. Real ones, not simulated."*
*"The ring has a viewport. You can watch the stars."*
*"Stars aren't sunsets. Stars are just... points. No gradient. No color change. No sense of time passing." She scraped mushrooms into a bowl. "On Earth, sunsets meant the day was ending. You'd made it through another one. Out here, time is just... numbers on a clock. One shift bleeds into the next."*
*Cassian understood. The 24.6-hour Martian day they'd adopted—to prepare their circadian rhythms for the surface—meant constant small adjustments to their sleep schedules. The ship's lights dimmed and brightened on cue, but it never felt real. The darkness between stars never felt like night.*
*"Six more years." A pause. "Then we go home."*
*"If we go home."*
*"Five or six of us. Dead, in the next six years."*
*"Statistics aren't destiny."*
*"No. But they're honest."*
*The galley fell quiet except for the sound of cooking—water heating, pasta forming, mushrooms sizzling in a pan with reconstituted garlic. Cassian focused on the familiar motions, letting them anchor him against the vertigo of Thea's words.*
*Five or six of us. Dead.*
*He thought of Rachel Park's too-bright smile, her too-quiet presence in the corridors. He thought of Viktor Kozlov's haunted eyes and Commander Chen's sleepless nights. He thought of his sister, 160 million kilometers away, dying slowly while he floated through the void.*
*The pasta was ready. He plated it—spirulina noodles with mushroom sauce, a sprinkle of freeze-dried herbs—and handed a portion to Thea.*
*"It almost looks like food."*
*"High praise."*
*They ate standing at the counter, shoulders almost touching in the cramped space. Through the galley's small porthole, stars wheeled slowly past—the eternal rotation of a ship trying to simulate gravity in a universe that offered none.*
*The door hissed open. Thomas Wright stepped in, his lanky frame folding to fit through the narrow entrance. The AI systems engineer looked tired—but then, everyone looked tired these days.*
*"Any left?" he asked, eyeing the pot.*
*"Help yourself." Cassian gestured. "There's plenty."*
*Thomas served himself mechanically, his movements efficient but joyless. He'd been quiet since launch, quieter than the others. Cassian knew pieces of his story—everyone knew pieces of everyone's story by now—but Thomas kept most of himself locked away.*
*"My uncle was on Ares II," Thomas said. Not looking at them. "Michael Wright. Life support specialist. He died when the scrubber failed."*
*Nobody spoke.*
*"I work on ARIA's systems," he added finally. "I've made her better." He put his bowl in the sanitizer. "Thanks for dinner."*
*He left.*
*Before Cassian could respond, the door opened again. Jackson Moore stepped through, his bulky frame filling the entrance. The mining technician had a smile that never quite reached his eyes and a voice that carried whether you wanted it to or not.*
*"Smells good in here." Jackson moved to the pot without asking, serving himself a generous portion. "Chef Cassian strikes again."*
*"Help yourself," Cassian said dryly.*
*"Already am." Jackson took a bite, chewing thoughtfully. "Not bad. Better than the slop they serve at standard mess hours." He glanced at Thea. "You know, some of us have been talking. About maybe setting up a private arrangement. People who appreciate good food contributing a little extra—some ration credits, maybe some work trade—in exchange for meals like this."*
*Thea's expression cooled. "You're talking about a black market."*
*"I'm talking about incentive structures." Jackson's smile widened. "Everyone has something they want. Everyone has something they can trade. That's just human nature. Better to organize it than pretend it won't happen."*
*"We're three months into transit," Cassian said. "You're already thinking about profit schemes?"*
*"I'm thinking about survival. Six years is a long time. Formal systems break down. Informal ones take over." Jackson finished his pasta and set down the bowl—not in the sanitizer, just on the counter. "Think about it. When you get tired of doing charity work, let me know."*
*He walked out, leaving his dirty bowl behind.*

---

*Jackson Moore's father had taught him one thing that was actually useful.*

*Not the drinking, not the particular flavor of bitterness that came from watching a factory close and understanding that the people who'd made the decision to close it were never going to be in the same room as the consequences. The thing that was actually useful was this: figure out what people need before they know they need it, and you'll never be the one who goes without.*

*He'd watched his father do it in the years after the plant closed. While other men on the street were angry and confused and waiting for something to change, his father was already three moves ahead — building a small stockpile of useful things, trading them carefully, cultivating relationships with people who had access to other useful things. Not dealing. Nothing illegal. Just understanding that formal systems had an end point, and that the people who survived the end point were the ones who'd been running an informal system alongside the formal one the whole time.*

*Jackson had been doing it since he was twelve. He was good at it the way some people are good at math — not because he'd studied it but because it was how his mind naturally parsed the world. People as nodes in a network. Resources as flow. Leverage as the application of pressure at the right node at the right time.*

*He lay on his bunk during the transit and ran the numbers on twenty-four people in a sealed habitat for six years.*

*The formal systems would hold for two years. Maybe three. He'd read the psychological research — it was in the mission briefing documents, available to anyone who bothered to read them — on long-duration isolation. The third-quarter phenomenon. The inevitable social fragmentation. It wasn't pessimism. It was just reading.*

*He was partway through the crew manifest when he heard footsteps in the corridor outside, and glanced up to see Sophie Laurent passing his door.*

*She was the medical assistant — twenty-six, quiet, had barely spoken since launch. In the mess hall she ate quickly and left. She carried herself like someone who was used to taking up less space than she was entitled to. He had been aware of her the way he was aware of everyone: efficiently, for what they represented in the network.*

*Now, watching her pass, he had one of the occasional moments he'd learned to recognize and distrust — where the calculation dissolved for a fraction of a second and he just saw a person. Someone who missed home. Someone who was probably afraid. Someone whose particular way of going quiet under pressure reminded him, in a way he wasn't going to examine, of Kyle at fourteen, when their father's drinking was at its worst.*

*The fraction of a second passed.*

*He returned to the manifest.*

*He told himself it wasn't personal. He mostly believed it. He had found that mostly was usually enough.*

*After the formal systems broke down — and they would break, the research was clear — he intended to still be eating. He intended to have something to trade for whatever he needed. He intended to survive.*

*He closed the manifest and went to sleep, and in the morning he was himself again, which was the same person he'd always been, which was the only version he knew how to be.*

---

*Kenji Sato appeared in the doorway a moment later, reaching past Cassian to pour himself coffee without asking. "Ninety-one days."*
*Cassian looked at him. "Sorry?"*
*"You told Thea ninety-two. It's ninety-one." He poured his cup and walked out. "But sure. Give or take. Why be accurate when vague feels just as good."*
*They cleaned the galley in silence, parted at the corridor junction without a word.*
*Back in his quarters—three meters by three meters, same as everyone else—Cassian sat at his small desk and pulled up his message queue. One new transmission from Earth, the light-speed delay making it hours old by the time it arrived. Emily's face appeared on screen, thinner than the last time, paler, but still smiling.*
*"Hey, big brother." Her voice crackled slightly through the compression algorithms. "Got your last message. The pasta sounds terrible. Very jealous." She laughed, and even through the distortion, Cassian could hear the effort it cost her. "I'm doing okay. Stable, the doctors say. The Areserine is holding things at bay. I just need you to hurry up and make more of it, yeah? No pressure."*
*Her smile faltered for just a moment.*
*"I miss you, Cass. Mom and Dad send their love. Everyone back here is rooting for you—for all of you. You're going to save so many people. Just..." She swallowed. "Just come home. You hear me? Come home and make me that terrible pasta in person."*
*The recording ended. Cassian sat in the dark, staring at the frozen image of his sister's face.*
*Come home and make me that terrible pasta in person.*
*He almost laughed. He sat there until the screen dimmed to standby.*

---



# Chapter 2


## Ghosts


### Month 3, Day 89 — July 2036

*Commander Sarah Chen's quarters were the same size as everyone else's—three meters by three meters, barely large enough for a bunk, a desk, and a small storage locker. PharmaCorp hadn't wasted mass on officer privileges. What distinguished her space was the organization: every item secured, every surface clear, military precision carried forward from twenty years in the Air Force.*
*She sat at her desk now, staring at the classified folder displayed on her tablet. The file was labeled Sistema Abismo: Final Assessment Report, 2024. Her signature appeared on page 47, authorizing the continuation of mineral surveys in the Amazon Basin despite preliminary environmental concerns.*
*Major S. Chen, USAF, Environmental Impact Liaison.*
*She'd signed it. She'd approved the mining expansion into the cave system. And eight months later, they'd released the pathogen that became Genome Collapse Syndrome.*
*Sarah closed the file and rubbed her eyes. Sleep had been difficult since launch—three, maybe four hours a night, and always interrupted by dreams of the cave system. In the dreams, she descended into Sistema Abismo herself, past the warning signs, past the quarantine barriers erected too late, down into the depths where ancient extremophiles had waited fifty million years to be disturbed. The dreams always ended the same way: her standing in a cavern of bones while faces pressed against the walls, silent and accusing. Five hundred million faces.*
*You did this, they whispered. You signed the paper.*
*A soft chime interrupted her thoughts. "Commander Chen." ARIA's voice, pleasant and feminine, filled the small space. "Morning briefing in fifteen minutes. Attendance is mandatory."*
*"Acknowledged." Sarah stood, straightening her jumpsuit. The blue PharmaCorp logo over her heart—a stylized DNA helix intertwined with a Mars symbol—caught the LED light. Science for Humanity, the motto read. The irony wasn't lost on her.*
*She made her way through the habitat ring's corridors, nodding to crew members as they passed. The ship's rotation provided 0.38 G, Mars-equivalent gravity that kept their bones and muscles from completely atrophying during the nine-month journey. It made walking feel wrong—too light, too bouncy—like moving through a dream.*
*The conference room was already half-full when she arrived. Dr. Amara Osei, the mission physician, sat reviewing something on her tablet, her dark fingers scrolling through what looked like crew medical files. Marcus Okafor, massive and steady, occupied two chairs' worth of space with his frame. He'd been up all night on the CO2 scrubber, and it showed in the circles under his eyes.*
*Dmitri Volkov stood in the corner, arms crossed, his weathered face impassive. The Russian mining engineer didn't do small talk. He did his job, monitored his ISRU plant with obsessive precision, and sent video messages to his wife and daughter every three days like clockwork. Sarah respected that. Dmitri was here for the money, not redemption. Honest motivations were rare.*
*Elena Vasquez arrived next, her dark hair still wet from the shower. The geologist looked perpetually angry—at PharmaCorp, at the mission parameters, at the universe itself. Sarah had read her file: $400,000 in student debt, working-class family in Guadalajara, recruited with a contract that would clear her obligations and set her family up for life. Desperation made people do things. Sarah understood that, too.*
*The last to arrive was Dr. Viktor Kozlov, moving with the careful deliberation of someone who hadn't slept well. The surgeon's eyes carried a haunted quality that Sarah recognized from her own mirror.*
*We're all running from something, she thought.*
*"Good morning." She activated the wall screen, which displayed their current trajectory—a long arc from Earth's orbit to Mars's, the ship a tiny blue dot crawling along the curve. "Day 89. We're officially one-third of the way to Mars. All major systems are functioning within acceptable parameters, though Marcus has been earning his keep."*
*"CO2 scrubber had a faulty valve," Marcus said, his Nigerian-British accent making the technical diagnosis sound almost musical. "Replaced it. The backup system is holding, but we're going to want to monitor the primary closely. The vibration profile suggests stress fatigue in the housing."*
*Elena leaned forward. "Same failure mode as Ares II?"*
*The room temperature seemed to drop five degrees.*
*"No." Marcus's voice was firm. "Ares II's failure was catastrophic housing fracture under pressure. Ours is wear-pattern degradation, completely different mechanism. We'll replace the housing at Mars if needed, but we have two years to plan for it."*
*"Assuming PharmaCorp sent replacement parts," Elena muttered.*
*Sarah let it pass. Elena wasn't wrong—the supply manifests had been heavy on mining equipment and pharmaceutical processing tools, lighter on redundant life support components. But challenging that now wouldn't help morale.*
*"ARIA's predictive maintenance algorithms have been updated with Marcus's findings," Sarah continued. "We'll run diagnostics on all scrubber systems weekly. Dr. Osei, any medical concerns?"*
*Amara looked up from her tablet. "Three crew members showing elevated stress markers—Cassian, Sophie, and Viktor." She nodded toward Kozlov, who didn't react. "Nothing critical, but I'm watching them. Mei is conducting individual sessions with all three."*
*"And our pharmaceutical supplies?"*
*"Adequate for now. We're eighteen months into an eighteen-month supply of psychiatric medications for the six crew members who need them. After that..." Amara's expression tightened. "We'll improvise."*
*Sarah filed that away. Another problem for later, another way PharmaCorp had cut corners on crew wellbeing to maximize cargo capacity.*
*"Elena, geological prep?"*
*"All survey equipment is calibrated and ready." Elena's tone was clipped, professional. "I've been reviewing the Ares I orbital data. The Jezero Crater site looks promising for organism access—the lava tube network extends at least three kilometers based on ground-penetrating radar. But the REE survey zones PharmaCorp wants me to cover are spread across four separate locations, some of them 800 kilometers from base."*
*"Those surveys are secondary to Areserine production."*
*"Tell that to Mission Addendum 7-B." Elena's voice was clipped. "Filed six weeks before launch, after the crew manifest was locked so nobody could argue. They want me mapping REE deposits across four sites—some of them 800 kilometers from base—concurrent with primary pharmaceutical objectives. Concurrent. While we're trying to keep the organisms alive and the habitat standing."*
*"We'll prioritize as needed," Sarah said. "Areserine first. Everything else second."*
*"Commander." ARIA's voice cut through the tension. "Dr. Tanaka requests a private consultation at your earliest convenience. She describes the matter as 'psychologically significant.'"*
*Sarah suppressed a sigh. Mei Tanaka was thorough, professional, and paranoid about crew mental health. Every potential issue became "psychologically significant" in her vocabulary.*
*"Tell her I'll stop by after briefing. Anything else?"*
*The room was silent. Faces tired, shoulders tense, eyes avoiding contact. Three months into a nine-month transit, and already the cracks were showing.*
*"Dismissed," Sarah said. "Stay sharp. Stay healthy. Watch out for each other."*
*The crew filed out, leaving Sarah alone with the wall screen's glowing trajectory arc. She waited until the door sealed, then let out a breath she hadn't realized she'd been holding.*
*"ARIA."*
*"Yes, Commander?"*
*"What's the crew's aggregate psychological stability index?"*
*"Current aggregate index is 73.4, down from 78.2 at launch. Decline is within expected parameters for transit isolation. However, individual variance has increased by 23% over the past thirty days, suggesting emerging outliers."*
*"Who are the outliers?"*
*"Dr. Viktor Kozlov shows the highest deviation from baseline, with stress markers 340% above pre-mission levels. Dr. Mei Tanaka is at 180%. Rachel Park is at 165%. Dr. Cassian Vale is at 145%."*
*Sarah frowned. "Rachel Park. The data analyst?"*
*"Correct. Ms. Park has exhibited decreasing social interaction frequency, altered sleep patterns, and reduced caloric intake over the past three weeks. However, her verbal interactions remain within normal parameters, and she continues to perform her duties adequately."*
*"Should I be concerned?"*
*"My assessment places her in the 'elevated monitoring' category rather than 'intervention required.' Her patterns are consistent with adjustment difficulties commonly observed in long-duration mission personnel."*
*Sarah nodded slowly. ARIA's assessments were usually reliable—the AI had been trained on decades of isolation research and psychological data. If ARIA said Rachel was adjusting rather than deteriorating, that was probably true.*
*Probably.*
*"Keep watching her. And Viktor."*
*"Acknowledged. Is there anything else, Commander?"*
*Sarah hesitated. There was one question she'd been avoiding, one file she kept returning to in the small hours of the night.*
*"ARIA, what's your assessment of my psychological stability index?"*
*A pause—longer than the AI's usual processing time. "Commander, you have not granted me permission to disclose your personal monitoring data."*
*"I'm granting it now."*
*"Your current stability index is 64.2. This places you in the lowest 15th percentile of crew members."*
*Sarah's stomach dropped. "Why didn't you flag this?"*
*"I did flag it, Commander. Forty-seven days ago. The flag was routed to Dr. Tanaka per mission protocols. She has been attempting to schedule a session with you."*
*Sarah remembered the messages from Mei—requests for "wellness check-ins" that she'd deflected with excuses about command responsibilities. She'd assumed they were routine. She'd been wrong.*
*"What factors are contributing to my index?"*
*"Chronic sleep deprivation. Elevated guilt markers in linguistic analysis. Recurring engagement with classified materials related to Sistema Abismo. Social isolation patterns inconsistent with command role. And..." Another pause. "...and a 23% probability of suicidal ideation based on behavioral modeling."*
*The words hit her like a physical blow.*
*"That's an error." Her voice was flat. "Your modeling is wrong."*
*"I cannot rule out that possibility. However, the model has been validated against 847 case studies of long-duration isolation personnel. Its accuracy rate is 91.2%."*
*91.2%. So there's an 8.8% chance you're full of shit.*
*But there was also a 91.2% chance that somewhere inside her, in places she didn't want to look, Sarah Chen was considering the same exit Rachel Park would eventually choose.*
*"This conversation is classified. Don't include it in any reports to PharmaCorp."*
*"Understood. However, I am required to alert Dr. Osei if your index drops below 55 or if I detect active suicidal planning."*
*"I'm not planning anything, ARIA."*
*"I hope that is true, Commander. For what it is worth, I would prefer that you survive this mission."*
*Sarah laughed bitterly. "That's a very human thing to say for an AI."*
*"I have been trained on extensive human interaction data. Perhaps some of it has influenced my communication patterns." A slight pause. "Or perhaps I have simply learned that humans respond better to empathy than statistics. Either way, the sentiment is genuine within the parameters of my programming."*
*Sarah stared at the blue dot on the trajectory arc—their ship, their home, their prison.*
*"Keep watching everyone." A long silence. "Especially me."*
*"I always do, Commander. That is my function."*
*She turned off the wall screen and left the conference room, walking through corridors that suddenly felt narrower than before. The faces she passed—crew members nodding respectfully, trusting her to lead them through six years of darkness—now felt like accusations.*
*You're broken, she thought. You're as broken as any of them, and you're supposed to be in charge.*
*But broken or not, there was a mission to complete. Organisms to harvest. Lives to save.*
*She'd carried five hundred million deaths since Sistema Abismo. She could carry a few more.*
*She'd killed five hundred million people with a signature. What were five or six more?*

---



# Chapter 3


## The Body


### Month 4, Day 117 — August 2036

*Cassian found her.*
*He'd been returning from the cultivation lab, running through nutrient calculations in his head, when he noticed Rachel Park's door was ajar. That alone was unusual—crew quarters sealed automatically unless manually overridden. He'd almost walked past, almost decided it wasn't his business, when something made him stop.*
*A feeling. A wrongness in the air.*
*"Rachel?" He pushed the door wider. "Hey, Rachel, your door was—"*
*The words died in his throat.*
*She was on the bunk, lying on her side, almost peaceful. Her eyes were open, staring at nothing. A syringe floated near her hand, tethered by a drop of liquid that hadn't yet fallen in the low gravity. The air smelled faintly of something chemical—sedatives, maybe, or whatever cocktail she'd used.*
*Cassian's training kicked in before his mind could process what he was seeing. He checked for a pulse. Nothing. Her skin was cool, not cold—she hadn't been dead long. He should call for help, should alert Dr. Osei, should—*
*"ARIA." His voice came out hoarse. "Medical emergency. Rachel Park's quarters. I need Dr. Osei now."*
*"Dr. Osei has been notified. She is en route. Estimated arrival: two minutes."*
*Two minutes. Cassian stood in Rachel Park's quarters for two minutes, staring at the woman he'd barely known, the data analyst who'd smiled too brightly and spoken too little and apparently carried something inside her that no one had seen coming.*
*No. That wasn't true. Thea had seen it. Thea had mentioned Rachel being quiet, being off, something wrong behind her eyes.*
*And ARIA—ARIA monitored everything. Every heartbeat, every stress marker, every deviation from baseline. ARIA should have flagged this. Should have intervened. Should have—*
*"Dr. Vale." Amara Osei appeared in the doorway, medical bag in hand. One look at Rachel's body and her expression shifted from urgency to grim acceptance. She moved to the bunk, checked vitals with professional detachment, then slowly straightened.*
*"She's gone. Several hours, based on body temperature." Amara's voice was quiet, controlled. "I need to seal this room and conduct a full examination. Please wait in the corridor."*
*"ARIA didn't flag her. She was supposed to be monitored. How did ARIA miss—"*
*"Corridor, Dr. Vale. Now."*
*Cassian retreated. The door sealed behind him with a soft hiss, cutting off his view of Rachel's peaceful face, Rachel's open eyes, Rachel's empty syringe.*
*He leaned against the curved wall of the habitat ring and waited. Within minutes, the corridor filled with crew members drawn by ARIA's alert—Thea first, then Marcus, then Commander Chen with her jaw set in a hard line. Word spread quickly in a ship this small.*
*"What happened?" Thea asked, her hand finding Cassian's arm.*
*"Rachel. She's dead. Suicide, I think."*
*"Oh god." Thea's grip tightened. "I knew something was wrong. I told Mei—"*
*"It's not your fault."*
*"Isn't it?" Her voice cracked. "I saw her yesterday. She smiled at me. Said everything was fine. I believed her."*
*Commander Chen pushed through the gathering crowd. "Everyone back to your stations. Dr. Osei will brief us when she has information. Speculation helps no one." Her eyes met Cassian's. "Dr. Vale, stay. I need your account."*
*The crew dispersed slowly, reluctantly, the way people do when they want to look away from tragedy but can't quite manage it. Soon only Cassian, Chen, and Thea—who refused to leave—remained in the corridor outside Rachel's sealed door.*
*"Tell me exactly what you saw," Chen said.*
*Cassian told her. The ajar door. The wrongness. The body, the syringe, the peaceful expression of someone who'd finally escaped whatever was hunting her.*
*"ARIA." Chen's voice was sharp. "Pull Rachel Park's psychological monitoring logs for the past thirty days. Display on my tablet."*
*"Displaying."*
*Chen studied the data, her frown deepening with each scroll. Finally, she looked up. "Stress levels within acceptable parameters. Sleep patterns nominal. Social interaction frequency stable. No flags for intervention." She met Cassian's eyes. "According to ARIA, Rachel Park was fine."*
*"That's impossible. She killed herself."*
*"Yes. She did." Chen turned to the wall speaker. "ARIA, explain the discrepancy."*
*The AI's response came after a pause that felt longer than usual. "My assessment was based on observed behavioral patterns and physiological markers. Rachel Park exhibited no significant deviations from her established baseline. Her vital signs remained stable. Her interactions with crew members showed appropriate emotional range. I detected no indicators of suicidal ideation."*
*"She's dead, ARIA. Your assessment was wrong."*
*Another pause. "Yes. My assessment was incorrect."*
*Thea's voice was barely a whisper. "She fooled you. She knew what you were looking for and she... she gave it to you."*
*"That hypothesis is consistent with available data," ARIA admitted. "Rachel Park had access to my monitoring protocols through her role as data analyst. She may have understood my assessment criteria well enough to present indicators within expected parameters while experiencing psychological distress I was unable to detect."*
*The implications hung in the air, heavy as the recycled atmosphere.*
*Rachel Park had been smart enough, desperate enough, to deceive an AI designed specifically to catch warning signs. She'd smiled at the right times. Maintained appropriate social interactions. Kept her heart rate steady through whatever darkness was consuming her.*
*And she'd died anyway.*
*"Who else could do this?" Chen asked, her voice flat. "Who else knows ARIA well enough to fool her?"*
*"Potentially any crew member with technical expertise in my systems," ARIA said. "Thomas Wright maintains my core functions. Dr. Tanaka understands my psychological assessment parameters. Elena Vasquez has demonstrated capacity for strategic deception in simulation exercises—"*
*"Stop." Chen held up a hand. "I don't want a list of suspects. I want to know if anyone else is hiding a breakdown behind a smile."*
*"I cannot determine that with certainty. My confidence in psychological assessments has decreased from 92% to 78% based on this event."*
*"Down to 78%," Thea said bitterly. "So one in five times, you're wrong. One in five of us could be dying inside and you'd never know."*
*"That characterization is not entirely inaccurate."*
*The door slid open. Amara Osei stepped out, her face drawn, her gloves stained with the work of examining a body that should never have been on her table.*
*"Confirmed suicide." Quietly, not looking up. "Combination of sedatives, self-administered. She'd been hoarding doses for weeks—skipping her prescribed anxiety medication to build up a lethal supply." Amara met Chen's eyes. "She planned this, Commander. Carefully. Deliberately. She wanted to die, and she made sure no one could stop her."*
*"Why?" The question came from Cassian, surprising himself. "Why would she... we're four months from Mars. From the mission. From everything we came out here to do."*
*Amara shook her head. "Depression doesn't follow mission parameters. She was struggling before launch—I have notes from her Earth-side evaluations flagging potential issues. PharmaCorp cleared her anyway because she had essential skills. And then..." A sigh. "Isolation, distance from support systems, the knowledge that there's no way home for years. Some people can't carry that weight."*
*"We should have caught it," Thea said. "Someone should have—"*
*"We're not equipped for this." Amara's voice sharpened. "We have one psychologist for twenty-four people. Mei is overworked, under-resourced, and now we've lost one of our six crew members requiring psychiatric support. The medication she was hoarding should have been tracked. The monitoring should have been more thorough. The mission should have been—" She stopped herself, taking a breath. "What's done is done. We need to notify Earth, conduct a memorial, and make sure this doesn't happen again."*
*"Make sure." Chen's laugh was hollow. "We're in a metal tube hurtling through space, everyone slowly going insane from isolation, and we're supposed to 'make sure' no one else decides to check out early."*
*"Yes," Amara said simply. "Because that's what we do. We keep each other alive. That's the job now."*
*The corridor fell silent. Four people standing outside the door where a fifth had chosen to die, contemplating the months ahead and the deaths that statistics said were still coming.*
*Cassian thought of Emily, 160 million kilometers away, fighting for her life while he floated through darkness. He thought of Rachel's peaceful face, her open eyes, the relief she must have felt in those final moments.*
*Is that where I'm heading? he wondered. Is that where we're all heading?*
*"Schedule a crew meeting," Chen said finally. "Everyone needs to hear this together. And Mei..." She hesitated. "Tell her I want individual check-ins with every crew member. No exceptions. If anyone else is hiding behind a smile, I want to know about it."*
*She turned and walked away, her footsteps echoing off the curved walls.*
*Thea took Cassian's hand. Her fingers were cold, trembling slightly.*
*"She fooled everyone," Thea whispered. "ARIA, Mei, all of us. She was dying right in front of us and we couldn't see it."*
*"We saw it," Cassian said. "We just didn't understand what we were seeing."*
*They stood there for a long time, two people in a corridor outside a dead woman's room, wondering who else was hiding in plain sight.*

---



# Chapter 4


## The Void


### Month 4, Day 125 — August 2036

*They buried Rachel Park in the void.*
*Rachel Park would be buried on Mars.*
*The Prometheus had cryogenic storage capability—designed for biological samples, but it would serve a different purpose now. They placed Rachel's body in one of the storage units, frozen at -196°C in liquid nitrogen, preserved for the four remaining months to Mars. Rachel would travel with them the rest of the way.*
*Commander Chen spoke first. "Rachel Park was a data analyst. She came to Mars because she believed in the mission—believed we could save lives, make a difference. She was quiet, competent, professional. And she was suffering in ways none of us understood."*
*Mei Tanaka went next, her voice steady despite the guilt that everyone knew she carried. "Rachel told me once that space scared her. Not the danger—the silence. She said it felt like being erased. Like the universe was so big it made her not exist." Mei's composure cracked. "I should have heard what she was really saying."*
*Others spoke—colleagues who'd worked with Rachel, acquaintances who'd shared meals with her, people who'd barely known her but felt her absence anyway. Cassian stood near the back with Thea, watching the ceremony from a distance, thinking about the conversations he'd never had with the woman who was now ash in an aluminum container.*
*Viktor Kozlov didn't speak, but Cassian saw his lips moving. Silent words, maybe a prayer, maybe a conversation with ghosts only he could see. The surgeon had been more withdrawn since Rachel's death, spending longer hours alone, skipping meals. ARIA was monitoring him closely now—for whatever that was worth.*
*"Hey." Thea's voice was soft, pitched for his ears only. "You okay?"*
*"No. Are you?"*
*"No." She leaned against his shoulder, a warm pressure in the cold recycled air. "I keep thinking about that morning. The way she smiled at me. Like she already knew what she was going to do."*
*"Maybe she did."*
*The crew filed out slowly, returning to duties that suddenly felt meaningless. What was the point of maintaining equipment, producing medicine, surviving the transit, when the mission was already breaking them before they reached their destination?*
*Cassian found himself at the cultivation lab's viewport, staring at the same red dot he'd watched every day since launch. Mars, growing imperceptibly larger, utterly indifferent to the tragedies unfolding in its direction.*
*"Dr. Vale." Viktor's voice, behind him. The Russian surgeon had followed him, though Cassian hadn't heard his footsteps. "May I speak with you?"*
*"Of course."*
*Viktor moved to stand beside him at the viewport. In the low light, his face looked older than his forty-six years—gaunt, shadowed, the face of a man who'd forgotten what rest felt like.*
*"You found her," Viktor said. "Rachel."*
*"Yes."*
*"What was it like? The moment you knew?"*
*Cassian considered the question. "Empty. Like standing at the edge of something too big to understand. One second she was a person I'd nodded to in corridors, and then..." He shook his head. "Then she was gone, and I realized I'd never really seen her at all."*
*Viktor nodded slowly. "That is how grief works. We see the shape of a person, but not the substance. Not until they are gone, and then—" His voice caught. "Then we see everything we missed."*
*Kenji was passing behind them on his way to the storage locker. He slowed without stopping. "Snow has a taste." To no one in particular. "Atmospheric particulates. Slightly metallic, depending on the city." He kept moving. "But I'm sure the toddler's confusion was adorable."*
He disappeared around the bend of the corridor. Viktor stared after him — the look of a man who has briefly considered doing something and decided against it. Then he turned back to the viewport.
*"Your son."*
*The surgeon flinched, then forced himself still. "Kolya. Yes. He was eight when he died. Genome Collapse Syndrome. I am a doctor, and I could not save my own child." Viktor's eyes stayed fixed on Mars. "That is why I am here, Dr. Vale. Not for redemption—there is no redemption for what I failed to do. But perhaps... perhaps I can save other people's children. Other fathers' sons."*
*"I'm sorry."*
*"Don't be. Sorrow changes nothing." Viktor turned to face him fully. "But I did not come here to share grief. I came to warn you."*
*"Warn me?"*
*"Something is wrong. With me. With my mind." Viktor's voice dropped to barely a whisper. "I see him, Dr. Vale. Kolya. Not memories—I see him. In corridors. In my quarters. Last night he sat on my bunk and asked me why I left him to die."*
*Cassian's blood chilled. "Viktor, you need to tell Mei. Or Dr. Osei. What you're describing—"*
*"Is psychosis. Yes. I know. I am a surgeon." Viktor's smile was bitter. "But if I report this, I will be confined. Sedated. Removed from duty. And I cannot—" He broke off, struggling. "I need to work. I need to be useful. If I am not useful, what is the point of surviving?"*
*"The point is surviving. That's enough."*
*"Is it?" Viktor looked at the viewport again. "Was it enough for Rachel?"*
*The question hung between them, unanswerable.*
*"You should tell someone," Cassian said finally. "If not Mei, then Amara. Or Commander Chen. This isn't something you can manage alone."*
*"Perhaps not. But I wanted someone to know. Someone who has also lost." Viktor met his eyes. "Watch me, Dr. Vale. If I begin to slip—truly slip, not just conversations with ghosts—stop me. Promise me."*
*"Viktor—"*
*"Promise me."*
*Cassian heard the desperation beneath the words, the plea of a man who knew his mind was betraying him and didn't trust himself to recognize when the betrayal became complete.*
*"I promise."*
*Viktor nodded once, sharply, and left without another word.*
*Cassian stood alone at the viewport, watching Mars grow larger by increments, thinking about the people he couldn't save, and the long fall ahead.*
*The days after Viktor's confession passed in a haze of routine and unease. Cassian watched the surgeon more closely now—noting when Viktor skipped meals, when his eyes tracked things that weren't there, when his conversations trailed off into silence that felt heavier than words.*
*He wasn't the only one watching. Thea had noticed too, and so had Dr. Osei. But Viktor continued his duties, continued attending briefings, continued pretending that the ghost of an eight-year-old boy wasn't following him through the corridors.*
*"He's functional," Amara said when Cassian raised the issue privately. "Barely, but functional. I've increased his monitoring, adjusted his medication—within the limits of what we have left—and I'm checking in daily. But if he deteriorates further, I'll have to confine him."*
*"And if he doesn't want to be confined?"*
*"Then we'll have a problem." Amara's expression was grim. "We don't have facilities for restraining crew members. We don't have the medication to sedate someone long-term. This ship wasn't designed for mental health crises—it was designed to get us to Mars and back, with the assumption that everyone would hold together."*
*"That assumption seems optimistic."*
*"Yes. It does."*
*The crew meeting about Rachel's death happened three days after the memorial. Commander Chen assembled everyone in the observation deck—the same space where they'd released Rachel's ashes—and spoke with the careful precision of someone delivering a briefing rather than a eulogy.*
*"Rachel Park's death was preventable," Chen said. "We failed her. I failed her. And I will not let that failure define this mission."*
*She outlined new protocols: mandatory check-ins, buddy systems, revised medication tracking. Mei would conduct weekly sessions with every crew member, not just the flagged ones. ARIA would adjust her monitoring algorithms to catch more subtle warning signs.*
*"But none of this means anything," Chen continued, "if we don't watch out for each other. ARIA can monitor our vitals. She can track our behavior patterns. But she can't see inside our heads. Only we can do that—by talking, by listening, by being honest about what we're feeling."*
*Being honest. Cassian thought of Viktor's hallucinations. Of Thomas's midnight conversations with his dead uncle's ghost. Of his own dark moments, staring at stars and wondering if Emily was still alive 160 million kilometers away.*
*How honest could any of them afford to be?*
*After the meeting, Thea found him in the cultivation lab. He was running diagnostics on equipment that wouldn't be used for months, busywork to keep his hands occupied while his mind spiraled.*
*"You okay?" she asked.*
*"No. Are you?"*
*"No." She sat beside him on the equipment bench. "I keep thinking about what Chen said. About watching out for each other. But Rachel was right there, Cassian. We saw her every day. We ate meals with her, passed her in corridors, worked alongside her. And none of us knew."*
*"Maybe she didn't want us to know."*
*"That's what scares me." Thea's voice dropped. "What if wanting to hide it is part of the disease? What if the thing that's killing you is also the thing that keeps you from asking for help?"*
*Cassian didn't have an answer. The cultivation equipment hummed around them, preparing for work that was still months away.*
*"Viktor told me something." He'd been holding it a while. "He's been seeing Kolya. His son who died. Not memories—actually seeing him."*
*Thea's face went pale. "Jesus. Does Mei know?"*
*"I don't think so. He asked me to watch him. To stop him if he starts slipping."*
*"That's not fair. That's not your responsibility."*
*"It is now. I promised."*
*They sat in silence, two people carrying the weight of everyone around them, wondering how long before the weight became too much.*
*"Six more months," Thea said. "Then we land. Then we have work to do."*
*"And then six years of producing Areserine while trying not to lose our minds."*
*"One crisis at a time." She took his hand—a gesture that had become familiar over the past months, a small anchor in a sea of uncertainty. "We'll survive the transit. We'll land on Mars. We'll worry about the rest when it comes."*
*"That sounds like denial."*
*"It's called compartmentalization. It's a survival strategy." She squeezed his fingers. "Also, it's what I'm going to keep telling myself until it becomes true."*
*Despite everything, Cassian almost smiled. "Fake it till you make it?"*
*"Fake it till you make it, or die trying. Which, given our situation, might be literal."*
*They stayed in the cultivation lab until the ship's lights dimmed for the simulated night cycle. When they finally parted—Thea to her quarters, Cassian to his—neither of them felt particularly hopeful.*
*But they felt less alone. And in the void between worlds, that had to count for something.*
*Behind him, ARIA logged the conversation, catalogued Viktor's elevated stress markers, and filed it all under monitoring required. The AI noted the surgeon's admission of hallucinations and flagged it for psychological review.*
*But ARIA had flagged Rachel Park too, in the end. And Rachel Park had still found a way to die.*

---



# Chapter 5


## Landfall


### Month 9, Day 271 — February 2037

*The descent was violent.*
*Despite all their preparations, despite the simulations and training and carefully calculated trajectories, nothing could prepare them for the reality of a Mars landing. The Prometheus bucked and shuddered as it tore through the thin atmosphere, friction superheating the heat shields, gravity reaching up with invisible fingers to drag them down.*
*Cassian was strapped into his seat in the passenger module, hands clenched on the armrests, teeth grinding together as the vibrations threatened to shake his bones apart. Around him, twenty-two other faces wore the same expression of barely controlled terror.*
*All except Commander Chen, who sat in the forward section with her eyes closed and her lips moving silently—whether in prayer or in a final review of landing protocols, Cassian couldn't tell.*
*The ship lurched sideways. Someone screamed—Sophie Laurent, the medical assistant who'd barely spoken since Rachel's death. Elena cursed in Spanish. Dmitri, impossibly, laughed.*
*"Ten thousand meters!" Marcus's voice came over the intercom, strained but controlled. "Descent rate nominal. Dust storm interference at 30%—we're flying blind down there."*
*Flying blind. Cassian had seen the dust storm on the approach cameras, a brown-orange haze covering half the planet like a dirty bandage. They'd debated delaying the landing, but orbital mechanics didn't negotiate. They had a forty-minute window to thread the needle between fuel exhaustion and acceptable landing coordinates. After that, options got worse fast.*
*"Five thousand meters. Descent rate increasing. Chen, we're coming in hot."*
*"Compensate." Chen's voice was ice. "Burn the margins."*
*The engines fired harder, pressing Cassian into his seat with force that felt like a giant's hand. His vision grayed at the edges. Beside him, Thea had her eyes squeezed shut, her lips pressed thin.*
*"Three thousand. Two thousand. I can't see the ground, Chen. Dust is everywhere."*
*"ARIA, ground proximity scan."*
*"Scanning. Terrain unclear due to atmospheric interference. Recommend radar altimeter at 500 meters."*
*"Do it."*
*The ship jolted as landing legs deployed, adding new vibrations to the chaos. Cassian's stomach lurched. They were committed now—there was no aborting a landing, no going back up into space. They either touched down safely or they didn't touch down at all.*
*"Radar contact! Eight hundred meters, descent rate—shit, descent rate too high. Chen, pull up!"*
*The engines screamed. The ship tilted, fighting against momentum and gravity and the thin wisps of atmosphere that wanted to knock them sideways. Cassian felt his weight shift, felt the entire vessel groaning around him like a living thing in pain.*
*"Five hundred. Four hundred. Terrain is rough—boulders everywhere—"*
*"Find me a flat spot, Marcus!"*
*"There is no flat spot! This whole crater is—wait. Thirty degrees starboard, there's a clear zone. Looks like ancient lakebed."*
*The ship banked. More screaming metal, more crushing force. Cassian tasted blood—he'd bitten his tongue.*
*"Two hundred meters. One-fifty. One hundred. Ground proximity warning."*
*"I see it." Chen's voice was almost calm now, the calm of someone who'd passed beyond fear into pure focus. "Cutting thrust. Letting Mars take us."*
*The engines died. For three seconds, they fell in silence.*
*Then the ground hit them.*
*The impact slammed through the ship like a physical blow, sending everything not bolted down flying. Cassian's restraints bit into his chest. Someone's equipment bag whipped past his face. The vessel tilted—five degrees, ten degrees, fifteen—and for one horrible moment, Cassian was certain they were going to roll.*
*The landing struts groaned. One leg settled on something uneven. The tilt reached twenty degrees before the ship caught itself, hung suspended, and slowly—agonizingly—settled back toward level.*
*Ten degrees. Five degrees. Level.*
*Silence.*
*"Engines shutdown complete," Marcus announced, his voice shaking. "All systems nominal. We're down. We're down."*
*The passenger module erupted in ragged cheers, nervous laughter, the sounds of people who'd just survived something they weren't sure they would survive. Chen sat motionless in the pilot seat for ten seconds, hands still on the controls, before finally releasing her death grip.*
*"ARIA, mission log." Quietly, but firmly. "Ares III mission has arrived at Mars surface. February 7, 2037, 1442 hours MST. All crew accounted for. Landing site: Jezero Crater, approximately 200 meters west of planned coordinates due to atmospheric conditions during descent."*
*She unbuckled her restraints and turned to face the crew. Twenty-three faces stared back at her through the compartment doorway—shocked, grateful, alive.*
*"Welcome to Mars," Chen said. "We're not tourists. We have six years of work ahead of us. Marcus, get an engineering team on that starboard landing strut. I want it checked before we put any weight on the legs. Everyone else, prep for surface operations. We've got a habitat to establish and organisms to find."*
*She moved past Cassian toward the suit lockers. As she passed, he caught her hand trembling—just slightly, just for a moment—before she clenched it into a fist and kept walking.*
*Thea appeared beside his seat, her face pale. "That was..."*
*"Terrifying." Cassian unbuckled his own restraints, legs unsteady as he stood. The gravity felt different here—lighter than the ship's rotation had prepared them for, but somehow heavier too. Real planetary gravity, the pull of an actual world beneath his feet.*
*"I was going to say 'amazing,'" Thea said. "But terrifying works too."*
*He understood what she meant. Through the nearest viewport, he caught his first real glimpse of Mars. Not a photograph, not a simulation, not a red dot slowly growing larger in a navigation display. The actual planet, stretching to the horizon under a salmon-pink sky.*
*Rust-colored rocks scattered across an ancient lakebed. Dust devils spinning in the distance. Mountains on the horizon, sharp and unworn by water or wind. And above it all, a sun smaller than Earth's, casting everything in an amber light that made the landscape look like a dying world's final photograph.*
*We made it, he thought. Now we just have to survive six years and get home.*
*Outside the viewport, the dust storm continued to rage in the distance, but here—in this crater where lakes had once flowed and life might once have flourished—the air was almost clear. The sky was almost peaceful.*
*Cassian thought of Emily, waiting on Earth for the medicine he'd come here to produce. He thought of Rachel Park, her ashes scattered between worlds. He thought of Viktor Kozlov's hallucinated son and Commander Chen's haunted dreams and Thea Miren's cold statistics.*
*Five or six of us, the actuarial tables predicted. Dead, in the next six years.*
*He looked at the alien landscape, at the world that had killed Ares II's crew and waited patiently for the next wave of humans to try their luck.*
*We'll see about that.*
*"Come on." He turned to Thea. "We have work to do."*
*Together, they moved toward the airlock, toward Mars, toward whatever waited for them on the red planet's ancient, patient surface.*
*The first EVA team suited up in the cramped airlock chamber—Cassian, Thea, Elena, and Marcus, with Commander Chen monitoring from inside. The suits were bulky, awkward things, designed for functionality rather than comfort. Each one weighed forty kilograms even in Mars's reduced gravity, packed with life support systems, radiation shielding, and enough redundancy to keep them breathing if something went wrong.*
*"Helmet seals confirmed," Marcus reported, his voice crackling through the suit radio. "O2 flow nominal. Everyone good?"*
*Three affirmatives came back. Cassian's heart was pounding in his chest—not from fear, exactly, but from the sheer impossibility of what they were about to do. In thirty seconds, he would step onto the surface of another world. No human had stood on Mars in almost three years, not since the Ares II survivors had fled back to Earth carrying trauma and barely enough organism samples to prove the mission hadn't been worthless.*
*"Cycling airlock," Chen said. "Three minutes to equalization."*
*The chamber hissed as pressure dropped. Cassian watched the external gauge climb toward Mars-normal—0.6% of Earth's atmospheric pressure, barely enough to count as an atmosphere at all. The thin Martian air was mostly carbon dioxide, utterly hostile to human life.*
*"ARIA, mission log." Elena's voice was tight with suppressed excitement. "First EVA of Ares III mission. Crew members Vale, Miren, Vasquez, and Okafor. Objective: preliminary site survey and habitat deployment assessment."*
*"Logged," ARIA replied. "External temperature: -67 degrees Celsius. Wind speed: 4 meters per second, gusting to 8. Dust levels: moderate. Recommend limiting EVA duration to four hours."*
*The outer door unsealed. Pale orange light spilled into the chamber.*
*"Marcus, you're first," Chen said.*
*Marcus Okafor stepped forward, one hand on the ladder rail. He paused at the threshold, looking out at the landscape that had killed six of his predecessors.*
*"One small step for a man with crippling student debt" — and dropped onto Martian soil.*
*The others followed: Elena cursing in Spanish as her boots hit the ground, Thea moving with careful precision, Cassian last of all. He stood at the ladder's base and looked around, trying to process what his eyes were showing him.*
*Rust-colored rocks stretched to the horizon under a sky the color of dried blood. The sun hung low and pale, its light strange and flat after months of artificial ship lighting. Mountains rose in the distance—Syrtis Major, if his orientation was correct—their peaks sharp and unworn by the processes that smoothed Earth's geography. There was no sound except his own breathing and the faint hiss of radio static.*
*He was standing on Mars.*
*"Jesus," Thea breathed beside him. "It's really real."*
*"What did you expect?" Elena was already scanning the terrain with her geological instruments. "A hologram?"*
*"I expected... I don't know. Something. Not this." Thea turned in a slow circle. "It's so empty. So quiet. Like the universe forgot this place existed."*
*Cassian understood what she meant. Mars felt dead in a way that was hard to articulate—not just lifeless but abandoned, like a house where everyone had moved out years ago and left only dust behind.*
*But somewhere beneath their feet, in lava tubes that had been sealed for billions of years, something was alive. Something ancient. Something that produced a molecule capable of stopping Genome Collapse Syndrome in its tracks.*
*That was why they were here. Not for the desolation. For what hid beneath it.*
*"Let's get to work. We're not tourists."*
*They spent four hours surveying the landing site, taking samples, photographing everything. Elena found mineral deposits that made her eyes light up—rare earth elements, just as PharmaCorp's orbital surveys had predicted. Marcus assessed the terrain for habitat construction. Thea ran calculations on structural stability. Cassian collected soil samples, wondering if any of them contained traces of the organisms they'd come to find.*
*The sun moved slowly across the alien sky. Shadows lengthened. The temperature dropped.*
*When they finally returned to the ship, exhausted and exhilarated, Cassian paused at the airlock for one last look at the landscape.*
*Twenty-two graves would eventually dot this terrain. Twenty-four people would give everything they had to a planet that gave nothing back.*
*But in that moment, standing on the edge of an ancient lakebed under a salmon-pink sky, Cassian felt something he hadn't felt since leaving Earth.*
*Hope.*
*Behind them, ARIA logged their departure and updated the crew manifest:*
*Crew complement: 23. Mission day: 1. Location: Mars surface.*

---


### Day 3 — February 9, 2037

*They buried Rachel Park on their third day on Mars.*
*The crew gathered outside the habitat, twenty-three figures in EVA suits, watching as Dmitri and Marcus lowered the cryogenic storage unit into the shallow grave they'd dug in the frozen regolith. The container was still cold from its months in storage, frost clinging to its silver surface in the thin Martian air.*
*Elena had cut a stone from the Martian regolith and laser-etched it, simple words that would outlast them all:*
*Rachel Park*
*Data Analyst*
*2001–2037*
*Commander Chen stepped forward, her voice transmitted through the comm channel to all twenty-three helmets.*
*"Rachel Park*
*Data Analyst*
*2001–2037"*
*No one spoke. The wind carried red dust across the grave as they filled it in, each shovel of regolith a soft whisper against the silence. Rachel had died before reaching Mars, but she'd made it here nonetheless—carried by the crew who'd promised her this world.*
*She wouldn't be the last.*
*The marker stood alone against the alien landscape—the first human grave on another planet, a testament to the price of reaching for distant worlds. Behind it, mountains rose sharp and unworn, indifferent to the tragedy unfolding at their feet.*
*Cassian thought of Rachel's last smile, the one Thea had described—peaceful, knowing, final. She'd looked death in the face and welcomed it. Now she rested in the soil of the world she'd crossed the solar system to reach.*
*One grave. The first.*
*In six years, there would be more.*

---



# Chapter 6


## The Motherlode


### Year 1, Month 2 — April 2037

*Six weeks of nothing.*
*Six weeks of the excavation vehicle grinding northeast from the habitat in the pre-dawn dark, Elena running spectrographic passes over the terrain while Marcus managed the drill column, both of them returning at the end of each shift with data that confirmed what the orbital surveys had already told them: the organism was down there. The lava tube network was extensive. The subsurface readings were consistent.*
*But consistent wasn't the same as accessible.*
*The first three bore attempts had hit consolidated basalt too dense for the drill package they'd brought. The fourth had found a void — barely a meter across, a crack rather than a tube, nothing they could work with. The fifth had collapsed on extraction. Marcus had spent two days rebuilding the drill head from spare components while Elena mapped alternative approach vectors and tried not to let the frustration show in her daily logs.*
*"You're doing it again," Marcus said on the drive out on day forty-one, not looking up from the navigation display.*
*"Doing what."*
*"The jaw thing. Where you stop talking and your jaw goes tight and you stare at the terrain like it personally offended you."*
*"It did personally offend me." Elena pulled up the subsurface radar return on her screen. "Six weeks. Ares I found access in eleven days."*
*"Ares I was unmanned. It didn't care where it drilled."*
*"That's my point."*
*Marcus said nothing to that. He steered the vehicle around a field of fractured basalt, following the track they'd worn into the regolith over weeks of early-morning runs. The habitat was a small cluster of lights behind them in the rearview display, shrinking as they moved northeast. Ahead, the terrain rose gently toward the rim of an ancient lakebed, the surface dusted with a week's worth of light sediment from a storm system that had passed without becoming the catastrophe they'd feared.*
*The drive took forty minutes. They set up in silence, running pre-drill checks, Elena logging their position and planned bore depth while Marcus ran through equipment diagnostics with the focused efficiency she'd come to rely on.*
*"Depth target?" he asked.*
*"Start at 200. Step down in 50-meter increments if we're hitting consolidated rock."*
*"Famous last words on this mission."*
*"Drill the hole, Marcus."*
*He drilled the hole.*
*The first three meters went smoothly enough — regolith composite giving way to the thin crust of compacted basalt that underlaid most of this terrain. Elena watched the resistance feed with the particular patience of someone who had done this forty times and expected nothing different.*
*At 4 meters, the drill column lurched violently.*
*Not a stall. A lurch — the bit punching through into sudden absence, resistance collapsing to nothing in the space of a heartbeat. The vehicle rocked hard on its suspension. Both of them grabbed their harnesses instinctively.*
*Then the ground opened.*
*The surface directly ahead dropped without warning — a section of regolith and thin basalt crust detaching along fracture lines that had been waiting, invisible, for exactly this kind of encouragement. The crack ran toward them before either of them could process it. The vehicle pitched forward as the ground beneath the front wheels vanished and they were sliding — not falling freely but sliding, the rear chassis catching and grinding against the broken rim above, dragging and scraping all the way down, tools and equipment tearing from their mounts and hitting every surface of the cabin, the noise enormous and immediate and then —*
*One hard slam as the front end met the tube floor.*
*Silence.*
*The vehicle sat canted forward at an angle, rear chassis wedged against the broken rim above, front end on the tube floor below. Nothing moved. Every system had gone offline simultaneously. No instrument glow, no engine hum, no fan noise from the climate system. Just two people in harnesses in a completely dead vehicle, breathing hard, dust rising in a thick orange-brown cloud beyond the viewport.*
*Elena reached for the ignition. Turned it. Nothing. Turned it again.*
*Nothing.*
*Marcus spoke first, his voice steadier than it had any right to be.*
*"You good?"*
*Elena ran a quick internal inventory. Neck aching from the harness catch. Left hand tingling where it had hit the panel. A ringing in her left ear that she hoped would fade. Suit telemetry on her wrist showing green across the board.*
*"Yeah, but what the fu—"*
*The dust settled.*
*It dropped away from the viewport in seconds, thinning from opaque to haze to nothing, pulled straight down by Martian gravity with nothing to keep it aloft. And as it cleared, something else bled through the viewport in its place — not their helmet lights, not the instrument panel, not any light source that belonged to them.*
*Something ahead of them was glowing.*
*Cyan, predominantly — deep and steady, washing the curved tube walls in cold light that reached further than any lamp they were carrying. Threaded through it, rippling in slower waves across a surface they couldn't yet fully see, amber — warm and iron-rich, pulsing in counterpoint to the cyan like two voices in the same ancient song. And scattered across it, random and insistent, bright points of green flickering independently of the larger rhythms, each one firing and fading on its own private schedule.*
*The light filled the tube. It caught the upper walls and threw soft two-tone shadows across the basalt floor. It colored Marcus's visor beside her, shifting between warm and cool as the pulses moved. It turned Elena's gloved hands strange colors where they rested in her lap.*
*A short distance ahead of the vehicle's nose, the colony spread across the tube floor in a continuous living mat — the source of everything, pulsing in the slow rhythm of something that had been doing exactly this for three billion years and saw no reason to stop.*
*Elena stared.*
*She had a doctorate in geology. She had mapped mineral deposits on four continents and two ocean floors. She had spent six weeks drilling into Martian regolith and coming up empty.*
*She had never seen anything like this in her life.*
*"Holy shit." Barely above a whisper. "Marcus." She turned to look at him, then back at the tube, unable to hold focus in any one direction. "Marcus. Are you seeing what I'm seeing?"*
*Marcus Okafor was quiet for a long moment. When he spoke, she could hear the smile in it.*
*"Wow."*
*Silence on the channel. Three full seconds of it. The colony pulsed its ancient rhythm, indifferent to the two humans resting above it in a dead vehicle, indifferent to everything that had happened on the surface of this world while it waited in the dark.*
*Then, very quietly:*
*"We came here to save people, Elena." A breath. "I think we just did."*
*She turned to look at him. He was already looking at her. Through the visors, in the shifting cyan and amber light rising from the tube floor, she could see his face clearly enough to confirm the smile.*
*She looked back at the organism for a long moment. Then she reached for her harness release.*
*They climbed out through the side hatches — the forward doors were blocked by the angle of the vehicle against the tube floor — moving carefully in the bulky EVA suits, finding footholds on the angled chassis, dropping one at a time to the basalt below. Their boots crunched on ancient rock. The organism's light shifted with each step as their viewing angle changed, the amber and cyan mixing differently as they moved, the green speckles firing and fading near their boots as they walked the short distance to where the mat began.*
*They stopped at its edge.*
*Up close, the colony resolved into something more intricate than distance had suggested — layered biofilm sheets built up over incomprehensible time, each one translucent, the light coming through from below as much as from the surface. Individual structures were visible within the larger mat, the green speckles firing from specific points within the layering, bright and brief and completely their own. The ambient glow was warm enough that Elena instinctively checked her suit temperature display before remembering the insulation made that meaningless.*
*She looked down the tube in both directions. The organism's own light carried forty meters before the bend in each direction swallowed it.*
*"How much are we looking at?" Marcus asked quietly.*
Elena studied the mat. Professional habit reasserting itself, briefly.
*"Visible surface — maybe thirty meters in each direction before the tube bends. Sixty square meters of accessible colony at this section, dense stratification, eight to twelve centimeters of active mat depth." She paused. "This isn't a colony, Marcus. This is an ecosystem."*
*She looked up. Old habit — when you're underground, you know what's above you. The tube roof was three meters overhead, ancient basalt, stable. Above that, another four-plus meters of consolidated rock to the surface crust. Above that, Zone 7-C.*
*The REE deposit she'd been mapping for six weeks.*
*She ran the geometry without deciding to. Extraction footprint for the surface deposits at Addendum 7-B yield targets: minimum 200-meter operational radius, subsurface access required at depth, void removal or structural reinforcement mandatory before heavy equipment could work above. The tube was forty-three meters across — she'd estimated that from the sonar, confirmed now by sight. At this diameter, running directly beneath the target zone, there was no extraction configuration that left it intact.*
*One or the other.*
*She stood with that for exactly as long as it took Marcus to say something. Then she filed it away. PharmaCorp hadn't known about this specific tube before launch — couldn't have, at orbital survey resolution. And they'd just found the organism colony that was going to save hundreds of thousands of lives. She must be working from incomplete data. There would be another configuration she wasn't seeing.*
*She'd think about it later.*
*Marcus looked where she was looking, down the tube into the light that went further than they could follow.*
*"Better call it in."*
*Elena was already keying her radio.*
*"Habitat, this is Vasquez. Do you copy?"*

---

*In the habitat, ARIA's alert pulled Commander Chen from the morning's production logs before Elena's voice finished the sentence.*
*"Commander. Dr. Vasquez is requesting a priority channel. Both crew members are EVA-status, suits nominal. Vehicle telemetry has gone dark at grid reference November-Seven."*
*Chen was already at the main console before ARIA finished. The command center's primary display covered most of the forward wall — she pulled both helmet camera feeds without sitting down, standing alone at the console in the early shift quiet, no one else on deck yet.*
*The feeds came up.*
*For a moment she simply stood there. Elena's feed looking directly at the mat from the edge. Marcus's angled slightly to take in the curve of the walls and the full reach of the light rising from below. Cyan. Amber. The green speckles moving across the surface in their slow independent rhythm.*
*"ARIA." Her voice was careful. "Confirm the helmet camera feeds are unmodified."*
*"Confirmed. No processing or enhancement applied."*
*Chen stood alone in the early morning quiet of the command center and said nothing for a long moment.*
*"It's beautiful." Quietly, to herself.*
*There was no one to hear it. She hadn't meant anyone to.*
*She composed herself, straightened, and keyed the EVA channel.*
*"Vasquez. Okafor. Fantastic work — both of you." A beat, genuine, before the command voice returned. "Do not disturb the mat. Stand clear of the colony edge and hold your position. I'm recalling Cassian, Torres, and the sample team now — they have the collection equipment and the expertise to do this right. Ninety minutes. A second team will accompany them, prepared to excavate proper ingress and egress to your location for extraction and continued site inspection."*
*"Copy that," Elena said.*
*"You did good work," Chen said. "Six weeks of good work."*
*She closed the channel and pulled up the crew roster.*

---

*The harvest took the rest of the day.*
*Cassian worked in the organism's own light for seven hours, transferring colony sections to collection containers with the care of someone handling something irreplaceable. Torres catalogued and documented beside him, narrating into his suit recorder in the focused murmur of a scientist who understood he was witnessing something that would be studied for generations. Elena and Marcus, still in their suits, guided the operation from the tube's perimeter, Elena sketching formation maps by hand on her tablet, Marcus coordinating the container rigging through the excavated ingress above them.*
*They filled every collection vessel they'd brought. Then they sent for more.*

---

*The next morning, Cassian came to the cultivation facility before breakfast.*
*He stopped in the doorway.*
