*All ten tanks were at full capacity — substrate dense, colony established, every vessel glowing at an intensity the facility had never reached before. The room was brighter than it had ever been, cyan and amber playing across the walls in overlapping waves, green speckles firing steadily through the early morning quiet.*
*He stood there for a while, not moving.*
Thea found him there a few minutes later, handing him one of two cups of what passed for coffee. She stepped up beside him, looked at the tanks, and didn't say anything, watching the tanks alongside him.
*Then her hand found the middle of his back. Warm. Steady. Just there.*
*"How many?" she asked eventually. Quietly.*
*"At current production rates?" He did the arithmetic without needing to think about it. "Enough for a full payload by return window. Three hundred thousand people, first year of distribution."*
Thea was quiet.
*"Three hundred thousand." As if trying to make the number real.*
*"Give or take."*
*Her hand moved slightly against his back.*
*"Emily's in that number."*
*He didn't answer. He didn't need to.*
*They stood together in the doorway until the coffee went cold, watching the ancient light pulse in all ten tanks, and for the first time since leaving Earth, the mission felt like something they might actually survive.*

---


---


Marcus found Cassian in the equipment bay running inventory checks on the cultivation supplies, which was the kind of work that required just enough attention to justify doing it alone.

He pulled up a crate and sat on it without asking. For a while he said nothing.

“You know who Viktor is.” Eventually.

“Surgeon. Moscow Medical Institute. Reconstructive and neurological specialist.” Cassian kept counting. “I read everyone’s file during transit.”

“Then you know the haptic interface work.”

“Remote surgery. Yes.”

Marcus was quiet for a moment.

“I grew up in Ibadan. My father coached football — youth teams, nothing professional, but he was good at it and the boys loved him. There was a player on his under-seventeen squad. Emeka. Quick, technically gifted, the kind of player that makes the people watching him feel lucky.” He turned a nutrient canister in his hands. “Spinal injury. Collision during a match, 2029. C5-C6. He was sixteen.”

Cassian set down his tablet.

“The hospital in Lagos said the window for intervention was gone. Four weeks post-injury, secondary damage, they didn’t have the specialist capability anyway. Emeka would have some function. Not what he’d had.” Marcus set the canister down. “Viktor Kozlov performed the procedure remotely from Moscow. Nine hours, haptic interface, a surgical team he’d briefed over video for three days prior.”

He stood.

“Emeka plays football again. Every Sunday with my father’s current squad. He coaches the younger ones now.”

He replaced the canister precisely where he’d found it.

“I put in for this mission three times before they accepted me. I wanted to be in the same room as the man.”

He left without waiting for a response.
*Three months passed. The habitat rose from the Martian soil. The work began.*


# ACT TWO: THE MISSION



# Chapter 7


## First Light


### Year 1, Month 3 — May 2037

*The cultivation facility was the only place on Mars where Cassian felt like he understood what he was doing.*
*Ten tanks. Slow cyan pulse, threaded with amber. The same rhythm he'd been waking to for three months, the same light he'd learned to read the way you learn a person's breathing — the normal variations, the small deviations that meant something, the baseline that lived behind his eyes now whether he was in here or not. He moved between them the way he moved through his own quarters: without thinking, because he'd stopped needing to.*
*And yet, watching them pulse in the dim facility lighting, he sometimes felt like they were watching back.*
*He pulled up his tablet. "ARIA, current yield?"*
*"87 kilograms annualized. Enough to treat roughly 43,000 patients per year."*
*Better than the five kilograms Ares II had managed. Better than nothing.*
*"Flag anything unusual."*
*"Acknowledged."*
*He moved between the tanks, checking seals and nutrient feeds, running the same diagnostics he'd run a hundred times before. The work was meditative in its repetition — a small island of control in a mission that often felt like controlled chaos.*
*Dr. James Torres entered the facility, tablet in hand, his perpetually distracted expression suggesting he'd been deep in research before remembering he had a physical body that needed to be somewhere.*
*"Tank 7 and Tank 3 are pulsing in synchronization," Torres said without preamble. "Separated by four meters. No direct physical connection."*
*"Is that normal?"*
*"I have no idea." His eyes were bright with it. "We have no baseline for normal. They evolved in isolation for three billion years. But the synchronized tanks are showing slightly higher Areserine output." He made a note. "I'm going to see if I can trigger the behavior deliberately."*
*He left as abruptly as he'd arrived. Cassian turned back to the tanks, watching the slow cyan pulse threaded with its amber counterpoint — the rhythm that had become the backdrop of his days.*
*The facility door hissed open behind him. Thea stepped through, carrying two steaming cups of what passed for coffee on Mars—reconstituted powder mixed with recycled water, bitter and barely palatable.*
*"Breakfast of champions." She handed him a cup.*
*"You're too good to me."*
*"I know." She leaned against the tank nearest to him, watching the organisms pulse. "They're beautiful, aren't they? In a weird, alien sort of way."*
*"They're saving millions of lives. That makes them beautiful enough."*
*Thea sipped her coffee, grimacing at the taste. "I got a message from my sister this morning. She says the public response to our first Areserine shipment was... complicated."*
*"Complicated how?"*
*"Protests. People angry that only 2,500 patients got treatment from the Ares II supply. Demanding to know why PharmaCorp isn't producing more, faster. Some conspiracy theories about the company hoarding doses for the wealthy." She shook her head. "They don't understand how hard this is. They see the numbers—500 million infected, 2,500 treated—and they think we're not trying hard enough."*
*"We're trying as hard as we can."*
*"I know. But they don't see that. They see a red planet on the news and they think we're up here having adventures while their families die."*
*Cassian thought of Emily, waiting on Earth, her Stage 2 GCS held in stasis by the rationed Areserine from Ares II. She understood. She'd always understood.*
*"We just have to keep working. Produce what we can, send it home, and hope it's enough."*
*"Hope." Thea's laugh was bitter. "That's a lot to put on three billion-year-old bacteria."*
*"Archaeons."*
*"Whatever. The point stands." She finished her coffee and set the cup aside. "Commander Chen wants a full crew meeting at 1400. Something about the quarterly review."*
*"Let me guess. PharmaCorp wants more production."*
*"PharmaCorp always wants more production. That's what corporations do." Thea pushed off from the tank. "I'll see you there. Try not to spend all morning talking to your glowing friends."*
*She left. Cassian stayed, watching the organisms pulse their slow blue light, wondering if they had any concept of the weight they carried. Probably not. They were just cells, doing what cells did.*
He wasn’t sure how long he stood there before he noticed it — a brightness that wasn’t quite the baseline cyan he’d catalogued a hundred times, threaded through Tanks 3 and 7 in the same coordinated pattern Torres had flagged two weeks ago. The same tanks. The same synchronization. He checked his tablet: no temperature deviation, no feed anomaly, no change in substrate chemistry. The reading was already returning to baseline by the time he thought to timestamp it. He made a note — T3/T7 coordinated luminescence variance, 0.3% above mean, duration approx. 40 seconds, no causal indicator — and filed it under the growing folder of things the organisms did that he didn’t yet have language for. Torres would probably say it was residual stimulus from the nitrogen flush an hour ago. That was almost certainly what it was.

---

Thomas Wright had caught his palm on an exposed equipment bracket during the ISRU installation — a lateral cut four centimeters long, deep enough that the edges wouldn’t stay closed on their own. He’d wrapped it in a field dressing and found Dr. Osei’s bay empty. Viktor was at the adjacent workstation, reviewing scans on his tablet.

Viktor looked at the hand without being asked. “Let me see.”

He unwrapped the dressing with careful fingers, examined the cut without touching it, then turned Thomas’s hand slightly toward the light. The wound was clean-edged — a bracket cut rather than a tear — and had bled freely, which was good.

“Sit,” Viktor said.

Thomas sat on the examination bench. Viktor assembled his suture kit with movements entirely below conscious thought — the physical arrangement of instruments automatic, his hands finding each item without looking. Needle driver. Forceps. Irrigation syringe. Suture thread, non-absorbable, 4-0. He drew two milliliters of local anesthetic into a syringe and held it up briefly.

“This first.” He held the syringe toward the light. “Small needle. It will sting for a moment and then you won’t feel the rest.”

He administered the anesthetic in three small injections along the wound margins, waiting thirty seconds between each. Thomas watched his own hand with the slightly disconnected attention of someone trying to decide how concerned to be.

“How deep is it?” Thomas asked.

“Four centimeters long, approximately three millimeters at the center. Six sutures, possibly seven.” Viktor irrigated the wound with saline, flushing it clean. “You were fortunate. A centimeter to the right and you’d have caught the tendon sheath.”

He positioned the needle driver and began. The first suture passed through the dermis on one side of the cut, crossed the gap, caught the other side — Viktor drawing the thread through with smooth unhurried tension, tying the knot with four practiced throws of the instrument, cutting the tail to three millimeters. The second suture four millimeters from the first. Then the third.

He worked in silence — not the silence of someone with nothing to say, but the silence of someone for whom speech would have divided attention the work in front of him was owed entirely.

“Breathe.” Without looking up.

Thomas hadn’t realized he’d stopped.

“Sorry.”

“Don’t apologize.” The fourth suture. The fifth. “Look at the wall if it helps.”

Thomas looked at the wall. Viktor placed the sixth suture and examined the closure, turning the hand slightly toward the light. The wound edges were approximated evenly, no tension pulling at the margins. He placed one more.

“Seven.”

He dressed the wound with antibiotic ointment, covered it with a non-adherent pad, secured it with tape in four neat strips.

“Keep it dry for forty-eight hours. Day five I’ll check it. Day ten the sutures come out, assuming normal healing. Redness spreading beyond the margins or increasing pain — come to me immediately.”

“Understood. Thanks, Dr. Kozlov.”

Viktor was already cataloguing the used instruments for sterilization. “Viktor.” He didn’t look up. “We have six years.”

Thomas flexed the hand carefully, feeling the sutures hold beneath the dressing.

“Does it bother you?” A pause. “Working out here. Relative to what you’re used to.”

Viktor considered this.

“The patient is in front of you or they are not.” He set the last waste wrapper down. “When they are, everything else becomes very simple.”

He disposed of the waste materials and left.


---

*The crew meeting confirmed Thea's suspicions.*
*Commander Chen stood at the front of the common area, her face set in the hard lines that had become her default expression over the past year. Behind her, a wall screen displayed production charts, health metrics, and a message from PharmaCorp that no one wanted to read.*
*"Quarterly review," Chen began. "Production is at 87% of projected targets. Health metrics are within acceptable ranges, with some concerning trends I'll address in a moment. And PharmaCorp has sent us new mission parameters."*
*She pulled up the message. The room went silent as they read:*
*ARES III MISSION DIRECTIVE UPDATE*

*Effective immediately, annual Areserine production targets are increased from 85 kg to 120 kg. This adjustment reflects improved understanding of organism cultivation potential and Earth-side demand projections.*

*Crew work schedules should be adjusted accordingly. PharmaCorp reminds all mission personnel that production targets are contractually binding and directly impact post-mission compensation packages.*

*Science for Humanity.*
*PharmaCorp Mission Operations*
*"120 kilograms," Elena said flatly. "That's a 40% increase. We're already working fourteen-hour days."*
*"I'm aware." Chen's voice was controlled, but Cassian could see the tension in her shoulders. "I've drafted a response explaining our current limitations. But I want to be clear with everyone: PharmaCorp is not asking. They're telling."*
*"They can tell all they want from 225 million kilometers away," Dmitri rumbled. "They cannot make organisms grow faster by sending emails."*
*"No, but they can adjust our compensation. They can modify our contracts. They can make our lives very difficult when we return to Earth—assuming we return." Chen met each crew member's eyes in turn. "I'm not saying we have to comply. I'm saying we need to discuss our options."*

---

*The message from Anya had been waiting since morning.*

*Dmitri watched it alone in his quarters, door sealed, volume low. She was nine years old and she'd recorded it sitting on the floor of her bedroom, the bookshelves he'd built behind her. She'd grown. He couldn't stop noticing it — the way she'd grown in increments across the transmissions, a few centimeters between each one, her face losing the last of the roundness it had carried when he left.*

*She talked for six minutes. School, her science project on tidal forces, a book her grandmother had given her that she didn't like but was reading anyway because it seemed important to her grandmother. She talked about the winter. About the snow. About how different snow looked when you were actually inside it compared to looking at it through a window.*

*"I drew a picture of your Mars for school." Near the end. She held up the drawing. Red planet, black sky, a stick figure with a flag. The flag had his name on it. "The teacher said it was accurate. I told her my papa sent me photographs."*

*She held the drawing for a moment, then lowered it.*

*"I love you, Papa. Come home."*

*The recording ended.*

*Dmitri sat on the edge of his bunk for a while afterward. He was not a man who cried — had not been, for most of his adult life — but he was finding that the specific distance between himself and that bookshelf, that bedroom, that nine-year-old growing in increments he could only measure in transmission gaps, was doing something to him he didn't have a precise word for.*

*He would be home in four years. She would be thirteen.*

*He filed the thought in the same place he filed everything that couldn't be solved tonight, and went back to work.*
*The discussion lasted two hours.*
*In the end, they voted 14-9 to increase production efforts. Not because they believed it was possible, but because the alternative—defying PharmaCorp while trapped on Mars with no way home—seemed worse.*
*Cassian voted with the majority. Thea voted against.*
*Afterward, walking back, she took his hand.*
*"This is how it starts."*
*He held her hand tighter and kept walking.*

---

*Later that week, Cassian ran into Dr. Amara Osei outside the medical bay. She looked troubled, scrolling through data on her tablet with a frown that deepened the longer she studied it.*
*"Everything okay?" he asked.*
*Amara hesitated—unusual for someone usually so direct. "Quarterly physicals. Most of the crew is responding to the radiation exposure exactly as we'd expect. Normal adaptation, normal countermeasures."*
*"Most?"*
*"A few crew members are showing... better results. Significantly better. Bone density barely affected, cellular repair happening faster than the models predicted." She shook her head. "It could be individual variation. Some people just handle radiation better than others. But it's strange that it maps so cleanly to specific crew members."*
*"Which ones?"*
*"Marcus, Thomas, Commander Chen. A few others." Amara put away her tablet. "I'm sure it's nothing. Just statistical noise. PharmaCorp's pre-mission genetic screening was supposed to select for radiation tolerance anyway—maybe some of us just got luckier in the genetic lottery."*
*She walked off, leaving Cassian with a vague unease he couldn't quite name. Genetic lottery. Something about the phrase felt wrong, but he couldn't articulate why.*

---


### Year 1, Month 6 — August 2037

*Cassian stood at the viewport, watching ARIA pilot a supply drone through the thin Martian atmosphere. The autonomous craft banked smoothly, adjusting for wind shear, its trajectory flawless.*
*"You're staring." Thea appeared beside him, tablet in hand, always working.*
*"Just thinking."*
*"About?"*
*He hesitated, then shrugged. "I applied to pilot school when I was nineteen. Top scores in navigation, orbital mechanics, spatial reasoning. I could calculate Hohmann transfers in my head."*
*"What happened?"*
*"Medical disqualification. Mild astigmatism, slight depth perception issue. Nothing that mattered for most things, but..." He watched the drone complete its approach. "Not good enough to fly ships. Good enough to grow plants."*
Thea was quiet. "You regret it?"
*"No. Yes. Sometimes." He turned away from the viewport. "I loved flying. The simulators, the math, the feeling of controlling something that moved through three dimensions. But botany... botany is what brought me here. What gave me a way to help Emily. So maybe it worked out."*
*"Maybe it did." Thea's expression was unreadable. "Though I bet you'd make a decent pilot."*

*She paused. “Is that why you say ‘0.58 AU’ instead of ‘three months’?”*

*He was quiet for a moment. “You noticed.”*

*“First week in the observation ring.” She wasn’t teasing. “I just didn’t ask.”*

*“It’s the language I have for it. Pilot math. Nearest I ever got.” He looked at the organisms. “I figured it didn’t matter.”*

*“It matters,” Thea said simply. She said it the way she said most things—like she’d already decided it was true and wasn’t interested in arguing about it. “For what it’s worth.”*

*"Doesn't matter now."*
*He returned to the cultivation facility, to the organisms that needed him, to the work that defined him.*

---

*The message arrived during the evening shift, routed through the standard personal correspondence queue. ARIA flagged it as a video transmission from PharmaCorp Mission Operations — Director level, priority normal.*

*Cassian watched it alone in his quarters, sitting on the edge of his bunk with the tablet propped against the wall.*

*The man on screen was in his mid-fifties, silver at the temples, the kind of face that had spent years learning to appear at ease in front of cameras. He was sitting at a desk — real wood, not laminate — with a window behind him showing an overcast London sky. Blue tie. The PharmaCorp logo on the wall to his left.*

*"My name is Richard Halsey. I'm the Director of Mission Operations — the person responsible for making sure you have what you need to do what you're doing." A slight smile. Genuine, Cassian thought. Or well-practiced enough to be indistinguishable. "I've been meaning to record this for a few months, and I keep finding reasons to put it off. Then I realized that was its own kind of answer. So here I am."*

*He leaned forward slightly.*

*"Six months on the surface. I want you to stop for a moment and let that land. You are the first human beings to establish a sustained presence on another planet, and you've done it while maintaining production yields that our science team honestly didn't believe were achievable in Year One." A pause. "Dr. Vale — the cultivation data has people here genuinely excited. Commander Chen, the operational management has exceeded every benchmark we set."*

*He glanced off-camera briefly — someone in the room, comfortable, not a cue card — before returning.*

*"I know the distance is something I can't fully understand from here. I know what the delay means for personal messages, for the feeling of connection to home. My daughter asked me last week what you do every day up there." The smile again, fuller. "I told her: they save lives. One kilogram at a time. That's what you are. That's what this mission is. And I wanted you to hear that from me directly — not from a memo."*

*He held the camera for a moment.*

*"Keep going. We're with you."*

*The recording ended. Cassian sat with it for a moment, then set the tablet down and went back to work.*

*He didn't know what he'd expected. Something more institutional. The man had seemed — present. Like someone who'd thought about what he wanted to say.*

*He filed it under personal correspondence and didn't think about it again until Sarah Mitchell was dead.*

---



# Chapter 8


## Breach


### Year 2, Month 3 — March 2038

*Sarah Mitchell died on a Tuesday.*
*It was supposed to be a routine EVA—exterior hull inspection, checking for micrometeorite damage accumulated over thirteen months on the Martian surface. Sarah had done dozens of similar inspections. She knew the procedures better than anyone except Marcus, who was partnered with her for the day.*
*The morning was clear by Mars standards, the sky its usual salmon-pink, the sun casting long shadows across the dusty landscape. Sarah and Marcus suited up in the airlock, running through their checklists with the casual efficiency of people who'd done this a hundred times before.*
*"O2 flow nominal," Sarah reported. "Suit pressure holding. Seals look good."*
*"Confirmed on my end," Marcus said. "ARIA, we're ready for exterior access."*
*"Acknowledged. External conditions: temperature -58 degrees Celsius, wind 3 meters per second, dust levels minimal. Recommended EVA duration: four hours maximum."*
*The airlock cycled. The outer door opened. Mars waited.*
*They worked methodically along the habitat's exterior, checking seams, inspecting solar panel mounts, documenting everything with helmet cameras. The work was tedious but necessary—a single undetected breach could mean slow decompression, could mean death while sleeping.*
*Two hours in, Sarah called a break. They sat on a rocky outcrop fifty meters from the habitat, looking out at the landscape that had become their prison and their purpose.*
*"You ever think about what we'll do when we get back?" Sarah asked.*
*Marcus laughed. "Every day. First thing I'm doing is eating a proper meal. Meat that came from an animal instead of a vat. Vegetables that grew in actual soil. Maybe a beer."*
*"Just one?"*
*"To start. Then maybe ten more." He turned to look at her through his visor. "What about you?"*
Sarah was quiet. "I want to see rain. Stand outside in it, feel it on my face. I haven't felt rain in almost two years."
*"That's poetic."*
*"That's desperate." She stood, brushing red dust from her suit. "Come on. Let's finish this section before—"*
*The sound was small. A whisper, really. The kind of sound that shouldn't have been audible through a helmet, through the thin Martian atmosphere, through all the layers of technology designed to keep them alive.*
*But Marcus heard it. And he saw Sarah's face change.*
*"Breach." Her voice was calm—too calm, the calm of someone whose training had kicked in before their fear could catch up. "Left shoulder seal. I've got a breach."*
*Marcus was already moving, crossing the distance between them in two bounding strides. He could see it now—a thin jet of vapor escaping from Sarah's suit near the joint where her arm met her torso. The O-ring had failed. A component that had been inspected, tested, certified by PharmaCorp's quality assurance team, had simply... stopped working.*
*"Patch kit." He was already pulling the emergency supplies from his belt. "Hold still, I'll—"*
*"Marcus." Sarah's voice was still calm, but there was something underneath it now. Something final. "Look at the rate. I've got maybe ninety seconds."*
*He looked. The vapor was streaming now, not wisping. Her suit pressure was dropping fast—faster than his hands could work, faster than any patch could seal.*
*"No." He pressed the patch against the breach anyway, knowing it was hopeless, knowing it wouldn't hold. "No, Sarah, just hold on—"*
*"Tell Chen I'm sorry." Sarah's breathing was becoming labored as the pressure differential began to affect her lungs. "Tell her... the Sistema Abismo thing... it wasn't her fault. None of it was."*
*"Tell her yourself. You're going to be fine. You're going to—"*
*Sarah Mitchell smiled at him through her visor. Her lips were turning blue.*
*"Take care of them, Marcus. They need you."*
*Her eyes went glassy. Her body sagged. The vapor stopped streaming because there was no more atmosphere left to escape.*
*Marcus held her for a long time, kneeling in the red dust of Mars, cradling a dead woman in his arms while the indifferent sun crept across an indifferent sky.*

---

*They buried her that evening.*
*Twenty-two people gathered outside the habitat, suited against the cold, watching as Dmitri and Marcus lowered Sarah's body into the shallow grave they'd dug in the frozen regolith. There was no coffin—just the body bag they'd improvised from thermal blankets, and the stone marker Elena had laser-etched.*
*Sarah Mitchell*
*Life Support Engineer*
*1998–2038*
*Commander Chen spoke the eulogy. Her voice was steady, but Cassian could see her hands trembling inside her gloves.*
*"Sarah Mitchell came to Mars because she believed in this mission. She believed we could save lives, make a difference, prove that humanity was worth saving. She worked harder than anyone I've ever known. She checked her equipment obsessively, ran redundancies on redundancies, did everything right." Chen's voice cracked. "And she still died. Because somewhere on Earth, someone decided that a cheaper O-ring was good enough. Someone looked at a spreadsheet and decided that a few dollars of savings was worth the risk."*
*She paused, gathering herself.*
*"Sarah died because of corporate negligence. Because PharmaCorp cut corners to maximize their margins. And there's nothing we can do about it except document it, report it, and hope that someone on Earth gives a damn." Chen looked at the grave, at the marker, at the red dust already beginning to cover everything. "Sarah, I'm sorry. I'm sorry I couldn't protect you. I'm sorry the system failed you. And I'm sorry that your death will probably change nothing."*
*She stepped back. One by one, crew members came forward to say their own farewells—shared memories, whispered apologies, silent tears that froze on cheeks before they could fall.*
*Cassian stood at the edge of the gathering, Thea beside him, and felt something cold settle into his chest. He'd barely known Sarah—a dozen conversations over two years, a shared tendency to work late. What moved through him wasn't grief so much as recognition. An understanding of what this mission really was.*
*They weren't pioneers. They weren't heroes. They were expendable resources, sent to a hostile world by people who would never face the consequences of their cost-cutting. Sarah's death wasn't a tragedy to PharmaCorp. It was an actuarial adjustment.*
*"She was the first," Thea said quietly. "But she won't be the last."*
*"No." Cassian watched as Dmitri began filling in the grave, each shovel of regolith a soft whisper against the silence. "She won't."*
*That night, he stayed late in the cultivation facility, watching the organisms breathe their dim bioluminescence into the dark. Somewhere in their three-billion-year existence, they had learned to survive in conditions that should have killed them. They had endured where nothing else could endure.*
*He wondered if humans were capable of the same.*
*The tanks seemed brighter tonight—or maybe that was just his imagination, his grief seeking patterns where none existed. Torres had mentioned the synchronized pulsing behavior, the possibility of colonial communication. Was it possible that the organisms sensed the crew's distress? That they were responding to the death of a woman they'd never known?*
*Probably not. They were just cells, after all. Complicated cells with unusual properties, but cells nonetheless. They didn't mourn. They didn't feel loss.*
*And yet.*
*Cassian placed his palm against Tank 7's observation port. The glass was cool, the substrate beyond it glowing with that characteristic layered light — cyan dominant, the amber threading through it, a green speckle flaring briefly near the base and then going dark. The organisms pulsed steadily—0.7 Hz, the same rhythm as a resting human heartbeat.*
*"We lost someone today." His voice was quiet. "Her name was Sarah. She was good at her job, good at taking care of us. And now she's gone, because someone on Earth decided to save a few dollars on an O-ring."*
*The pulse didn't change. The light didn't flicker. The organisms went on doing what they'd done for three billion years, uninterested in the temporary creatures that had come to harvest them.*
*He stayed longer than he needed to, watching Tank 7's pulse.*

*Then he pulled out his personal log — not the mission log, not the cultivation report, but the small encrypted file he'd started in Month Two for observations he didn't want ARIA parsing — and made his first deliberate entry.*

*Year 2, Month 3, Day 4. Post-burial. Tank 7 bioluminescence index: 1.34, against a twenty-four-hour baseline of 1.18. Elevation of 0.16 above baseline. Duration of elevation: approximately fourteen minutes following the introduction of human voice in the facility. Temperature stable. CO2 within standard range. No external stimulus I can identify.*

*Filed in the mission report as: ambient thermal variance, self-correcting. Because that's the closest legitimate category.*

*He stared at the entry for a moment, then added one line:*

*It probably is thermal variance. I'm going to keep watching anyway.*

*He closed the file. He went back to the nitrogen adjustments. The tanks kept pulsing at their slow steady rhythm, three billion years of practice, unbothered by whatever he thought he'd noticed.*

*But he kept watching. That part was true.*

---


### Year 2, Month 5 — May 2038

*Cassian killed the overhead lights. The cultivation room dropped to cyan glow only—the tanks themselves the sole source. Pumps hummed low, almost mammalian. He stood in the aisle between Tank 4 and Tank 7, tablet dark in his hand. No need to look anymore; the numbers lived behind his eyes.*
*ARIA had flagged the variance at 02:41: temperature drift of 0.3°C over four hours, k_s creeping down to 91.7% saturation. He'd walked the line, adjusted the glycol loop by hand, watched the readout settle. Now the projection blinked green again.*
*87.4 kg annualized. Roughly 87,400 patient-years at current dosing.*
*He exhaled through his nose, breath fogging the observation port of Tank 4. The mat inside pulsed once—slow, cyan-dominant, a single orange fleck drifting like a slow ember. Three billion years of waiting, and here it was, responding (or not responding) to a 0.3°C nudge from a botanist who still ran the sims he used to run before the letter arrived.*
*Eighty-seven thousand four hundred.*
*He let the number sit. Not abstract anymore. Not PharmaCorp's line item. Faces. Hospital beds in Portland, in Guadalajara, in Lagos. One of them had Emily's thin smile, the way she used to tilt her head when she teased him about "saving the world with pond scum."*
*He pressed his palm to the cool glass. The mat brightened fractionally under his hand—0.3-second phase lag, same as always. ARIA would log it as electromagnetic artifact. He chose to believe it noticed the warmth.*
*"One more quarter like this," he whispered, "and the backlog clears enough. You won't have to wait anymore, Em."*
*The tanks did not answer. The pulse continued the way it always had, measuring time in units that had nothing to do with human deadlines or light-speed delays.*
*He stayed another minute, counting breaths against the rhythm. Then he straightened, wiped the fog from the port with his sleeve—habit, not necessity—and walked out. The door sealed behind him with the soft hiss of something permanent.*
*In the corridor the habitat lights were dimmed for night cycle. ARIA's voice followed, gentle and precise.*
*"Yield projection stabilized at 87.4 kg annualized. Variance corrected. Good work, Dr. Vale."*
*He did not reply. He walked the line between Tank 4 and Tank 7 one more time, adjusting a flow rate that didn't need adjusting, checking a number he'd already memorized.*

---

*Commander Chen dealt with the administrative aftermath: reports to PharmaCorp, equipment audits, revised safety protocols that everyone knew would make little difference. The O-ring that killed Sarah had been inspected three days before her death. It had passed every test. And it had failed anyway.*
*Thomas had said quietly, almost to himself, that the meal was surprisingly good.*
*Kenji set his bowl in the sanitizer and turned around, leaning back against the counter with his arms folded. "Surprisingly," he said, with the tone of someone examining a word for structural defects. "The ingredients haven't changed. The method hasn't changed. If you're surprised, that's a you problem." He looked at Thomas, then at the bowl. "It's fine. Not surprising. Just fine."*
*Thomas stared at him. Then at Cassian. "How has no one thrown him out an airlock?"*
*"Patience," Cassian said. "Mostly patience."*
*Kenji smiled — not warmly — and walked out.*
*Hassan had developed a habit, in the months since Rachel's death, of appearing in the cultivation facility during the evening shift.*

*He never announced himself. He'd materialize near Tank 3, settle his broad frame onto the equipment step, and watch the organisms pulse. Sometimes he brought tea — two cups, one for Cassian, handed over without comment.*

*"You have grandchildren?" Cassian asked one evening. He'd meant to say nothing.*

*"Four." Hassan's voice was warm in the way of a man for whom this is the easiest subject in the world. "Farouk is seven. The twins — Yasmine and Dina — are five. And the new one, Sami, is eighteen months. He was three months old when I left." He paused. "He won't remember me not being there. That's the thing I keep thinking about. He'll just remember me as someone who was always there. He'll never know there was a gap."*

*"That seems like the right way to remember it."*

*"Yes." A pause. "My wife says he has my nose. I've been trying to see it in the photographs. I think she's being kind."*

*Cassian smiled. "How does she manage? Four kids, six years—"*

*"She managed before I existed," Hassan said simply. "I have always been the one who needed her more than she needed me. She will be fine." He looked at the tanks. "This is a beautiful thing you grow, Dr. Vale. Ugly planet. Beautiful thing."*

*"The organisms grew themselves. I just adjust the nitrogen."*

*"That is what parents say about their children." Hassan drank his tea. "My cardiac review came back this week. Amara says the stress indicators are elevated." He said it the way he said everything — calmly, as a point of information.*

*"Are you worried?"*

*"I'm sixty-one years old and I am on Mars." He set down the cup. "I stopped being worried about the right things a long time ago." He rose, unhurrying. "Good work tonight. The tanks look healthy."*

*He left. Cassian stood alone in the cyan light, thinking about an eighteen-month-old who would never know there had been a gap, and a man who had made peace with that — with all of it — in a way Cassian couldn't quite map onto any framework he had for how people were supposed to work.*

*Three months later, Hassan walked into the oxygen generator housing and didn't come out.*

*Cassian thought about the gap. About Sami, who would never know.*
*Three graves now. Rachel, buried on arrival. Sarah Mitchell, killed by corporate negligence. And Hassan Farooq, who had sacrificed himself so they could all keep breathing.*
*Statistically speaking, the actuarial tables said, you should expect five or six deaths.*
*They were already at three. And they were only two years in.*

---

*The message arrived three days after they buried Sarah.*

*Cassian watched it in the common area, alone at 0600 before anyone else was up. He'd been awake since 0300.*

*Halsey was at the same desk. Same window, same grey London sky. He looked like a man who had prepared what he was going to say and found, on the way to saying it, that preparation wasn't the same as readiness.*

*"I want to begin by acknowledging Sarah Mitchell." A pause — a single, visible swallow before her name. "She was a skilled engineer and by every account a remarkable person, and her loss is a loss to this mission and to everyone who knew her." He held the beat a moment longer than corporate language typically allows. "I'm sorry. I mean that."*

*The recovery was practiced and immediate.*

*"We are conducting a thorough review of the EVA seal specifications. Preliminary analysis points to manufacturing variance in the O-ring batch — a tolerance anomaly that our procurement team is taking up with the equipment contractor. We are committed to accountability."*

*He shifted slightly in his chair — a small recalibration, the condolence paragraph filed and the operational paragraph beginning.*

*"I also want to share some news that I hope provides context for the work you're doing. With your Q1 yields, our distribution team projects treating an additional 8,000 patients this calendar year beyond original estimates." His voice warmed on the numbers in a way it hadn't warmed on Sarah's name — not cynically, Cassian thought. Just naturally. The numbers were where he lived. "Sarah's work. Everyone's work. It's reaching people."*

*He looked into the camera.*

*"We're also transmitting updated production parameters for Year Three. I want to frame these correctly — they reflect our confidence in what you've already demonstrated, not additional pressure. Recognition."*

*The smile came back. It came back too quickly.*

*"Take care of each other. We're watching, and we're proud."*

*The recording ended.*

*Cassian sat in the empty common area for a while, thinking about the swallow before Sarah's name and the warmth in the production figures and the word watching, which had seemed like comfort and now seemed like something else.*

*He went to the cultivation facility. He adjusted a nitrogen feed that didn't need adjusting and stayed longer than he needed to.*

---

*EARTH — PHARMCORP HEADQUARTERS, LONDON*
*MISSION REVIEW: ARES III — Q1 2038*

*The conference room had a view of the Thames. Nobody was looking at it.*

*Seven people around a polished table, tablets open, coffee going cold. The wall display showed a spreadsheet: production yields, health metrics, mission costs broken against humanitarian output. The numbers were good. In several columns, better than projected.*

*Martin Reyes, VP of Mission Finance, advanced the slide.*

*"Genetic modification tier performance." The new slide showed a color-coded matrix. Twelve names in green. Six in amber. The amber names had variance flags on the bone density and cellular repair columns. "Basic tier is showing accelerated deterioration relative to modeling. Within acceptable parameters for mission completion, but the health trajectory is steeper than the Year Two benchmarks suggested."*

*Across the table, Dr. Sarah Huang, Deputy Director of Crew Resources, looked at the amber names. She had met all twenty-four crew members. She had shaken their hands at the pre-launch briefing. She looked at the amber names and then at her tablet.*

*"Define acceptable parameters."*

*"Basic tier crew are projected to complete their mission-essential functions through Year Six with high confidence. Post-mission health outcomes are outside our liability window." Reyes advanced the slide. A cost comparison: full Enhanced modifications for all twenty-four crew versus the tiered approach. The differential was $847 million. "The tiered protocol achieved the mission objective within budget."*

*Huang looked at the six amber names. One of them was someone who had told her, at the pre-launch briefing, about his grandchildren. She couldn't remember which name.*

*"There's a follow-up question on legal exposure," said someone from the compliance team, already moving on. "If differential health outcomes become public post-mission, what's our disclosure position?"*

*"Standard medical confidentiality provisions in the employment contracts," legal replied. "Crew members consented to proprietary modification protocols as a condition of mission participation. Full disclosure falls under trade secret protection."*

*"And if a crew member discloses independently?"*

*"The NDA provisions cover post-mission disclosure. There are financial penalties."*

*Huang was still looking at the amber names.*

*"Dr. Huang." Reyes's voice was neutral, professional. "Is there a concern you want to raise?"*

*She looked up. Seven faces, waiting. Reasonable people. People who had families and mortgages and performance reviews and a spreadsheet on the wall that had already answered every question she was about to ask before she asked it.*

*"No." A pause. "No concern."*

*Reyes advanced to the next slide. The meeting continued for forty-five minutes. At the end, Huang approved the minutes, and went back to her office, and did not think about the six amber names until three years later when one of them had a name she recognized attached to a death report.*

*By then she had been promoted.*

---



# Chapter 9


## Pressure


### Year 2, Month 11 — November 2038

*The second quota increase came without warning.*
*Cassian was in the middle of a nutrient adjustment when ARIA's voice cut through the facility's ambient hum: "Incoming priority transmission from PharmaCorp. All senior personnel to the command center immediately."*
*He exchanged a look with Dr. Torres, who was analyzing organism samples at the adjacent workstation. Neither of them spoke. They both knew what priority transmissions meant.*
*The command center was crowded by the time Cassian arrived—twenty-one people crammed into a space designed for six, all of them staring at the message displayed on the main screen:*
*ARES III MISSION DIRECTIVE UPDATE*

*Following analysis of current production rates and Earth-side demand projections, PharmaCorp is implementing the following adjustments:*

*1. Annual Areserine production target increased from 120 kg to 150 kg.*
*2. Geological survey activities (Mission Addendum 7-B) to be accelerated. Phase 2 surveys must be completed by Month 30.*
*3. Non-essential crew recreation time reduced to optimize mission efficiency.*

*These adjustments reflect PharmaCorp's commitment to maximizing humanitarian impact. All personnel are reminded that mission performance directly affects individual compensation packages and future employment opportunities.*

*Science for Humanity.*
*PharmaCorp Mission Operations*
*The silence that followed was different from the last time. The first quota increase had produced argument, anger, Elena's voice carrying through the common area for an hour. This one produced quiet.*
*"150 kilograms," Elena said. Flat. Not dangerous — just flat, the way a surface is flat when everything has been worn off it.*
*"I know," Chen said.*
*"The organisms can't produce faster." Torres didn't look up from his tablet. "It's not possible."*
*"I know that too."*
*Kenji was leaning in the doorway, arms folded. "The contract language is unambiguous. Section 14 has always read this way."*
*"We know what it says," Marcus said.*
*"I'm saying it's not a surprise. If it's a surprise to you—"*
*"It's not a surprise." Chen's voice was very quiet. "None of this is a surprise anymore."*
*The vote was fourteen in favor of compliance, seven against, one abstention. Narrower than before. Same result. People filed out without talking.*
*Cassian voted to comply. So did Thea, though she wouldn't meet his eyes afterward.*
*"I hate who we're becoming." Said as they walked back to their quarters.*
*He didn't have an answer. ARIA logged their heart rates during the walk back: both elevated, neither flagged as requiring intervention.*

---

*Thomas Wright was sitting in the inner airlock vestibule when Cassian found him.*

*Not in a suit — just inside the sealed inner door, the viewport showing the Martian night. Not against regulations, exactly. Just unusual.*

*"Thomas."*

*"I'm fine." He didn't turn. "Just needed a minute that wasn't recycled air."*

*Cassian stood beside him. Through the viewport, the Martian stars were motionless and thick — no atmosphere to make them shimmer. They looked artificial. A backdrop.*

*"My uncle used to say the worst thing about Ares II wasn't the dying," Thomas said. "It was how PharmaCorp talked about it afterward. Operational parameters. Acceptable variance. Like they'd already decided what his death was worth before he left."*

*Cassian said nothing.*

*"I joined this mission because I wanted it to be different. I wanted to be part of the one that worked. The one that mattered." He turned from the viewport. In the vestibule light his face was young in a way Cassian forgot sometimes — twenty-eight, trying hard to hold something together. "I voted to comply tonight. Did you see that?"*

*"I saw."*

*"My uncle would have voted no." Thomas looked back at the stars. "He always voted no. It's why they didn't renew his contract for Ares III. Too much trouble. Too many questions." A pause. "He died on Ares II because they sent him anyway on a different crew, and the CO2 scrubbers failed, and PharmaCorp called it an acceptable variance." He said the last two words with a precision that had been practiced until it no longer sounded like anger — just recitation. "I keep wondering when I'm going to get to stop making his choices instead of mine."*

*Cassian didn't have an answer for that. He stayed anyway, standing in the sealed vestibule with the Martian night on the other side of the glass, until Thomas was ready to go back inside.*

*They walked back in silence. At the junction, Thomas stopped.*

*"Thanks. For just — standing there."*

*"Anytime."*

*Thomas nodded and went to bed. Cassian stood at the junction a moment longer, thinking about Michael Wright and about the specific weight of inheriting someone else's unfinished argument with the world.*
*He'd been mapping the synchronization pattern for three weeks before he trusted what he was seeing.*

*It had started as a notation — the Tank 3 to Tank 7 coordination Torres had documented in Year One, confirmed, unremarkable on its face. What caught Cassian was the timing. Not that the tanks synchronized. The sequence in which they synchronized.*

*Tank 3 always initiated. The pulse moved to Tank 7, then Tank 4, then Tank 1, always in the same order, across ten tanks, with a 0.3-second lag between each adjacent pair. Total propagation time across the facility: approximately three seconds.*

*He'd mapped it across twenty-three events without a single deviation in the propagation sequence.*

*He sat in the cultivation facility at 0300 with his personal log open, writing the entry he wasn't going to file with ARIA.*

*Random would show variable initiation points and irregular lag times. What he was seeing was a fixed sequence that repeated across twenty-three documented events with no variation.*

*Possible explanation one: a physical substrate connection he couldn't see — a mycelial-type network running beneath the tank bases. He'd checked the substrate reports three times. Nothing.*

*Possible explanation two: chemical signaling through the shared atmospheric environment. Possible, but the 0.3-second regularity was tighter than chemical diffusion should allow at this distance.*

*Possible explanation three: something he didn't have a framework for.*

*He closed the log and went back to the standard production report — yield figures, Areserine accumulation rates, substrate levels, everything normal, everything on track. The report PharmaCorp would receive in the morning.*

*He filed the observation under the other thing. The one he was keeping to himself.*

---



# Chapter 10


## The Healer


### Year 3, Month 7 — July 2039

*Mei Tanaka's door was locked when Cassian came to check on her.*
*She'd missed breakfast. She'd missed the morning briefing. She'd missed three scheduled sessions with crew members who needed her guidance more than ever. In a ship full of people hanging by threads, the psychologist's absence was a wound that everyone felt but no one wanted to examine too closely.*
*"Mei?" Cassian knocked again. "Are you okay? ARIA said your vitals looked off this morning."*
*Silence.*
*"I'm going to override the lock if you don't answer."*
*Still nothing. Cassian's heart began to race. He thought of Rachel Park—the peaceful face, the empty syringe, the weeks of hidden desperation that no one had seen.*
*"ARIA, emergency override. Mei Tanaka's quarters."*
*"Override requires confirmation from a senior officer or medical personnel."*
*"Then get Dr. Osei. Now."*
*Amara arrived within minutes, her face tight with concern. She didn't ask questions—just entered her override code and pushed through the door as soon as it unsealed.*
*Mei was on her bunk, eyes open, breathing shallow. Empty pill bottles littered the floor around her—sedatives, mostly, plus the antidepressants she'd been secretly stockpiling for months. Her tablet was propped against the wall, displaying a message she'd written but never sent:*
*I can't do this anymore. Three more years. I can't.*

*I'm sorry. I tried. Tell my parents I tried.*

*Tell the crew I'm sorry I couldn't help them. I couldn't even help myself.*
*"She's still breathing," Amara said, already checking vitals. "Barely. Help me get her to medical."*
*They carried Mei through the habitat's narrow corridors, drawing stares and whispered questions from crew members who didn't yet understand what was happening. By the time they reached the medical bay, word had spread. By the time Amara stabilized her—barely, just barely, with everything they had left in their depleted pharmacy—the entire crew knew.*
*Their psychologist had tried to kill herself.*
*The woman responsible for keeping them sane had broken first.*

---

*Mei survived, technically. But the woman who woke up three days later wasn't the same person who'd greeted them at launch, smiling and capable and ready to guide them through the psychological challenges of deep space.*
*The change was visible in small ways: the tremor in her hands that hadn't been there before, the way her eyes darted to corners of rooms as if expecting something to be lurking there, the long silences that stretched between questions and answers. She was present, but not entirely present—a ghost of the psychologist they'd known, going through motions that no longer connected to meaning.*
*"I was on medication," she told Chen and Amara, her voice flat and empty. "Before the mission. Sertraline. For depression. PharmaCorp cleared me anyway because you needed a psychologist and I was the best candidate willing to go."*
*"You didn't disclose this in your medical history," Amara said.*
*"I brought my own supply. Off the books. I thought... I thought I could manage it. I thought the work would help." Mei laughed, a hollow sound that made Cassian's chest ache. "Turns out, being responsible for everyone else's mental health while your own is falling apart isn't the therapeutic experience I hoped for."*
*"Why didn't you tell someone?" Chen asked.*
*"Who would I tell? I'm the psychologist. I'm supposed to have the answers. If I admitted I was struggling, how could anyone trust me to help them?" Mei's eyes finally met Chen's. "You all look at me like I'm supposed to be stronger than you. Like I'm supposed to have special insight into the human mind that makes me immune to its failures. But I'm just a person, Commander. A broken person trying to fix other broken people while pretending I'm not one of them."*
*Chen didn't respond. What was there to say?*
*The conversation that followed was technical—medication adjustments, restricted duty assignments, monitoring protocols.*
*Mei was placed on restricted duty—no more one-on-one sessions, no more crisis interventions, no more being the person everyone turned to when they felt themselves slipping. Dr. Osei took over the psychological support role, adding it to her already overwhelming medical responsibilities.*
*"I don't know what I'm doing," Amara confided to Cassian later, in the relative privacy of the medical bay. "I'm a physician, not a psychiatrist. I can treat patients and manage treatment protocols. But this?" She gestured at the air. "How do I fix something I can't see?"*
*"You do your best," Cassian said. "Like the rest of us."*
*She looked at him for a moment. Then she went back to work.*
*"We're three years in," Thea said that night, lying beside Cassian in his narrow bunk. They'd started sharing quarters a year ago—not officially, but Commander Chen turned a blind eye to crew relationships as long as work got done. "Three more to go."*
*"We'll make it."*
*"Will we? Mei was the strongest of us, Cassian. She was trained for this. If she broke..."*
*"Then we'll be stronger. We'll watch out for each other better. We'll—"*
*"We'll what? Work harder? Sleep less? Push ourselves until we end up like her—or worse, like Rachel?" Thea's voice cracked. "I'm scared. I'm scared all the time. I calculate our survival probability every night, and the numbers keep getting worse, and I can't stop running them because that's what I do. That's all I know how to do."*
*Cassian held her closer. "What do the numbers say?"*
*"Right now? 62% chance that all remaining crew members survive to mission end. Down from 77% at launch." She pressed her face against his shoulder. "That's eight or nine people, Cassian. Eight or nine of us who won't make it home."*
*"Statistics aren't destiny."*
*"You keep saying that."*
*"Because I need to believe it."*
*They lay in silence, listening to the habitat's ambient sounds—the hum of life support, the distant clang of machinery, the whisper of recycled air that kept them breathing in a world that offered no breath of its own.*
*"I love you," Thea said suddenly.*
*Cassian's heart stumbled. They'd never said it before. Had never put the word to what they had.*
*"I love you too."*
*"Good." She pressed closer to him. "Because if we're going to die out here, I want to have said it at least once."*
*He held her until she fell asleep, and then he lay awake, staring at the ceiling, thinking about probabilities and the three years still stretching ahead of them.*
*Sixty-two percent.*
He was in the cultivation facility by 0530, an hour before his shift technically began. He’d been coming in early for months now — not because the work required it, but because the facility was the one place on the station where the silence felt intentional rather than imposed.
The tanks were running at standard output. He checked the feeds, the substrate levels, the Areserine accumulation figures. Everything nominal. He was logging the overnight production numbers when he noticed Tank 7’s bioluminescence index — 4.1% above baseline, the highest it had registered outside of a maintenance cycle. Tank 3 matched it almost exactly. The same pairing Torres had flagged eighteen months ago.
He stood looking at the numbers. Last night he’d said I love you to another person for the first time in three years and meant it completely, without calculation, without the reservation that had become his default mode out here. He didn’t know if that was relevant. He was reasonably certain it wasn’t.
He filed the reading under T3/T7 coordinated variance — recurring and asked ARIA to cross-reference against habitat thermal logs.
“Ambient temperature in cultivation facility remained within standard parameters overnight,” ARIA reported. “No mechanical or environmental cause identified for bioluminescence variance. Logging as statistical fluctuation pending further data.”
“Pending further data,” Cassian agreed.
He stayed another twenty minutes, watching the tanks return to baseline, before going to breakfast.

---

*Two days later, in the galley at the tail end of breakfast, Lian Wei pushed her bowl six centimeters across the table toward Cassian without looking up from her tablet.*
*"Still too much spirulina. The ratio is wrong." She turned a page. "It's been wrong for three years."*
*Cassian looked at the bowl, then at her. "I'll adjust it."*
*"I know you will." She turned another page. "You always do. It's still wrong after."*
*Hayashi, across the table, made a sound that was almost a laugh. He was reassembling a sensor housing with the methodical focus he brought to everything — tools laid out in order, each piece set down in exactly the place it would be needed next. He didn't look up either.*
*"The flour's fine. It's the honey ratio."*
*"It's not the honey ratio," Lian said.*
*"It is."*
*They didn't argue. They just held their respective positions in the comfortable way of people who had been disagreeing about the same thing long enough that the disagreement itself had become a kind of warmth.*
Amara found him in the cultivation facility four days after Mei’s overdose, during the window between end-of-shift and dinner when the habitat’s corridors went briefly quiet.
She didn’t say anything at first. Just stood beside him in front of Tank 4, watching the organisms pulse. Cassian had learned not to push her — she came to things at her own pace, and pushing only sent her back behind the clinical mask she wore for everyone else.
“I’ve been coming in here.” Finally. “At night, sometimes. When I can’t sleep.”
“I know. I’ve seen the access logs.”
“Does that bother you?”
“No.”
She was quiet again. The tanks ran their slow rhythm, cyan deepening toward something almost violet at the outer edges of the colony clusters.
“The night Mei took the pills,” Amara said. “I was in here. Before we knew. I couldn’t sleep and I came in here and just — stood. The way you do.” She paused. “The tanks were different that night. Brighter. Not all of them — just the cluster in the middle. Tanks 3, 4, 7. Pulsing together.”
Cassian looked at her. “How much brighter?”
“I don’t know. I’m not you, I don’t have baseline numbers in my head. Brighter than they look now.” She folded her arms across her chest, the gesture she used when she was being careful with herself. “I’m not saying it means anything. I know it doesn’t mean anything.”
*He stayed after Amara left.*

*He pulled up the bioluminescence log for the past week and ran it against the habitat event log — the private timeline he'd been building, noting crew incidents against bioluminescence readings. He'd done this before. He'd gotten used to the process: find an event, check the timestamp, check the tank readings for the surrounding window.*

*He found what he was looking for in eleven minutes.*

*Year Three, Month Seven, Day Six. Mei's overdose. ARIA's medical alert: 23:14.*

*He scrolled the bioluminescence log.*

*The tanks had been at baseline — 1.18 — through 22:49. At 22:51 the index began climbing. Peak of 1.39 at 23:08. Sustained through 23:31. Return to baseline by 23:54.*

*The overdose occurred at 23:14.*

*The elevation had begun twenty-three minutes before ARIA's alert.*

*He sat with that for a long time. He checked the temperature logs, the CO2 readings, the substrate report. Nothing. He checked for electromagnetic anomalies. Nothing. He checked whether any crew member had entered the cultivation facility during the elevated window.*

*No entries logged between 21:00 and 06:00.*

*Three rooms and a pressure seal between the cultivation facility and Mei's quarters. No physical contact. No chemical pathway he could trace. No mechanism he could identify.*

*He thought about what Amara had said. She'd been in here before the overdose. The tanks were brighter. She was right. She'd been standing in this room while it was already happening, three rooms away, and she'd felt it without knowing what she was feeling.*

*He made the entry in his private log. He wrote the numbers carefully and precisely, the way you write things you can't explain — because the numbers at least are real, even when nothing else is.*

*Then he went back to work, and he did not file it, and he did not tell anyone, and the tanks pulsed on behind him in the dark.*
“You could file an observation report.”
“I could.” She looked at Tank 4’s slow pulse. “It was probably the overnight thermal cycle. Or something I ate. Or three years of not enough sleep catching up with me.” She turned toward the door. “I just thought you should know.”
She left. Cassian turned back to the tanks.
The cluster she’d named — 3, 4, 7 — was running at standard output. He checked the bioluminescence index one more time — standard output, all tanks — then switched off the facility lights and followed her out.

---

*The communications bay was supposed to be empty at 0200.*

*Cassian had come for a quiet place to think — the cultivation facility too warm since the heating recalibration, his quarters too small — and found Kenji Sato instead. Sitting at the primary terminal with the transmission log open and both hands flat on the desk.*

*Kenji didn't acknowledge him when Cassian entered.*

*On the screen: an outbound message queue. Personal correspondence. Twenty-three messages addressed to J. Sato, Kyoto. Spanning eighteen months. Status: Delivered. Response count: zero.*

*Cassian said nothing. He sat at the secondary terminal and did not look at the screen.*

*After a long time, Kenji said: "She hasn't answered any of them."*

*"Your daughter?"*

*"My wife." A pause. "Ex-wife. We were finalizing the paperwork when the mission launched. The agreement was that she'd still — that we'd maintain some—" He stopped. "It doesn't matter what the agreement was."*

*Cassian waited.*

*"I keep sending them anyway," Kenji said. "I'm aware of how that reads. I have a reasonable understanding of how I appear to other people." He looked at the screen. "I just don't seem to be able to stop."*

*"That doesn't read badly."*

*"It reads desperate." Kenji closed the log. The screen went dark. "I'd rather you didn't mention this."*

*"I won't."*

*Kenji stood. His face had already returned to its usual arrangement — closed, slightly contemptuous, as though the previous three minutes had been a weather event he'd waited out.*

*"I'm going to make an observation, Dr. Vale. You are the only person on this crew I don't actively find exhausting." He said it as flatly as he said everything else — the caloric counts, the contract law quotations, the weather reports no one had asked for. "I'm not sure what to do with that information. But I thought you should have it."*

*He left.*

*Cassian sat in the empty communications bay for a long time, in front of a screen full of sent messages with no replies, thinking about the particular kind of courage it takes to keep doing something that isn't working, and the particular kind of loneliness that looks, from the outside, exactly like contempt.*

---



# Chapter 11


## The Depth


### Year 3, Month 9 — September 2039

*The anomaly report came through at 0219 hours, routed to Dmitri's personal terminal with a priority flag that meant the autonomous system had already halted operations and was waiting on a human decision.*
*He was dressed and walking before ARIA finished repeating the alert.*
*The equipment bay was cold at that hour—PharmaCorp's heating efficiency protocols, shaving kilowatts off the overnight draw. His breath misted in the dim service lighting. Mining Unit 2 sat in its cradle with its status indicators amber, drill column retracted, exactly as the autonomous failsafe required. The machine had done what it was designed to do. Something had changed at depth, and it had stopped.*
*Dmitri pulled the bore log.*

Stratum 1,   0–118m:   Regolith composite. Resistance nominal.
Stratum 2, 118–433m:   Consolidated basalt. Resistance nominal.
Stratum 3, 433–809m:   Fractured basalt, ice-filled fissures. Resistance elevated.
Stratum 4, 809–851m:   Resistance collapsed to near-zero. Drill free-rotating. Auto-halt triggered.

*He read it twice. Then he pulled up the sonar attachment—the secondary imaging package that rode the drill column and mapped surrounding geology as it descended. Low resolution at this depth, roughly three meters per pixel. But the image was unambiguous.*
*At 851 meters, the drill had punched through the roof of a void.*
*Not a crack. Not a fissure. A void forty-three meters in diameter, running northwest to southeast for as far as the sonar could resolve—at least 600 meters, probably more. The walls were smooth in the way that only meant one thing to anyone who'd spent time underground: ancient lava flow, cooled over millennia, polished by billions of years of thermal cycling. A lava tube. A large one.*
*Dmitri leaned back in the operator's chair and looked at the coordinate overlay.*

*Zone 7-C, Survey Priority Alpha per Mission Addendum 7-B. Three kilometers northeast of the habitat. On the surface, the spectrographic returns were exceptional—neodymium, dysprosium, lanthanum, all the elements PharmaCorp had flagged before launch. He'd been drilling here six weeks. This was the deepest bore of the mission.*
*He let the sonar complete its full scan cycle—twelve minutes—and watched the image build.*
*The void floor wasn't bare rock.*
*The return texture was wrong for basalt. Wrong for ice. Wrong for anything geological. It had the diffuse, layered signature that he'd seen in the organism cultivation data—irregular surfaces distributed in overlapping mats, the way biological colonies built up over time. Dense. Extensive. Whatever was down there had been growing for a very long time.*
*He sat with that for a while.*
*Dmitri Volkov had spent twenty years underground—Siberian nickel operations, Kazakh copper mines, two seasons at a South African platinum facility that had nearly killed him twice. He understood geology the way other men understood faces. He understood what you did when your bore intersected an unexpected void: you stopped, you mapped, you redesigned around it. You didn't keep drilling into empty space without understanding what you'd found.*
*Marcus had mentioned at the morning briefing that peak storm season fell around month eight — something to factor into drilling schedules.*
*Kenji had stopped in the equipment bay doorway on his way past. Turned around slowly. "Month seven. Southern solstice is six point five, peak lag is three to four weeks. Month eight is outside the window." He held Marcus's gaze for a beat, with the patience of someone explaining something to someone slow. "Month. Seven." He turned and kept walking. "Engineering." Already gone.*
*Marcus stared after him. Put down his tool. Picked it up.*
*He understood something else, too. Zone 7-C's surface REE deposit sat in the basalt cap directly above this tube. To extract at the scale Addendum 7-B specified, you couldn't leave the void intact. The extraction footprint would require either structural reinforcement—expensive, complex, probably impossible at current crew capacity—or controlled collapse. There was no third option.*
*The organism colony below the tube—if it connected to the main network—would not survive a controlled collapse.*
*He filed the anomaly report with ARIA, attached the sonar data, flagged it for Commander Chen's morning review. Protocol required disclosure. He documented everything.*
*What he didn't write down was the thing that kept him sitting in the cold equipment bay until 0415: that PharmaCorp had chosen Zone 7-C as Priority Alpha before they launched. The orbital spectrography that predicted the surface REE deposits would have shown this tube system. Ground-penetrating radar at the resolution they'd used in mission planning would have seen the void. They had mapped this from 225 million kilometers away, and they had still labeled it Priority Alpha.*
*Which meant they'd known.*
*Not guessed. Known.*
*He shut down the terminal at 0415. The bore log was still open on the secondary display when he left. He didn't close it.*
*He was still in the equipment bay at 0600 when Marcus arrived for the morning shift.*

*"You've been here all night."*

*"The ISRU secondary valve is giving variance readings." Dmitri didn't look up from the console. "I wanted to watch it over a full cycle."*

*Marcus looked at the bore log still open on the secondary display. He looked at Dmitri. He decided, with the particular intelligence of a man who knew when not to ask questions, not to ask them.*

*"Coffee's on." He left it there.*

*Dmitri closed the bore log.*

*He'd been thinking, through the hours of watching valve readings, about a parent-teacher meeting before launch — the last one, Anya sitting beside him with her homework folder, the teacher saying she was exceptional at spatial reasoning, that she had a gift for understanding how systems fit together.*

*"She gets it from you," the teacher had said. Clearly a compliment.*

*He had not told the teacher that he'd spent twenty years underground — Siberian nickel operations, Kazakh copper mines, two seasons in a Chilean lithium operation that had taken three of his colleagues and given back nothing he hadn't already known about human cost. That his gift for spatial reasoning had been developed in the dark, reading rock strata and void signatures, understanding the geometry of things that could kill you if you read them wrong.*

*He had smiled and said thank you.*

*Now he sat in an equipment bay on Mars with a bore log that meant something he couldn't unknow, thinking about a ten-year-old with a gift for understanding systems and a father who had spent his life inside the ones that no one talked about. Wondering what she would understand, when she was old enough, about where the money came from.*

*He stood up. He got coffee. He went back to work.*

*The bore log stayed closed.*

---

*Commander Chen read the report at 0630, standing at her desk with coffee cooling in her hand.*
*She called Elena.*
*"Zone 7-C bore data."*
*"I saw it." Elena's voice was already tight. "The void signature is consistent with a major lava tube. The biological return on the sonar—"*
*"Could be mineral texture artifact."*
*"Could be." A pause. "Isn't."*
*Chen set down the coffee. "Pull Zone 7-C from the drilling schedule. Flag everything for assessment."*
*"PharmaCorp will push back."*
*"Yes." She ended the call.*
*The pushback arrived in eleven minutes—four seconds faster than the current light-speed delay technically allowed, which meant someone at PharmaCorp Mission Control had been monitoring incoming crew transmissions in the middle of the night.*
*Suspension noted. Geological analysis to be conducted concurrent with active operations per Mission Addendum 7-B parameters. Time-sensitive survey window requires continuous progress. Resume operations at earliest opportunity.*
*She filed it in the encrypted partition ARIA couldn't access, suspended Zone 7-C, and drafted an incident report documenting both the instruction and her decision to override it on safety grounds.*
*The second message came an hour later. The language was the same—compliance requirements, contract section references, performance evaluation implications—but the tone had sharpened. She filed that one too.*
*Three weeks later, when the water recycling crisis consumed the habitat's full attention, the drilling schedule collapsed anyway. Zone 7-C sat dormant for six weeks without anyone from PharmaCorp raising it again.*
*The fourth message, when it finally came, was different. No compliance language. No performance metrics. It was routed through the administrative channel and addressed to the mission record rather than to Chen directly.*
*Zone 7-C survey data received and reviewed by PharmaCorp geological team. Site reclassified as secondary priority. Preliminary extraction assessment transferred to Ares IV mission design package. No further action required from Ares III crew.*
*Elena was standing behind Chen when she read it.*
Neither of them spoke.
*"They're already planning a fourth mission," Elena said. Her voice was flat.*
*"Yes."*
*"Before we've finished Year 3."*
*"Yes."*
*Elena looked out the viewport at the rust-colored plain stretching northeast, toward the invisible bore hole with its sealed collar and its 851 meters of excavated Martian rock. "The orbital data they had before launch. The ground-penetrating radar from Ares I. They would have seen the tube system."*
*Chen didn't answer.*
*"They knew what was down there," Elena said. "They labeled it Priority Alpha anyway." She turned from the viewport. "They sent us here to prove it was viable. To do the survey work that makes Ares IV worth funding."*
*Still Chen didn't answer. There was nothing to add that Elena hadn't already said.*

---

*He spent two weeks in Year Three, Month Nine trying to prove himself wrong.*

*The controlled conditions required the facility empty, which meant the small hours, which meant working between the 0200 and 0600 maintenance checks. He told himself it was yield optimization work. ARIA logged it as yield optimization work. In the official records, that's what it was.*

*The first night: he sealed the facility, confirmed no human presence, held temperature and CO2 and substrate composition and light levels at standard parameters. Then he sat outside in the corridor for four hours and watched the bioluminescence readings on his tablet.*

*The tanks held within 0.04 of baseline across all ten. No synchronization events. Pulse patterns normal and unremarkable.*

*He re-entered the facility.*

*Within ninety minutes, Tank 3 began its familiar initiation sequence. The 0.3-second propagation rippled outward across the other nine tanks in the established order. Bioluminescence climbed 0.18 above baseline.*

*He stood very still and watched it happen.*

*He ran it again the following night. And the night after. Twelve nights in total, eliminating variables one by one — temperature artifacts, electromagnetic interference from the ISRU plant, substrate batch variation. Each time the tanks quieted without him. Each time they responded when he came back.*

*On the ninth night, he couldn't sleep before his session and arrived at the facility in a state he would have characterized, if he were filing anything officially, as elevated distress. He'd been thinking about Emily. About the message that wouldn't arrive for another two years, assuming she was still alive to send it.*

*The tanks that night exceeded 0.25 above baseline. His highest recorded reading.*

*He sat on the equipment step for a long time afterward, not taking notes, just sitting.*

*What's left when you eliminate the alternatives is the thing you don't have a framework for.*

*He was not going to file this. If he filed this, PharmaCorp received it, and PharmaCorp either ignored it or shut it down or did something with it that he didn't want them doing. This organism had been in this rock for three billion years. Whatever it was, whatever it was doing — it was not his to hand over.*

*He locked the file and went to bed.*
*Three kilometers northeast, in a sealed bore hole with amber status indicators and a drill column held in retract, 851 meters of Martian rock waited. And somewhere below, in a lava tube that had been dark and uninterrupted for three billion years, something ancient and bioluminescent pulsed on, unaware of what had briefly, violently touched the ceiling above it.*


# Chapter 12


## The Hero


### Year 3, Month 11 — November 2039

*The oxygen generator started failing on a Thursday.*
*ARIA's alert came at 0347 hours, pulling the crew from sleep with the tone that everyone had learned to dread: "Warning. Primary oxygen generation at 67% capacity and declining. Recommend immediate diagnostic inspection."*
*By 0400, Marcus had the system open and was staring at the damaged component with an expression that Cassian had never seen on the engineer's face before.*
*Fear.*
*"The catalytic converter is degrading," Marcus reported to the assembled senior crew. "Thermal stress, probably from the dust storm last month. We're losing oxygen production at roughly 2% per hour. At current rate, we'll be below minimum sustainable output within eighteen hours."*
*"Backup systems?" Chen asked.*
*"The secondary generator was cannibalized for parts six months ago. PharmaCorp decided we didn't need full redundancy." Marcus's voice was bitter. "We have emergency oxygen reserves—maybe twelve hours' worth if we ration carefully. But if we can't fix the primary generator, we're dead."*
*The room absorbed this in silence.*
*"What do you need?" Chen asked.*
*"Time, tools, and a miracle." Marcus pulled up schematics on the wall display. "The converter housing needs to be replaced entirely. I can fabricate a new one from materials we have on hand, but it'll take at least sixteen hours. And I can't do that while the system is running—the temperatures would kill me."*
*"So we shut down the primary, switch to reserves, and you work as fast as you can."*
*"That's the plan. But there's a problem." Marcus zoomed in on a specific component. "The thermal damage has weakened the housing around the main reaction chamber. If we shut down improperly—if the cooling sequence isn't perfect—the whole thing could rupture. We'd lose the generator entirely, and probably whoever's standing nearby."*
*"So we need to keep it running while you work."*
*"Yes. But that means someone has to be inside the housing, managing the coolant flow manually, while I fabricate the replacement. The temperatures in there are—" Marcus stopped, swallowed. "The temperatures are survivable. Barely. In a suit. For maybe forty minutes before heat stroke becomes inevitable."*
*"I'll do it."*
*Everyone turned. Hassan Farooq stepped forward, his weathered face calm beneath the fluorescent lights. The atmospheric scientist was fifty-three years old, the oldest member of the crew—an Indian-British engineer from Manchester with bad knees and a heart condition that Amara had been monitoring since Year 1.*
*"Hassan, no." Amara's voice was sharp. "Your cardiac profile—"*
*"My cardiac profile means I'm the most expendable person in this room." Hassan met her eyes. "I'm an atmospheric scientist. My primary mission objectives were completed in Year 1. Everything since then has been data collection that any of you could do."*
*"That's not—"*
*"It's exactly true, and everyone knows it." Hassan turned to Chen. "Commander, Marcus needs someone in that housing. Someone who understands thermal dynamics, who can read the instruments and make adjustments in real time. That's me. My work is done. Let me be useful one more time."*
*Chen's face was unreadable. "There has to be another way."*
