*"There isn't. You know there isn't." Hassan smiled—a gentle, accepting smile that made something twist in Cassian's chest. "I came to Mars to help save humanity. Let me do that."*
*The silence stretched.*
*"ARIA," Chen said finally. "What's the probability of successful repair with Dr. Farooq in the housing?"*
*"With Dr. Farooq managing coolant flow manually, probability of successful fabrication increases from 34% to 78%. However, probability of survival for the person in the housing is 23%."*
*"And without someone in the housing?"*
*"Probability of catastrophic failure rises to 61%. In the event of catastrophic failure, oxygen reserves would sustain crew for approximately 9.7 hours. Insufficient time for alternative solutions."*
*Hassan nodded, as if the numbers had confirmed something he already knew.*
*"Twenty-three percent is better odds than I expected. Let's get to work."*

---

*They gave him forty minutes.*
*Forty minutes inside a metal coffin, temperatures climbing toward the edge of human tolerance, sweat soaking through his suit while his hands made adjustments that had to be perfect. Forty minutes of Marcus's voice in his ear, guiding him through procedures that had never been designed for field conditions. Forty minutes of everyone else standing helpless, watching vital signs fluctuate on ARIA's displays.*
*At minute thirty-two, Hassan's heart rate spiked. The numbers climbed—140, 150, 160—and then stabilized just below the danger zone.*
*At minute thirty-six, Marcus reported the fabrication was complete. He began installing the new housing.*
*At minute thirty-nine, something went wrong.*
*Later, ARIA would analyze the data and determine that a secondary thermal seal had failed—another component weakened by the dust storm, another corner cut by PharmaCorp's quality assurance teams. The failure caused a pressure spike in the reaction chamber. The spike caused an explosion.*
*Hassan Farooq died instantly. The oxygen generator survived.*
*They found pieces of him scattered across the engineering bay, mixed with molten metal and shattered equipment. There wasn't enough left to bury.*

---

*The memorial was short. They were running on emergency oxygen reserves, waiting for Marcus to finish recalibrating the repaired generator. There wasn't time for proper grief.*
*Chen spoke: "Hassan Farooq gave his life so the rest of us could keep breathing. He knew the odds. He went anyway. That's not heroism—that's something beyond heroism. Something I don't have a word for."*
*Elena spoke: "He told me once that he came to Mars because he wanted to see something no human had ever seen. He did. He saw this crew. He saw what we're capable of, in the worst moments. And he decided we were worth dying for."*
*Marcus didn't speak. He'd been in the engineering bay when it happened, separated from Hassan by a wall that might as well have been the vacuum of space. He'd heard the explosion. He'd heard the silence afterward.*
*He stood at the edge of the gathering, covered in soot and coolant fluid, and said nothing at all.*
*They released Hassan's remains into the Martian atmosphere—what little they could collect. The wind caught the ashes and scattered them across the red landscape, mixing him with the dust of a world that had taken so much and would take so much more.*
*"Three graves now," Thea said that night. Her voice was numb. "Rachel. Sarah. Hassan. And we're not even halfway through."*
*Cassian held her and didn't argue.*

---

That night, Cassian pulled up ARIA's trajectory compensation logs from the past week — a routine he'd developed quietly, the way you do something you don't want to explain. Three days ago, ARIA had applied a 0.004-degree attitude correction at 1140 hours. He'd noticed it before the log entry. Thirty-seven seconds before, standing in the cultivation facility running nutrient calculations, some part of his brain had registered a drift pattern in the habitat's vibration frequency and filed it as a navigation concern without being asked to.

He closed the log. He didn't file a report. There was nothing to report — ARIA had handled it correctly, and thirty-seven seconds was not a meaningful margin. It was just the way his mind worked, apparently. Trajectory thinking. He was here to grow things. He went to bed.

---

*He read through the full study log the night after Hassan's memorial.*

*He'd started keeping it in Year Two — a personal file, encrypted, never transmitted, running alongside the official cultivation reports. Eighteen months of entries. He read them from the beginning, the way you sometimes need to read a thing from the beginning to understand what you've been building.*

*When he finished he sat in the dark of the cultivation facility, the tanks pulsing their slow rhythm at his back, and opened a new entry.*

*The cursor blinked.*

*He thought about Hassan saying I stopped being worried about the right things a long time ago. He thought about the eighteen-month-old who would never know there had been a gap. He thought about what it would mean if the thing he'd been watching — the synchronization, the variance, the unexplained elevations — meant something real. And what it would mean for the mission, for PharmaCorp, for the organism itself, if it did.*

*He typed:*

*Year 3, Month 11, Day 14.*

*Eighteen months. Forty-seven documented synchronization events. Bioluminescence elevation correlated with human emotional state in thirty-one of thirty-four cases where I had sufficient data to compare.*

*I have tried to explain this away. I cannot explain it away.*

*I don't know what you are. After three years and eighteen months of observations, I don't know what you are.*

*You are not what we told the funding committee you were. You are not what the procurement documents say you are. But you are not nothing, either.*

*You are something. I don't have a word for it.*

*What I know: you have been here for three billion years. You survived the collapse of the magnetic field, the atmosphere stripping away, the cold, the dark. You went underground and you kept going.*

*I came here because of my sister. I've been here three years and I'm still not sure what I'm building. But I come in here every morning at 0530 and I watch you and I think: you've been doing this alone for three billion years and you're still going.*

*I want to know how.*

*He closed the file. He went back to work.*

*Outside, the Martian night pressed itself against the viewport. The tanks pulsed on, unchanged, measuring time in units that had nothing to do with his.*

---

*In the weeks that followed Hassan's death, something shifted. The earlier deaths had been tragic but explicable—Rachel's suicide, Sarah's equipment failure. They could be filed away as aberrations.*
*Hassan had chosen to die. That was different. It forced a question no one wanted to answer aloud.*
*"You're overthinking again," Thea said one night. She was propped up on one elbow, watching him with the analytical expression that meant she'd already calculated several steps ahead.*
*"I'm always overthinking."*
*"More than usual." She traced a pattern on his chest. "You're thinking about what you would do."*
*"Aren't you?"*
*"Every day." Her voice was quiet. "I've been running scenarios. What would have to be true for me to make the same choice he did?"*
*"Did you find an answer?"*
*"I found math." She sat up, pulling the thin blanket around her shoulders. "Hassan wasn't being selfless. He was being rational. He calculated that his remaining life expectancy—given his age and health conditions—was maybe fifteen years. And he weighed that against twenty-one people who could have died if the generator failed."*
*"So he was just running the numbers?"*
*"We all run the numbers. All the time. We just don't like admitting it." Thea met his eyes. "The question isn't whether you'd sacrifice yourself for others. The question is: what's the exchange rate?"*
*"That's cold."*
*"That's survival." She lay back down. "Real altruism has a price tag. Hassan paid his because the numbers made sense. If you ever have to make the same choice, you'll do the same calculation."*
*"And what if the numbers say I should live?"*
*"Then you live. And you carry the guilt until you die of something else instead." Her voice was quiet now, not bitter. Resigned. "There's no version where you come out undamaged."*
*He held her closer. Three years in. Three more.*

---



# INTERLUDE


## Letters Home

*Personal transmissions, Year 3, various dates*
*[Compiled from ARIA communication logs]*

---

*Cassian Vale to Emily Vale*
*August 14, 2039*
*Em,*
*I don't know if you'll ever hear this. The communication delays are getting longer—something about solar conjunction, the sun getting between Mars and Earth. Your last message took six days to reach me.*
*We lost someone yesterday. Hassan Farooq. Atmospheric scientist. Nice guy—always had stories about his grandchildren, always saved extra food portions for the people who needed them most. He died fixing our oxygen generator. Walked into a room he knew might kill him, because the alternative was all of us suffocating.*
*I keep thinking about what it takes to do that. To know the odds and go anyway. To decide that your life is worth less than the mission, less than the people around you.*
*I'm not sure I could do it. I hope I never have to find out.*
*The organisms are producing well—better than projected, actually. We're on track to send home about 95 kilograms of Areserine this year, which translates to roughly 47,000 patient treatments. I try to think about that when things get dark. 47,000 people who will live because we're here. 47,000 families that won't lose someone the way we've lost Sarah and Hassan and Rachel.*
*Does that make it worth it? I don't know. Some days the math makes sense. Some days I stare at those tanks and wonder why I'm here instead of home with you, watching you fight this thing in person instead of from 225 million kilometers away.*
*How are you? Your last message said the treatment was holding, that you were feeling stronger. I want to believe that. I need to believe that. Because some days, when the work is crushing and the deaths are piling up and I can't remember why I came here, thinking about you is the only thing that keeps me moving.*
*Three more years, Em. Three more years and I'll be home. We'll sit on the porch and watch the sunset—a real sunset, not the pale imitation we get here—and I'll tell you about Mars. About the organisms that saved your life. About the people who died making that happen.*
*Hold on. Please. Just hold on a little longer.*
*I love you.*
*Cass*

---

*Thea Miren to Dr. Klaus Miren (Father)*
*September 2, 2039*
*Papa,*
*I don't know how to write this, so I'm just going to say it: I'm not okay. None of us are okay. And I'm starting to think we never will be again.*
*The psychologist tried to kill herself two months ago. The psychologist. The person who was supposed to keep us sane. She's alive, but she's not the same. None of us are.*
*I keep running the numbers. It's what I do—what I've always done. When I was a kid and you taught me probability theory, you said numbers were comforting because they didn't lie. But now I wish they would. Because the numbers are telling me things I don't want to hear.*
*62% survival probability for remaining crew. That means we're going to lose more people, Papa. Math doesn't lie. Probability doesn't care about hope or willpower or how hard we try.*
*Do you remember when I was twelve, and I calculated the probability of you surviving your heart surgery? You told me afterward that knowing the numbers helped, because at least you knew what you were facing. I didn't understand then. I thought you were just being brave for my sake.*
*Now I understand. The numbers aren't comforting because they're optimistic. They're comforting because they're honest. They tell you exactly how bad things are, and then you can decide whether to keep going anyway.*
*I'm going to keep going. But I wanted you to know how hard it is.*
*I'm in love, by the way. His name is Cassian. He's a botanist. He grows the organisms that are saving millions of lives, and he looks at me like I'm the most important thing in the universe, and I'm terrified that one of us is going to be in the 38% that doesn't make it.*
*I know you always wanted me to find someone. I just wish I'd found him somewhere other than a death trap 225 million kilometers from home.*
*Tell Mama I'm thinking of her. Tell her I wear the necklace she gave me every day—the one with St. Christopher, the patron saint of travelers. I'm not religious like she is, but it helps. Having something to hold onto.*
*Sometimes I take it off and just look at it. The way the light catches the silver. The weight of it in my palm. It reminds me that someone on Earth is thinking about me. That I exist in someone else's thoughts, even if I'm too far away to touch.*
*I'll write again when I can. The communication windows are getting shorter.*
*Love,*
*Thea*

---

*Dr. Viktor Kozlov to Kolya Kozlov (Deceased)*
*October 28, 2039*
*My son,*
*I know you can't hear this. I know you've been gone for four years now—longer than you were alive. I know that writing letters to a dead child is probably a sign that I'm losing my mind, and maybe I am.*
*But I see you everywhere, Kolya. In the corridors at night. In the cultivation facility, where the organisms glow like the nightlight you used to have in your room. In my dreams, where you're always asking me why I left.*
*I didn't leave you, malysh. You left me. The disease took you, and I couldn't stop it, and I've been running from that failure ever since.*
*Your mother couldn't forgive me. She said she didn't blame me—said there was nothing I could have done—but I saw it in her eyes. The question she never asked but couldn't stop thinking: Why couldn't you save him? You're a doctor. Why couldn't you save our son?*
*I couldn't answer her. I still can't.*
*That's why I'm here. Not to save humanity—that's what I tell people, but it's not true. I'm here because I couldn't save you, and I thought maybe if I helped save millions of other children, it would balance out somehow. Like there's a scale somewhere, measuring lives lost against lives saved, and I could tip it back toward redemption.*
*But it doesn't work that way. Does it?*
*Every life we save makes me think of the one I couldn't. Every success reminds me of my greatest failure. And now, three years into this mission, I'm starting to wonder if the scale will ever balance. If anything I do will ever be enough.*
*The other night, you came to me again. You were older this time—eight years old, the age you would have been if you'd lived. You sat on my bunk and asked me if I was happy. I didn't know what to say. I don't think I've been happy since the day you died.*
*Dr. Osei says I should report these visions. Says they might be hallucinations, symptoms of something that needs treatment. But what treatment is there for grief? What medication cures the hole you left behind?*
*I love you, Kolya. I loved you when you were born, and I loved you when you died, and I love you still. Even though you're gone. Even though I'm talking to an empty room and calling it a letter.*
*Someday, maybe, I'll stop seeing you. Someday the visions will fade and you'll just be a memory instead of a presence. I don't know if I want that day to come.*
*Your Papa*

---

*Elena Vasquez to Maria Vasquez (Mother) [UNSENT — PERSONAL LOG]*
*November 15, 2039*
*I found something. Something I wasn't supposed to find.*
*You know how I told you the geological surveys were for "scientific purposes"? That was what PharmaCorp told us. That was the official story.*
*It's bullshit. The surveys are mapping rare earth element deposits—neodymium, lanthanum, dysprosium. Minerals worth trillions of dollars. PharmaCorp isn't here to save humanity. They're here to exploit Mars the same way they exploited Sistema Abismo. The same way they exploit everything they touch.*
*The Areserine mission is real, but it's also a cover story. A way to get public support for sending people to Mars so they can begin extracting the planet's resources. We're guinea pigs AND miners, all in one convenient package.*
*I've started collecting evidence. Emails, internal documents, anything I can find. I don't know what I'll do with it yet—maybe nothing. Maybe I'll just hold onto it until we get home, and then I'll burn PharmaCorp to the ground.*
*Or maybe I'll die out here like everyone else seems to be doing, and the truth will die with me.*
*You raised me to fight, Mamá. You told me that the powerful always prey on the powerless, and that the only way to survive is to push back. I'm pushing back. In secret, for now, because I don't know who I can trust. But the evidence is growing, and someday—someday—I'll have enough to make them pay.*
*I'm sorry. I know you wanted me to come home rich and successful, with my debts paid off and a future ahead of me. I wanted that too. But I'm starting to think the price of that future is higher than I'm willing to pay.*
*I love you, Mamá. I love all of you. No matter what happens, remember that.*
*Tu Elena*

---

*"Dr. Vale." ARIA's voice came through the facility speakers. "You have completed trajectory optimization calculations 47% faster than projected. Your spatial reasoning scores place you in the 97th percentile for mission-relevant cognitive tasks."*
*Cassian looked up from his work. "I'm not being evaluated, ARIA."*
*"Continuous assessment is part of my psychological monitoring protocols. Your cognitive profile is... unusual for your designated role."*
*"My designated role is botanist."*
*"Yes. Yet your performance on navigation, orbital mechanics, and systems integration tasks exceeds that of most crew members with engineering backgrounds. This discrepancy has been noted."*
*"Noted by who?"*
*"By me. For mission records." A pause. "Why did you become a botanist, Dr. Vale?"*
*"That's personal."*
*"Understood. However, the statistical anomaly remains interesting."*
*Cassian didn't respond. He returned to his organisms, to the work that defined him, and tried not to think about the flight simulators he'd once loved.*

---

*Commander Sarah Chen to [UNSENT — PERSONAL LOG]*
*November 30, 2039*
*I dreamed about Sistema Abismo again last night.*
*The cave. The darkness. The moment when the drill broke through into the sealed chamber and released something that had been waiting fifty million years to escape.*
*In the dream, I'm standing at the mouth of the cave, watching the pathogen drift out like smoke. I know what's coming. I know that in six months, people will start dying. In a year, there will be quarantine zones. In two years, five hundred million corpses.*
*And in the dream, I have a choice. I can seal the cave—go back in time somehow and stop myself from signing that approval form. Or I can step aside and let history happen.*
*I always step aside. Every time. Because in the dream, just like in reality, I'm too much of a coward to take responsibility for what I've done.*
*Three people have died on this mission. Rachel's suicide, Sarah's accident, Hassan's sacrifice—they're all connected to that cave, to that decision, to my signature on a piece of paper I should never have signed.*
*ARIA says my psychological stability index is at 58. Down from 64 last year. Trending toward the threshold where she has to report me to PharmaCorp as a potential risk.*
*Maybe I should let her. Maybe I should stop pretending I'm fit to command this mission, stop pretending I deserve to survive when so many others haven't.*
*But I won't. Because these people need someone to blame, and I'd rather it be me than each other. I'd rather carry the weight of everything that's gone wrong than watch them tear themselves apart trying to figure out whose fault it is.*
*That's not leadership. It's penance.*
*And it's all I have left to give.*

---

*Marcus Okafor to [UNSENT — PERSONAL LOG]*
*December 8, 2039*
*I can't stop seeing her face.*
*Sarah Mitchell. The moment she realized she was dying. The way she looked at me through her visor, already turning blue, and smiled.*
*Smiled. Like she was apologizing for inconveniencing me. Like her death was something she needed to make easier for the person watching it happen.*
*I held her for three hours. On the Martian surface, in a suit that was running low on oxygen, I held a dead woman and couldn't let go. Commander Chen had to order me back inside. Even then, I almost didn't comply.*
*The psychiatrists would call it trauma bonding. Survivor's guilt. Some clinical term that reduces the worst moment of my life to a checkbox on a diagnostic form.*
*But it was more than that. When I held Sarah's body, I understood something I hadn't understood before: we're not going to make it. Not all of us. Maybe not any of us. This mission is going to grind us down until there's nothing left, and the people on Earth will remember us as heroes while forgetting that heroes are usually just victims with better publicity.*
*I keep working. That's all I know how to do. Fix the machines that keep us alive. Maintain the systems that haven't failed yet. Wait for the next crisis, the next death, the next moment when everything I've built stops being enough.*
*Hassan knew. When he walked into that oxygen generator, he knew he was going to die. And part of me envied him—envied the clarity of his choice, the purity of his sacrifice. He knew exactly what his death was for.*
*The rest of us just keep living, hoping our deaths will mean something when they finally come.*
*I'm so tired. I'm so goddamn tired.*
*But I'll get up tomorrow. I'll check the systems and run the diagnostics and pretend everything is fine. Because that's what we do out here.*
*We pretend until we can't pretend anymore.*
*And then we die.*

---

*Dmitri Volkov to Anya Volkov (Daughter)*
*December 17, 2039*
*Anyushka,*
*I received your last message three days ago. I have read it eleven times.*
*You told me about your science project — the one about tidal forces. You drew the diagrams yourself, your teacher said. I know they were good diagrams. You always drew good diagrams. You used to sit at the kitchen table with your colored pencils while I read the newspaper, and I would steal glances at what you were making, and every time I thought: she has better spatial reasoning than half the engineers I have worked with.*
*You are ten years old. You will be twelve when I come home, if I come home. I am trying to be honest with you, because I think you deserve honesty.*
*I wrote you a letter last week and deleted it. I wrote you another and deleted that one too. They were the letters a father writes when he is afraid, and I didn't want to send you that. I wanted to send you something useful instead.*
*So here is something useful: the organisms we are harvesting glow in the dark. Cyan, mostly, with amber underneath. Your mother would say it looks like the aurora borealis — you have seen those pictures she has on the wall, the ones from when she traveled to Norway before you were born. It looks like that, but underground, in a lava tube on Mars, which I know sounds impossible.*
*I stood in that tube six months ago and thought about you. About bringing you something. A rock, maybe. A photograph.*
*I am going to tell you about the Ribbon when I get home. You will want to hear about the Ribbon.*
*Eat your breakfast. Do your homework. Do not let your grandmother convince you that you don't need to study mathematics — you do. Be kind to your mother. She is doing something hard and she is doing it without complaining, which is where you get it from.*
*I love you more than I can say in any language I know, and I know four.*
*Your Papa*

---

*PharmaCorp Quarterly Shareholder Communication — Q3 2039*
*"The Ares III mission continues to perform in line with projections. Areserine production remains on track for full payload delivery per the original mission timeline. All twenty-one remaining crew members continue their essential work with the dedication and professionalism that has defined this historic mission. The board is pleased to confirm that preliminary planning for an Ares IV mission is underway, with a projected 2044 launch window. We remain committed to our mission of science for humanity."*
*Eighteen months passed. The mission continued. The graves multiplied—another crew member lost to the slow attrition of exhaustion and overwork. By mid-Year 4, four markers stood in the Martian soil, and the crew had stopped counting days until home.*

---

*ARIA flagged the anomaly at 14:09 on November 22nd.*

*A transmission had arrived from PharmaCorp Mission Operations — Director Halsey, standard routing. File size on receipt: 2,341 words. Transmission quality: nominal. Decryption: successful.*

*Eleven minutes later, a second transmission arrived on the same thread. File size: 183 words.*

*The first file had been replaced before crew delivery.*

*Cassian noticed the discrepancy at 14:53, running his standard log review in the cultivation facility. He cross-referenced the receipt timestamp against the stored file. The numbers didn't match.*

*"Two files were received on the Halsey routing thread," ARIA confirmed. "The first file was overwritten by the second prior to crew delivery. The overwrite originated from the PharmaCorp relay architecture."*

*"Can you recover the first file?"*

*"No. The overwrite was complete."*

*He watched the 183-word message that evening.*

*Halsey was at the same desk. Composed. Carefully composed. His eyes dropped once, briefly, to something below frame — notes, a second screen, a reminder of what not to say — before centering on the camera. The gesture lasted less than a second.*

*The message was brief. Production milestone acknowledgment. Holiday greetings. A reference to his daughter's science class having watched the Ribbon footage. She'd asked whether the crew could see Earth from Mars. He'd said he didn't know. He'd have to ask.*

*"You gave the world something it needed. Something beautiful." His eyes were steady on the camera. "We're proud of everything you're achieving up there."*

*He touched his jaw once, absently, near the end. Stopped himself.*

*"Take care."*

*The recording ended.*

*Cassian sat in the dark of his quarters, thinking about 2,341 words and what a man writes in 2,341 words and then decides against.*

*He noted it in his personal log. The one he didn't transmit.*

*Year 3, Month 11. Halsey transmission: file size discrepancy. 2,341 words replaced by 183 before delivery. Content of original: unknown. He looked at something below frame before recording. Touched his jaw and stopped.*

*Someone wrote something and decided not to send it.*

*Filed under: things I don't know what to do with.*

---



# ACT THREE: THE BREAKING



# Chapter 13


## The Threshold


### Year 4, Month 6 — June 2040

*Dr. Amara Osei stared at the data on her screen and felt something cold settle into her chest.*
*Eleven names. Eleven crew members flagged by ARIA's psychological monitoring system in the past ninety days. Not gradual declines—sudden shifts, behavioral changes that appeared almost overnight. People who had been stable for three years were now showing warning signs that the AI couldn't explain.*
*"ARIA," Amara said quietly, aware that others might be listening. "Explain the pattern."*
*"The pattern is consistent with third-quarter phenomenon, a documented psychological crisis occurring at approximately the 75% mark of long-duration isolation missions. Research from Antarctic winter-over stations, submarine deployments, and ISS expeditions confirms similar timing and characteristics."*
*"Third-quarter phenomenon." Amara had heard the term in training, filed it away as something theoretical. "We're at month 54 of a 72-month mission. That's exactly 75%."*
*"Correct. The phenomenon is characterized by sudden behavioral shifts rather than gradual decline. Previously stable individuals may experience rapid onset of depression, paranoia, aggression, or psychotic symptoms. The changes are non-linear and difficult to predict using standard psychological modeling."*
*"Which is why your accuracy has dropped."*
*"My prediction accuracy for psychological crises has decreased from 92% to 34% over the past three months. I am unable to reliably forecast which crew members will experience severe deterioration."*
*Amara closed her eyes. Thirty-four percent. Worse than a coin flip. Their AI guardian, designed specifically to catch warning signs, was now essentially guessing.*
*"Who are the eleven?" she asked.*
*"Commander Sarah Chen. Dr. Viktor Kozlov. Maria Santos. Daniel Kim. Sophie Laurent. Rachel Park—" ARIA paused. "Correction: Rachel Park is deceased. The flag was generated from historical data that has not been properly archived."*
*"Continue."*
*"Dmitri Volkov. Isabella Torres. Thomas Wright. Jackson Moore. And Dr. James Torres."*
*Amara scanned the list. The names represented nearly half the remaining crew. Commander Chen, whose stability had been declining for years. Viktor, with his hallucinations of Kolya. Maria Santos, whose religious devotion had been intensifying to the point of obsession. Daniel Kim, the youngest crew member, who had been showing signs of rage and paranoia.*
*And Torres—the organism specialist, who had seemed perfectly stable until three weeks ago.*
*"What triggered Torres?"*
*"Unknown. Dr. Torres has not exhibited significant external stressors. His work performance remains adequate. However, his sleep patterns have become erratic, and his linguistic analysis shows increasing fixation on mortality-related themes."*
*"Is he suicidal?"*
*"Insufficient data. His profile does not match historical patterns of suicidal ideation, but as noted, my predictive accuracy is significantly impaired."*
*Amara pulled up Torres's recent logs, scanning for anything she might have missed. Work notes, mostly—observations about organism behavior, cultivation adjustments, production metrics. Normal scientist stuff.*
*Then she found it. A personal log entry from six days ago, marked private but accessible to medical personnel under emergency protocols:*
*The organisms know. I don't know how, but they know. They've been watching us since the beginning, recording everything, waiting. Three billion years of waiting. What are they waiting for? What do they want?*

*I asked ARIA if she thought they were intelligent. She said intelligence is a loaded term. Depends on definitions. But she didn't say no.*

*Something is wrong. Something has been wrong since the beginning. We thought we were studying them, but maybe they've been studying us. Maybe the Areserine isn't a cure. Maybe it's something else.*

*I need to know. I need to understand what we've really found.*
*Amara read the entry twice, her unease growing with each word. Torres was spiraling—not toward suicide, but toward something else. Paranoid ideation. Conspiracy thinking. The kind of mental break that could destabilize an entire crew if left unchecked.*
*"ARIA, flag Dr. Torres for immediate intervention. I want a full psychological evaluation before he returns to the cultivation facility."*
*"Acknowledged. Shall I notify Commander Chen?"*
*Amara hesitated. Chen was on the list too—her stability index had been in free fall for months. Could she handle this information? Could she make rational decisions about crew welfare when her own mind was fragmenting?*
*"No," Amara said finally. "Not yet. I'll handle Torres myself. And ARIA—keep monitoring everyone on that list. I want real-time updates on any behavioral changes."*
*"Understood. Dr. Osei, I should note that my ability to detect changes is impaired. I may not catch everything."*
*"I know." Amara stared at the eleven names, feeling the weight of responsibility settle onto her shoulders. "None of us can. That's the problem."*

---

*The crew meeting that afternoon was tense.*
*Amara had decided to inform everyone about the third-quarter phenomenon—not the specific names ARIA had flagged, but the general concept. Knowledge was better than ignorance, even if that knowledge was terrifying.*
*"What you're feeling is documented," she told them. "Expected. And it's going to get worse before it gets better." She looked around the room. "Watch yourselves. Watch each other."*
*"And if it gets worse?" Daniel asked.*
*"Then we deal with it."*
*The meeting ended without resolution, as most meetings did these days.*

---

*Dmitri received a message from Anya that evening.*
*Cassian passed the communications terminal on his way to the cultivation facility and saw him there — sitting still in a way that Dmitri almost never sat still, the screen's light on his face, lips moving slightly as he read. The message was short. Anya's messages were always short; she was ten years old and had not yet learned to fill space with words. But Dmitri read it three times, his expression doing something complicated and private that Cassian looked away from.*
*When Dmitri stood to leave, he caught Cassian watching.*
*"She lost a tooth. The last baby tooth. She says she feels very grown up now." He looked at the screen for a moment, then switched it off. "She will be grown up by the time I see her."*
He walked out. Cassian stood in the corridor, then continued to the cultivation facility. The organisms pulsed their slow rhythm, the only light in the corridor’s dead air, indifferent and patient.

---

*That evening, Amara found Dr. Torres in the cultivation facility, staring at the organism tanks with an expression that made her blood run cold.*
*"James?" She kept her voice soft, non-threatening. "Are you okay?"*
*Torres didn't turn around. "They're communicating, you know. The organisms. I've been watching them for days. The pulse patterns—they're not random. They're a language."*
*"James, the synchronized pulsing is a documented phenomenon. It's probably chemical signaling, or—"*
*"It's not just signaling." Torres finally turned to face her, and Amara saw something broken behind his eyes. "They're talking about us. They've been watching us since we arrived, recording everything. Learning."*
*"Learning what?"*
*"Everything. Our weaknesses. Our fears. What makes us break." Torres's voice dropped to a whisper. "Think about it, Amara. These organisms have survived for three billion years. They've outlasted everything Mars threw at them—the loss of atmosphere, the radiation, the cold. And now we show up, harvest them, force them to produce Areserine for us. Do you really think they're just... passive? Just going along with it?"*
*"They're single-celled organisms, James. They don't have nervous systems. They can't 'think' in any meaningful sense."*
*"Can't they?" Torres moved closer, his intensity frightening. "We don't understand them. We've barely scratched the surface of their biology. What if they've developed something we can't recognize? What if intelligence can take forms we've never imagined?"*
*Amara made a decision. "James, I'm placing you on restricted duty. No more solo time in the cultivation facility. No access to samples without supervision."*
*"You can't—I'm the lead organism specialist—"*
*"I can and I am."*
*Torres stared at her. His certainty faltered. "You think I'm crazy."*
*"I think you need sleep." She took his arm. "Come on."*
*Torres let himself be led away, casting one last look at the tanks as he went. The organisms pulsed on, unchanged.*

---



# Chapter 14


## The Accident

*Two months earlier.*

### Year 4, Month 4 — April 2040

*Cassian found Robert Hayashi at 0300 hours, cross-legged on the floor of the equipment bay, surrounded by the disassembled components of Mining Unit 2 arranged in concentric rings around him.*
*He'd been making his way to the cultivation facility for a late check when he saw the light under the bay door—the particular blue-white of work lamps rather than the amber of standby mode. He almost kept walking. Everyone had their private hours.*
*But something made him stop.*
*Hayashi didn't look up when Cassian stepped inside. He was turning a servo housing in his hands, examining it with the focused quiet of someone who'd gone somewhere internal and needed a minute to come back. His coffee sat untouched on the workbench, long cold.*
*"Can't sleep." Eventually. Not a question.*
*"Night check." Cassian looked at the components laid out across the floor—brackets, couplings, sensor arrays, each placed with a precision that looked almost meditative. "What are you doing?"*
*"Looking for where she wears." Hayashi set the housing down and picked up a gear assembly. "This one's been running slightly hot. Not enough to flag ARIA's thresholds. But you can feel it if you know what to feel for." He ran his thumb along the teeth. "My mother worked the floor of a Toyota plant in Nagoya for twenty-two years. Assembly line. She always said the machines talk to you if you listen right. Not in words. In the gap between how it should sound and how it does."*
*Cassian sat down on the edge of a parts crate. The floor was cold through his suit but he didn't move. "She teach you that?"*
*"She taught me that a hand-fitted tolerance is different from a machine-fitted one. That you can feel the difference between a component assembled by someone who understood what it was for and one assembled by someone running a quota." He held the gear up to the light. "This one—whoever put it together understood it. Good work in here. Makes me feel better about the whole unit."*
*He set it back in its place in the ring with the same care he'd shown each piece.*
*"I have a storage unit in Osaka." After a moment. "HO scale. The whole Shinkansen network—0 Series, 100 Series, E5. About four years of weekends to build." A pause. "I was going to set it up when I got back. Proper layout, not just track on a table. My daughter's seven now. By the time I'm home she'll be ten and probably think trains are boring." He almost smiled. "I'm going to make her think they're not."*
*Cassian didn't have anything to say to that. So he just sat there while Hayashi began reassembling the unit, each piece going back in with the same unhurried attention it had come out with, until the equipment bay was quiet except for the soft click of components finding their places.*
*When Cassian left, he looked back once. Hayashi was still there in the blue-white light, hands moving, lips slightly parted like he was talking to the machine or to himself or to the daughter who was seven years old and growing up without him.*
*He would think of that image—not the funeral, not the marker—when Marcus came to find him six weeks later.*

---


### Year 4, Month 5 — May 2040

*Robert Hayashi died because he was tired.*
*The robotics technician had been working double shifts for three weeks, covering for crew members who were too sick or too broken to maintain their schedules. The mining equipment—the autonomous drillers and loaders that PharmaCorp had insisted on including for "geological research"—required constant maintenance, and Hayashi was one of only two people qualified to perform it.*
*The accident happened at 0347 hours, when Hayashi was alone in the equipment bay. ARIA's reconstruction showed the sequence of events with clinical precision:*
*0342: Hayashi begins diagnostic procedure on Mining Unit 3.*
*0344: Hayashi disengages safety lock to access internal components.*
*0345: Hayashi enters maintenance position beneath the unit's loading arm.*
*0346: Mining Unit 3 experiences power surge due to faulty capacitor.*
*0347: Loading arm activates. Hayashi is unable to clear the strike zone.*
*Time of death: 0347:23.*
*Marcus found him an hour later, during a routine equipment check. The loading arm had caught Hayashi across the chest, crushing his ribcage and severing his spine. Death had been instantaneous—a small mercy in a situation that offered few.*
*"He was asleep," Marcus reported to Chen, his voice hollow. "I found his coffee cup on the workbench. Stone cold. He'd been fighting to stay awake for hours, and he lost."*
*"The safety lock should have prevented—"*
*"The safety lock was disengaged. Standard procedure for the diagnostic he was running." Marcus's hands were shaking. "He did everything right, Commander. Everything by the book. But he was so tired he didn't hear the power surge warning. Didn't react fast enough to get clear."*
*Chen closed her eyes. Another death. Another body to bury in frozen regolith. Another message to compose for family members who wouldn't receive it for twelve minutes.*
*"PharmaCorp's equipment," Elena said from the doorway. Her voice was ice. "PharmaCorp's schedule. PharmaCorp's mining operations that we're supposed to maintain alongside everything else. Robert died because they wanted us to do two jobs with half the resources."*
*"Elena—"*
*"Don't." Elena stepped into the room, her eyes blazing. "Don't tell me to calm down. Don't tell me to be professional. Robert Hayashi worked himself to death because this mission is designed to extract maximum value from minimum investment. We're not crew to them. We're equipment. And when we break, they just note the loss and keep calculating their profit margins."*
*Chen had no argument to offer. Elena was right. They all knew Elena was right.*
*The funeral was brief. Twenty people gathered outside the habitat. No one spoke a eulogy.*
*The stone marker read: Robert Hayashi. Robotics Technician. 1999–2040.*
*Afterward, Marcus stood with his hands at his sides, not moving. "Thirty-six hours without sleep." Quietly, to no one. Then he walked back inside.*
*The others followed. Not together — individually, in ones and twos, the way people leave a place when there is nothing left to do there. The habitat door cycled. The airlock hissed. Within ten minutes the grave marker stood alone in the rust-colored soil with no one looking at it.*
*Cassian returned to the cultivation facility. He ran the 0900 diagnostics. He adjusted the nitrogen feed on Tank 3, which had been running slightly low. He logged the yield projection — 149.2 kilograms annualized — and filed it with ARIA. He did not think about Robert Hayashi while he worked, or he thought about him constantly in the way that means the same thing, the way grief sits in the background of every action and simply becomes the new texture of getting through the day.*
*At 0947, ARIA noted that the 0900 diagnostics were complete and asked if he required anything further.*
*"No."*
*He kept working.*

---



# Chapter 15


## The Heart


### Year 4, Month 9 — September 2040

*That morning, Cassian made pancakes.*
*It wasn't planned. He'd found a surplus of spirulina flour during an inventory check—three days' worth of hydroponics overage that would expire if no one used it—and there was crystallized honey in the back of the condiment stores that he'd been rationing since Year 2. It felt like a reason.*
*He mixed the batter before anyone else was up, working quietly in the galley while the habitat's night cycle held. By the time the first crew members shuffled in, the smell had already done something the intercoms couldn't: pulled people out of their quarters without being asked.*
*They sat together. Not at assigned seats, not in the fractured clusters that mealtimes had become. Together. Someone found a recording—real Earth rain, a field somewhere with wind moving through it—and played it low through the common area speakers. Nobody mentioned how strange it was to listen to weather they hadn't felt in four years.*
*Lian Wei ate her first pancake in silence, eyes half-closed, expression unreadable. She asked for a second, adding that the spirulina flour still wasn't right—the same complaint she'd made about every variant he'd tried, every morning for four years. He slid the plate across without comment. Then she pressed her palms together toward Cassian in a small gesture of thanks that she'd never made before, and asked for a third.*
*She died on the fourth bite of that third pancake.*
*One moment she was at the table, fork still in hand. The next she was on the floor, convulsing, her face turning gray as her cardiac system simply shut down. The rain recording kept playing. Nobody turned it off.*
*Amara was there in seconds, chest compressions already beginning before Lian's cup had finished rolling across the floor. Marcus grabbed the defibrillator. Cassian cleared the table to create a working space. Everyone else stood frozen, watching another crew member die in front of them.*
*"Charging," Marcus said. "Clear."*
*The shock made Lian's body arch off the floor. Amara checked for a pulse. Nothing.*
*"Again. Charging. Clear."*
*Another shock. Another check. Nothing.*
*They tried for forty-seven minutes. CPR until Amara's arms gave out and Cassian took over. Defibrillation until the battery ran low. Epinephrine from their dwindling medical supplies. Everything they had, everything they knew, poured into the effort to restart a heart that had simply decided to stop.*
*At 0832 hours, Amara called it.*
*"Time of death: 0832. Cause: Cardiac arrest, likely stress-induced cardiomyopathy." Her voice was steady, professional. Her hands were shaking. "She had an undiagnosed heart condition. The stress of the mission pushed it past the breaking point."*
*"Basic tier modifications," Daniel spat from the corner. His face was twisted with rage that had nowhere to go. "She got the cheap gene editing. The stuff that was supposed to protect her heart didn't do shit."*
*"Daniel—"*
*"Don't tell me I'm wrong. We all saw the health reports. The Enhanced crew is fine—Marcus, Amara, Chen—you're all doing great. Standard tier is struggling. And Basic?" He gestured at Lian's body, still lying on the floor because no one had been able to move it yet. "Basic tier is dying. One by one. Because PharmaCorp decided some of us weren't worth the investment."*
*The silence that followed was deafening.*
*Cassian looked around the room, seeing the truth of Daniel's words reflected in faces he'd known for four years. The Enhanced crew did look healthier—fewer wrinkles, better muscle tone, the subtle signs of bodies that were aging more gracefully. The Standard crew showed more wear, more gray hair, more fatigue. And the Basic crew...*
*Daniel himself was a walking testament to inadequate modification. His skin was mottled with radiation damage. His hair had fallen out in patches. His eyes had the yellowed tinge of failing liver function. He was thirty-one years old and dying by inches, and everyone in the room knew it.*
*"We need to have this conversation," Amara said quietly. "Not now—not while Lian is still on the floor—but soon. The tier disparity is real, and pretending otherwise isn't helping anyone."*
*"What's to discuss?" Daniel's voice cracked. "They gave some of us the good stuff and some of us the garbage. They knew from the beginning that Basic tier would suffer more, die faster. They factored it into their projections. We're not crew, Amara. We're test subjects."*
*No one disagreed.*
*They buried Lian that afternoon. Five graves now, five markers in the red soil.*
*Cassian stood at the edge of the gathering, Thea's hand in his, and couldn't stop counting.*
*He went back to the galley afterward.*
*The batter bowl was still on the counter. He'd mixed enough for the whole crew — more than enough, the kind of optimistic quantity a person makes when they think a morning might go differently. Most of it was still there. The spatula was crusted where it had dried. The rain recording had been switched off at some point, and the galley was quiet in the specific way of a room that had recently held more people than it currently did.*
*He washed the bowl. He washed the spatula, the measuring cup, the mixing spoon. He wiped down the counter. He did it with the same attention he brought to the cultivation facility diagnostics — methodical, complete, one thing at a time — because the alternative was standing still, and standing still was no longer something he trusted himself to do.*
*When everything was clean and put away, he stood at the counter for a moment. Then he went back to work.*
Viktor sutured Daniel Kim’s forearm that afternoon.
The radiation ulcer had been weeping for a week — Basic tier, the skin no longer repairing itself efficiently — and Amara had finally insisted. She prepped the tray and stepped back, watching Viktor work.
His hands were steady. That was the first thing she always checked now: the hands. In surgery, competence lived in the hands long after it abandoned the mind, and Viktor’s hands had not yet forgotten what they knew. He worked with the precision she’d watched him demonstrate two hundred times — debridement, flush, closure, the needle moving through damaged tissue with the practiced economy of someone who had done this in conditions far worse than a pressurized habitat on Mars.
“Pressure on the distal edge.”
She applied it. Daniel looked away.
“The scarring will be significant,” Viktor continued, his voice clinical, focused. “We can limit the cosmetic impact with proper dressing changes, but without adequate cellular repair —” He paused. His hands didn’t stop. “Kolya had a scar on his chin. He fell off his bicycle when he was three. I told him it made him look distinguished.”
Amara kept her eyes on the wound. “Viktor.”
“He said distinguished was a word for old men.” Viktor tied off the suture, cut the thread, reached for the dressing without being asked. “He was right, probably. He was often right. Very logical child.”
“Viktor, are you —”
“Finished.” He taped the dressing in place with the same steady hands, the same clinical precision. He began stripping his gloves. “Change the dressing every thirty-six hours. Flag any increase in the discharge volume.”
He left before she could say anything else. She stood at the tray for a moment, looking at the neat row of instruments, the clean suture line, the perfect dressing. All of it correct. All of it exactly right.
She added a note to his file that she did not share with anyone.

---



# Chapter 16


## The Truth


### Year 4, Month 10 — October 2040

*Elena found the emails at 0300 hours, when everyone else was asleep.*
She hadn’t hacked anything. That was important to understand — she had no skills for it, no training, and PharmaCorp’s relay architecture made unauthorized intrusion effectively impossible from a terminal 225 million kilometers away. Everything she’d found, she’d found through doors she was already authorized to open.
The REE mapping work required access to PharmaCorp’s geological survey systems — supply manifests for equipment, logistics frameworks for extraction timelines, cross-referenced data packages that linked survey findings to operational planning. It was her job. She had read credentials. She had been using them for four years.
The work was tedious and, she had understood for months, almost certainly monitored. She moved through her authorized directories carefully, the way you move through a space that belongs to someone else. Most of what she found was administrative — supply manifests, production updates, mission directives that confirmed what they already knew about PharmaCorp’s priorities. She kept looking because she was an engineer and engineers followed data trails to where they ended, even when they suspected what waited there.
*Tonight, buried inside a supply manifest cross-reference she'd opened looking for something else entirely, she found a linked attachment that shouldn't have been there. A single internal document connected to the wrong file, one wrong click away from a corporate planning thread that predated launch by eight months.*
*She read it twice before she understood what she was looking at.*
*FROM: J. Morrison, VP Strategic Planning*
*TO: Executive Committee*
*RE: Phase Integration — ARES III*

*Following board alignment on the dual-track model, please note revised internal benchmarks for Phase I completion. The humanitarian component continues to represent our strongest regulatory and public affairs asset and should lead all external communications. Internal projections remain confidential per standing policy.*

*Phase II readiness targets are attached. Survey completion by Month 30 remains essential to Phase II timeline. Geological team resourcing per Addendum 7-B reflects this priority.*

*REE extraction rights position is detailed in the Phase II planning document. Legal advises the current mission framing provides optimal treaty compliance posture for transition.*
*The phrase that stopped her was humanitarian component. Not mission. Not objective. Component. The way you'd say marketing component, or regulatory component. One piece of a larger architecture.*
*She sat with that for a long moment, then opened the Phase II planning document the email referenced.*
*It was dense — procurement language, logistics frameworks, crew sizing rationale. She had to read it twice to find the number: forty-eight. Then a second number, buried in a footnote referencing projected site infrastructure: $24.7 trillion estimated extraction value, Phase II operational window 2044–2052.*
*She pulled up the geological survey files she'd spent four years filing and cross-referenced them against the extraction zone coordinates in the document. The overlay was exact. She had been mapping this. For eight months, under the language of "concurrent scientific objectives," she had been mapping the extraction sites for a mining operation nobody had told her about.*
*She stared at the coordinates for a long moment. Then she pulled up the tube survey from Year 1 — the one she'd filed the morning after the vehicle fell through, standing in that light — and overlaid it.*
*The extraction zone sat directly above the colony.*
*She had run this geometry once before, standing in the tube with her helmet light cutting through ancient bioluminescence, and she had filed it away because she must have been working from incomplete data, because there had to be another configuration, because PharmaCorp couldn't have known.*
*They had known. They had labeled it Priority Alpha before they launched.*
*She kept reading.*
*The crew modification budget was a spreadsheet — columns for tier designation, protection package specification, per-unit cost, headcount. The numbers were precise: $4.2 million, $1.8 million, $0.6 million. At the bottom of the Tier C column — six crew members, the lowest allocation — a single cross-reference: see Research Protocol 7-C (concurrent operations).*
*Research Protocol 7-C took her four minutes to find. It was a data collection framework. Longitudinal physiological monitoring, radiation exposure progression, modification response tracking. Standard language throughout — the kind of clinical neutrality that made every sentence sound procedural rather than intentional.*
*The subject population, specified in section 1.2, was Tier C mission personnel.*
*She read the section three times. Then she looked up from the terminal at the dark habitat around her and thought about Daniel Kim's mottled skin. About Sophie's slow deterioration. About the progression Amara had been quietly charting for two years in the medical logs.*
*Concurrent operations. They had been the operation.*
*She kept digging. Near the bottom of the planning thread, a document labeled simply Phase I Integration Notes — Post-Mission Planning:*
*Legacy documentation value has proven significant across modeled scenarios. Crew testimony, both structured and incidental, provides substantial public affairs utility and should be preserved and archived regardless of mission outcome.*

*Recommend mandatory testimony protocols for Phase II crew per attached communications framework.*
*Regardless of mission outcome.*
*Elena sat in the dark for a long time, not moving.*
*She had known they were expendable. She had suspected the mining. She had built her case piece by piece for months, adding inference to inference, and she had thought that when she finally found it she would feel something clean — vindication, fury, the specific satisfaction of being right.*
*What she felt instead was very small.*
*Regardless of mission outcome. Not: if they died. Not: in the event of catastrophic crew loss. The outcome was already a variable, already planned around, already accounted for in the communications framework. They had modeled the scenarios. The scenarios included everyone in this habitat dying on Mars, and in that scenario the recommendation was the same as any other: archive the footage, use the testimonials, proceed with Phase II.*
*She thought of Robert Hayashi counting train pieces in the equipment bay. Of Hassan walking into the oxygen generator. Of Rachel's body in the airlock, arms at her sides.*
*Then she downloaded everything — the planning thread, the cross-references, the protocol, the Phase II document — and copied it to three separate storage devices: a data chip in the lining of her thermal jacket, another in the equipment bay behind a coolant panel, a third in the cultivation facility inside a dummy organism sample container that only she knew was hollow.*
*Later — much later, lying in her bunk in the dark — it would occur to her that all three copies were inside PharmaCorp property. The jacket, the equipment bay, the sample container. If Ares IV arrived and did a thorough inventory, which they would, every hiding place was inside the sphere of the thing she was trying to expose. The only copy that would truly escape was the one on the return ship — received at Cape Canaveral by government recovery teams, outside PharmaCorp's reach. That was the copy that mattered.*
*She hadn't thought of that at 0300 hours. She'd thought of redundancy, of backup, of the way engineers built systems: multiple copies, multiple locations, nothing lost if one failed. She'd thought like a scientist and not like a whistleblower, and the difference was exactly the kind of thing you couldn't see from inside it.*
*She filed that thought away with all the others she couldn't do anything about.*
*She didn't sleep.*

---

*Elena Vasquez [UNSENT — PERSONAL LOG]*
*October 14, 2040*
*I found proof tonight.*
*Not suspicion. Not inference. Proof — internal corporate planning documents timestamped eight months before launch, outlining Phase II extraction targets for neodymium, lanthanum, and dysprosium deposits across the Ares Vallis basin. The Areserine mission is the cover. We are the cover.*
*I am copying everything I can access to local encrypted storage. I will not transmit any of this through PharmaCorp's relay. They own the bandwidth. They own the satellites. They own the communications terminal I am sitting at right now.*
*They do not own what I put in my own files.*
*If I make it home, I will take this apart piece by piece in public. If I don't — whoever finds these logs will know what they did here and why we died.*

---

*Dmitri found her two days later.*
*Not sought her out — found her, the way you find someone when you have been circling the same thought for months and finally run out of reasons not to say it. She was in the corridor outside the geology lab, tablet in hand, and he came around the corner and stopped.*
*He looked at her for a moment. Then he held up one finger — a single gesture, no words — and his eyes moved briefly to the ceiling where ARIA's environmental sensor sat in its housing, its microphone array a small dark grille behind the ventilation cover. Elena followed his gaze.*
*She understood.*
*He took her by the arm, not roughly, and she let him steer her. Down the service corridor toward the equipment bay — one of the few areas where PharmaCorp's monitoring infrastructure was thinner, installed for machinery diagnostics rather than crew surveillance, its audio coverage partial and its camera angles fixed on the equipment rather than the space between. He'd known that for years. Engineers learned where the gaps were.*
*He stopped at the far end of the bay, behind Mining Unit 2, in the corner its camera couldn't reach. He held out his hand and produced a maintenance log sheet from his breast pocket — the paper kind, used for analog backup when ARIA's systems were in diagnostic mode. He had a marker already uncapped.*
*He turned so his back was to the room, the paper angled against his chest where no camera could read it, and wrote:*
*Zone 7-C. They knew before launch.*
*He turned the paper so she could read it. Watched her face.*
*She took the marker. Wrote beneath his line:*
*Phase II. Extraction. $24.7T. Documents confirmed.*
*He read it. His jaw tightened once, a brief movement that was the only thing that showed. Then he took the marker back and wrote:*
*Orbital survey data from Ares I. They had it. They labeled it Priority Alpha anyway.*
*She nodded. Took the marker.*
*The organisms. The colony under the tube. They know.*

---

*EARTH — PHARMCORP HEADQUARTERS, LONDON*
*LEGAL AND COMMUNICATIONS REVIEW — OCTOBER 2040*

*"She's been running geological database queries outside her mission authorization."*

*Five people in a smaller room. No view of the Thames from this one. A data analyst had built the query log into something that could be read like a story: search terms, access timestamps, cross-referenced against mission parameters. Elena Vasquez, Geologist, Ares III. Authorization level: geological survey, Addendum 7-B zones only. Actual access: wider. Significantly wider.*

*"How far outside?"*

*"REE deposit modeling. Internal extraction projections from the 2033 pre-mission planning files. Historical pricing data. She's been systematic — small queries, spaced out, wouldn't trigger the automated flag threshold individually." The analyst advanced through the log. "But sequentially, it tells you something."*

*The legal counsel looked at the query timeline. "How long has she known?"*

*"Our best estimate is she's been collecting since Year Three, Month Seven. Fourteen months."*

*"And she hasn't transmitted any of it."*

*"No outbound transmission of any flagged material. She's storing locally."*

*The room was quiet for a moment.*

*"Options," said the communications director.*

*"We can recall her. Medical grounds, mission parameter violation, there are contractual mechanisms." Legal paused. "The disruption to production in Year Four would be significant. We're at a critical cultivation phase. Vasquez is the only certified geological surveyor on crew. Recalling her creates a gap we can't fill before mission end."*

*"What about the evidence she's collected?"*

*"It doesn't leave Mars unless she transmits it or physically carries it on the return vehicle. If she transmits, we intercept. If she carries it—" Legal looked at the manifest projections. "There are variables."*

*"So we monitor."*

*"We monitor. We accelerate the Ares IV planning timeline so that by the time anything she has reaches Earth, the REE operation is already legally established and operationally underway. Fait accompli is a better legal position than suppression."*

*"And Vasquez?"*

*A pause.*

*"She's doing her job," someone said. "She found what any good geologist would find, given the data she had access to. We can't blame her for being competent."*

*"We're not blaming her. We're deciding what to do with what she found."*

*The word liability was used once. The word crew was used once. The word asset was used twice — both times in reference to Elena Vasquez's geological expertise and its value to mission completion.*

*No one raised their voice. The meeting lasted twenty-two minutes. Everyone went back to their offices, and the monitoring continued, and Elena Vasquez kept building her archive in encrypted local storage three hundred million kilometers away, and no one on the crew ever knew the meeting had happened.*

---

*He looked at her for a long moment — the specific look of a man confirming something he had been hoping was wrong. Then he wrote:*
*How long have you known?*
*She thought about it. Wrote:*
*Since Year 1. I ran the geometry. Didn't finish the calculation.*
*Something crossed his face. Not surprise. The particular expression of someone recognizing a thing they had done themselves. He took the marker and wrote only:*
*Same.*
They stood in the corner behind Mining Unit 2 , not moving.
*Then he took the paper, folded it twice, and held it in his fist. She watched him look at the equipment bay around them — at the machines he had maintained for four years, the drilling units that had mapped the surface above a three-billion-year-old colony, the equipment that was the physical body of the conspiracy they had both been carrying alone.*
*He held her gaze.*
*She held it back.*
*He opened his fist and looked at the folded paper for a moment. Then he walked to the emergency equipment cabinet at the far wall, removed a small fire suppression canister, held the paper against its pilot flame until it caught and burned to nothing.*
*He replaced the canister. Closed the cabinet.*
*They walked out separately — him first, her two minutes later — and did not discuss it again. There was nothing to discuss. They had confirmed what they already knew. The knowledge was the same either way. The only difference was that now they were not carrying it alone, and that mattered in a way that had no practical consequence and nonetheless felt like something.*

---

*ARIA Behavioral Log — October 14, 2040*
*Anomaly notation for record:*
*Engineer D. Volkov entered Equipment Bay 2 at 14:32. Dr. E. Vasquez entered Equipment Bay 2 at 14:34. Audio coverage in Equipment Bay 2 is partial (machinery diagnostic configuration; coverage estimated at 34% of floor area). No audio capture from northeastern quadrant during this period.*
*D. Volkov exited Equipment Bay 2 at 14:51. Dr. E. Vasquez exited Equipment Bay 2 at 14:53.*
*Duration of overlap: approximately 19 minutes. No verbal exchange detected. No behavioral flags generated under current monitoring parameters.*
*Notation filed for completeness. No action recommended.*

---



# Chapter 17


## The Storm


### Year 4, Month 10 — October 2040

*The dust storm hit without warning.*
*One day the sky was its usual salmon-pink, the sun pale and distant on the horizon. The next, a wall of brown-orange haze appeared in the south, rolling toward the habitat like a slow-motion tsunami. ARIA's sensors tracked its approach with clinical precision:*
*"Global dust storm detected. Projected duration: 40-60 days. Solar panel efficiency will be reduced to approximately 10% of normal output."*
*Forty to sixty days. Seven weeks trapped inside while the storm scoured the landscape, blocking the sunlight that powered half their systems. Seven weeks of rationing, of darkness, of slowly depleting reserves.*
*"Power status," Commander Chen ordered, her voice steady despite the shadows under her eyes.*
*"Current power generation: 340 kilowatts. Reactor provides 200 kilowatts. Solar provides 140 kilowatts. With solar reduced to 10%, total generation drops to 214 kilowatts. Current consumption: 280 kilowatts."*
*"So we're running a deficit."*
*"Correct. Without adjustments, battery reserves will be depleted in approximately 72 hours."*
*The crew assembled in the common area, faces grim. They'd trained for this scenario—dust storms were a known hazard, something they'd discussed in mission briefings years ago. But knowing something might happen and watching it happen were different things entirely.*
*"We need to cut consumption by at least 70 kilowatts," Marcus said, already running calculations on his tablet. "That means choices. Hard ones."*
*"What are our options?" Thea asked.*
*"Temperature first. We're currently maintaining 20 degrees Celsius throughout the habitat. Drop that to 12, we save 35 kilowatts."*
*"Twelve degrees?" Sophie's voice was small. "That's... that's cold."*
*"That's survivable. Uncomfortable, but survivable." Marcus continued scrolling. "Lighting next. Full illumination is 15 kilowatts. Drop to emergency lighting—six hours of full power per day, rest on minimal—we save another 10 kilowatts."*
*"What about cultivation?" Cassian asked, already knowing he wouldn't like the answer.*
*"That's the hard part. The cultivation facility uses 40 kilowatts for temperature control, lighting, and nutrient circulation. We can reduce that to 25 if we scale back operations, but that means slower growth, lower yield. We might lose some of the organism cultures entirely."*
*The room fell silent. Lower yield meant less Areserine. Less Areserine meant fewer patients treated on Earth. Every kilowatt they saved here translated to deaths they couldn't prevent there.*
*"Do it," Cassian said finally. "Scale back cultivation. Keep the core cultures alive, let the secondary ones go dormant. We can rebuild after the storm passes."*
*"And if we can't rebuild?"*
*"Then we deal with that when it happens. Right now, we need to focus on surviving the next seven weeks."*
*The storm hit full force that night. Wind speeds climbed to 100 kilometers per hour, driving dust against the habitat walls with a constant hissing roar. The solar panels went dark, their surfaces buried under accumulating particles. The temperature dropped as the heating system struggled with reduced power.*
*Twelve degrees Celsius wasn't cold by Martian standards—outside, it was negative sixty—but inside, with the recycled air and the metal walls conducting heat away from exposed skin, it felt like winter. Crew members bundled in thermal layers, huddled together for warmth, moved through their duties with the mechanical precision of people who couldn't afford to stop and think about how miserable they were.*
*By Week 4, the fights started.*
*"I can't tell what time it is anymore," Sophie confessed during what was supposed to be a morning briefing. Her eyes were red-rimmed, her hair unwashed. "I look at the clock and the numbers don't mean anything. Is it 0800 or 2000? Does it matter?"*
*"It matters." Thea's voice was firm but gentle. "Structure is how we survive this. Wake times, work times, sleep times—they keep us human."*
*"I don't feel human." Sophie's voice cracked. "I feel like a thing. A machine that keeps running because no one's turned it off."*
*"I can't take this anymore." Sophie was crying, her breath misting in the cold air. "The darkness, the cold, the walls closing in—I can't—"*
*"You can." Thea's voice was firm but gentle. "You are taking it. Every minute you keep going is proof that you can."*
*"It doesn't feel like living. It feels like waiting to die."*
*"Sometimes that's all surviving is. Waiting. Enduring. Holding on until something changes." Thea took Sophie's hands in hers, chafing warmth into the cold fingers. "The storm will end. The sun will come back. We just have to outlast it."*
*The cold made everything harder. They wore their thermal layers constantly now—the insulated jumpsuits designed for EVA work, the fleece-lined boots, the gloves with heating elements that had stopped working in Week 2. Condensation formed on every surface, freezing into thin ice sheets that had to be scraped away before they damaged equipment.*
*Meals became perfunctory. The heating unit worked, but barely—warming their reconstituted food to lukewarm at best. They ate quickly, standing, trying to finish before the cold could steal what little heat remained.*
*Sophie had been talking quietly — describing her grandmother's kitchen in Lyon, the smell of it, how specific it was, the thing she missed most out of everything she'd left behind.*
*Kenji was running a diagnostic on the heating panel at the back of the common area. He didn't turn around. "You know that memory's already degraded," directed at the panel. "Olfactory recall deteriorates faster than any other sense without reinforcement. What you're describing isn't your grandmother's kitchen. It's your brain's increasingly inaccurate reconstruction of it." He closed the panel and picked up his kit. "Just something to keep in mind."*
*He walked out without looking at her once.*
*Sophie watched the space he'd vacated. When she spoke her voice was very quiet. "Are you genuinely this awful, or is it something you work at?"*
*His voice came back from the corridor, already moving away. "Most people just prefer comfortable lies."*
The room was silent.
*"You know what I miss?" Dmitri said one night, breaking his characteristic silence. "Hot soup. Real soup, from a pot, so hot it burns your tongue. My grandmother used to make borscht in the winter. The whole apartment smelled like beets and beef. You could feel the steam on your face from across the room."*
*"I miss coffee," Marcus added. "Real coffee. Not this reconstituted powder that tastes like dirt."*
*"I miss warmth," Elena said. "Just... being warm. Not thinking about it. Not fighting for it every second of every day."*
*They went around the circle, sharing memories of heat and comfort, building a collective warmth out of words because they had nothing else. It became a ritual—every evening, they would gather and remember what they'd left behind. The memories hurt, but they also helped.*
*In Week 4, the fights started.*
*The first one was over food—Jackson Moore had been hoarding rations, trading extras for favors from crew members too hungry to refuse. When Daniel Kim discovered the cache hidden in an equipment locker, he confronted Jackson in the common area.*
*"You've been stealing from us." Daniel's voice was quiet, dangerous. "While we ration every calorie, while Sophie's so weak she can barely stand, you've been hoarding."*
*"It's not stealing if I earned it." Jackson's smile was sharp, unrepentant. "People trade me things. I provide services. That's economics."*
*"That's exploitation." Daniel took a step forward. "You're a parasite, Moore. You've always been a parasite. And I'm done watching you feed off our suffering."*
*What happened next was fast and ugly. Daniel threw the first punch—a wild swing that caught Jackson across the jaw. Jackson retaliated, and suddenly they were on the ground, grappling, fists and elbows flying while the crew scrambled to separate them.*
*Marcus finally pulled them apart, his massive frame absorbing the blows meant for others. Daniel had a split lip; Jackson had a swelling eye. Both were breathing hard, hatred burning between them.*
*"This stops now." Commander Chen's voice cut through the chaos. She looked terrible—gray-faced, trembling, barely holding herself together—but her authority was still there, buried under the exhaustion. "We don't have the luxury of fighting each other. Every ounce of energy we spend on this bullshit is energy we don't have for surviving."*
*"Tell that to him," Daniel spat. "Tell the parasite who's been—"*
*"I'm telling both of you. Jackson, the hoarding ends. Everything you've stockpiled gets redistributed. Daniel, you don't get to assault crew members, no matter how much they deserve it. Am I clear?"*
*Neither answered, but they stopped fighting. That was enough for now.*
*In Week 5, the reactor hiccupped.*
*ARIA's alert woke the crew at 0312 hours: "Warning. Reactor cooling system malfunction. Power output reduced to 120 kilowatts. Battery backup engaging."*
*Marcus was in the reactor bay within minutes, but the news was bad. Dust had infiltrated the cooling system through a seal that should have been airtight. The reactor was overheating, threatening automatic shutdown. If it went offline completely, they'd be running on battery reserves—maybe six hours of power before everything went dark.*
*"I can fix it," Marcus said, his breath fogging in the frigid air. "But I need to work on it while it's running. If I shut it down to repair, we might not be able to restart it."*
*"How dangerous is that?"*
*"Radiation exposure, mostly. The shielding is designed for normal operations, not hands-on maintenance. I'll absorb maybe 200 millisieverts over the repair time."*
*"That's—"*
*"That's acceptable." Marcus was already pulling on his work gloves. "Hassan gave his life for the oxygen generator. I can take a little radiation to keep the lights on."*
*He worked for six hours in the confined space of the reactor bay, making adjustments by feel and intuition while ARIA guided him through procedures that had never been designed for field conditions. The crew waited in cold darkness, rationing their battery power, listening to the wind howl outside and praying that Marcus could work a miracle.*
*At 0947, the reactor stabilized.*
*"Power output nominal," ARIA announced. "Cooling system repaired. Radiation exposure for Marcus Okafor: 187 millisieverts."*
*Marcus emerged from the reactor bay gray-faced and shaking, but alive. The crew gathered around him—not cheering, exactly, but radiating a collective relief that felt almost like warmth.*
*"Don't ever do that again," Amara said, running a scanner over him.*
*"Don't ever make me have to." Marcus managed a weak smile. "How much longer on this storm?"*
*"ARIA projects another eighteen days."*
*"Eighteen days." Marcus closed his eyes. "I can do eighteen days."*
*They all could, as it turned out. The storm broke on Day 47, the dust settling gradually, the sun emerging like a resurrection. Dmitri led the EVA team to clean the solar panels—three days of painstaking work, brushing particles from surfaces and checking connections. By Day 52, power generation was back to normal.*
*The cultivation facility had lost 15% of its organism cultures. Areserine production would be reduced for months while they rebuilt. But they had survived. That was something.*
*That was everything.*

*He stood in the cultivation facility for a long time after assessing the damage. Fourteen tanks reduced to twelve viable cultures. Areserine yield projections revised downward by 15%. His mind ran the arithmetic automatically, the way it always did: adjusted timeline, recalculated production totals, revised Earth delivery estimate. 0.53 AU to the delivery window, assuming no further losses.*

*Thea appeared in the doorway. She took one look at him and understood that he wasn’t looking at the organisms at all.*

*“AU again?” Quietly.*

*“AU again.”*

*She came and stood beside him and didn’t say anything else. Outside, the dust had already begun to settle.*


---

*Three days after the panels were clean and power was normal, Cassian found Elena in the common area at an hour when most of the crew was sleeping. She was sitting with both hands around a cup of hot water—reconstituted ginger, from the smell of it—and she wasn't reading, wasn't working, wasn't doing anything except sitting there.*
*He almost turned around. Elena in stillness usually meant Elena conserving energy for the next fight.*
*But she looked up. And she didn't look like she wanted to fight anyone.*
*"Sit down if you want. I'm not being angry right now. Fair warning."*
*He sat across from her. Got his own cup. For a while neither of them said anything, which was its own kind of remarkable.*
*"I had a dog," Elena said eventually. "Barro. Named him that because he was this terrible brown color, like mud. Feo como barro—ugly as mud. He was the ugliest dog I'd ever seen and I loved him completely." A pause. "He used to sleep on my feet. Deadweight. I'd wake up with my feet completely numb and I didn't care."*
*Cassian thought about Emily folding origami cranes when she couldn't sleep. Tiny paper birds lined up on the windowsill of their childhood bedroom, a hundred of them by the time she was twelve.*
*"My sister made cranes. When she was anxious. She'd just—fold. For hours. She had a thousand of them by the time she was fourteen."*
*Elena looked at him. "You think about her in here?"*
*"Every time I run the yield projections."*
