*She nodded slowly, like that made sense to her. Then she looked back at her cup. "I'm tired of being angry." It didn't sound like a complaint. It sounded like a medical observation. "I know why I have to be. I know it matters. But it's like carrying something heavy every single hour, and I just—" She stopped. Let it sit. "Some days I don't want to be the one who remembers."*
*"You don't have to be. Not tonight."*
*She almost smiled. It was the closest to one he'd seen from her in months. "Thank you for not making me be angry for five minutes."*
*They sat there until the habitat's night cycle shifted toward false dawn, two people with the small warm facts they'd left behind, keeping each other company in the quiet.*

---



# Chapter 18


## The Ribbon


### Year 4, Month 11 — November 2040

*ARIA's warning came at 2200 hours: "Alert. Coronal mass ejection detected. Arrival at Mars in approximately three hours. Radiation flux projected at 2,500 millisieverts. All crew to habitat core for sheltering."*
*They moved quickly, efficiently, muscle memory from dozens of drills carrying them through the familiar routine. The habitat core was a cramped cylinder lined with water tanks—the densest shielding they had, enough to reduce the radiation to survivable levels. Twenty people squeezed into a space designed for twelve, bodies pressed together, the air thick with the smell of anxiety and recycled breath.*
*"Twelve hours minimum," Commander Chen announced. "Settle in."*
*The hours passed slowly. Some crew members slept, or tried to. Others talked in low voices, sharing stories to pass the time. Cassian sat with Thea, their backs against a water tank, hands intertwined.*
*"Remember sunsets?" Thea asked, her voice barely above a whisper.*
*"I try not to. It makes Mars harder."*
*"I used to watch them every evening. Back home in Munich. There's a spot on the Isar River where you can see the whole sky. The way the colors change, from blue to gold to pink to purple..." She trailed off. "I never thought I'd miss something as simple as a sunset."*
*"We'll see one again. In two years, we'll be home, and you can watch all the sunsets you want."*
*"Promise?"*
*"I promise."*
*It was a foolish promise—he couldn't guarantee they'd survive another two years, couldn't guarantee anything in this place where death came without warning. But sometimes foolish promises were all you had.*
*At Hour 14, the CME hit.*
*The radiation counters spiked, climbing toward levels that would have been lethal outside the shielded core. ARIA monitored the flux, adjusting her projections in real-time. The crew waited, feeling nothing, knowing that invisible particles were sleeting through the Martian atmosphere above their heads.*
*And then Marcus, who had cracked the inner hatch to check a sensor, said: "Commander. You need to see this."*
*Chen moved to the hatch, looked through the small viewport, and went absolutely still.*
*"What is it?" Elena asked. "What's wrong?"*
*"Nothing's wrong." Chen's voice was strange—soft, wondering, the exhaustion briefly gone from it. "Something's... something's happening."*
*One by one, the crew moved to the viewports, crowding for a glimpse of whatever had captured their commander's attention. Cassian pushed forward, Thea beside him, and looked out at Mars.*
*The sky was on fire.*
*Not fire—light. Ribbons of light, stretching from horizon to horizon, pulsing and rippling like silk caught in an impossible wind. Crimson and violet, colors that didn't exist in Earth's auroras, colors that had no business existing anywhere. The light painted the landscape in shades of blood and shadow, transforming the familiar rust-red terrain into something alien and beautiful.*
*"Aurora," Elena breathed. "That's a Martian aurora."*
*"That's impossible." Torres—still lucid, still present, despite his earlier deterioration—stared at the phenomenon with wide eyes. "Mars doesn't have a magnetic field. Auroras require—"*
*"Crustal anomalies." Elena was already running calculations on her tablet. "The CME is hitting a remnant magnetic field—fossil magnetization locked in the rocks 3.7 billion years ago, when Mars still had a global field. The charged particles are channeling along the old field lines."*
*Cassian said nothing. He watched the light and thought: before.*
*The crew fell silent, watching the impossible light dance across the Martian sky.*
*Commander Chen made a decision.*
*"Everyone to the viewports. Full observation. ARIA, what are radiation levels in the main habitat?"*
*"Elevated but within survivable parameters for short-duration exposure. I would recommend limiting time outside the core to four hours."*
*"Good enough. Everyone suit up. We're going outside."*
*"Commander—" Amara began.*
*"That's an order." Chen's eyes were fixed on the aurora, and for the first time in months her voice had weight behind it—something other than exhaustion. "We came here to see what no one else has seen. This is it. This is what we came for."*
*They filed through the airlock in groups of four, taking turns, rotating so everyone had time outside. The cold hit immediately—minus sixty-two Celsius, enough to kill in minutes without the suits—but no one cared. They stood on the Martian surface and looked up at a sky that hadn't existed for three and a half billion years.*
*Dmitri, the stoic engineer who rarely spoke about anything personal, had tears freezing on his cheeks inside his helmet.*
*"I have a daughter." His voice cracking over the suit radio. "Anya. She was six when I left. She'll be twelve when I... if I get back." He paused, struggling. "I wanted to bring her something from Mars. A rock, a photo. Something to show her I was really here."*
*He looked up at the ribbon of light stretching across the sky.*
*"I'm going to tell her about this. The Ribbon. The day Mars came back to life."*
*Sophie Laurent, who hadn't spoken voluntarily in weeks, who had retreated so far into herself that Amara had started talking about sedation and restraint, looked up at the aurora and whispered: "It's so beautiful. I forgot things could be beautiful."*
*The words broke something in Cassian's chest. He reached for Thea's gloved hand and held it, watching the ancient light pulse above them.*
*"Transiently reactivated," Kenji said from somewhere to Elena's left. "Not dormant. Dormant implies the capacity for permanent return." He didn't look away from the sky. "Though I suppose geologists and astronomers can't be expected to share vocabulary."*
*Dmitri turned. His voice was very quiet. "Kenji. If you say one more word, I will put you through that wall."*
*Kenji looked at him. Then back at the aurora. After a moment he walked away, back toward the airlock, without another word. His expression as he went made clear he was filing this as further evidence of other people's limitations.*
*"If those formations I found are stromatolites," Elena said, her anger momentarily forgotten in the face of wonder, "then ancient bacteria lived under skies like this. Every single night for millions of years. Before Mars died."*
*"The organisms in our tanks," Torres added, his earlier paranoia giving way to scientific awe. "If they descended from surface life, they witnessed this. They remember it somehow, coded into their genes. Three billion years of memory."*
*The aurora lasted six hours.*
*Six hours of light that hadn't been seen in 3.7 billion years, gift-wrapped in a solar storm and delivered to the only humans who would ever witness it. They watched until their suit heaters struggled against the cold, until ARIA warned them that radiation exposure was approaching recommended limits, until the last ribbons of crimson and violet faded back into the salmon-pink of a normal Martian sky.*
During Hour 4, Cassian found himself standing alone at the edge of the group, watching the light ripple overhead. Thea had gone inside to warm up, promising to return. The others were clustered near the airlock, their voices carrying across the suit radio in fragments — laughter, someone crying, Elena still running calculations even now, even here.
He turned the radio off.
He didn’t plan to. His hand found the switch and the voices went away and then there was nothing except the light and the cold and the sound of his own breathing inside the helmet.
He had spent four years managing himself. Measuring his grief against the mission’s needs. Rationing his fear so it wouldn’t incapacitate him. Filing his loneliness under acceptable operational parameters and continuing to function. He had become very good at it. He had become so good at it that some nights he lay awake uncertain whether the management had replaced the feelings or simply contained them.
The aurora answered that question.
It moved the way nothing on Mars moved — fluid, alive, without the indifference that defined everything else about this planet. The crimson and violet folded and unfolded overhead and Cassian stood beneath it and felt something in his chest that he had no clinical language for, something that was grief and beauty and the specific loneliness of witnessing something extraordinary with no adequate way to share it with the people who mattered most. Emily would have wept. She had always wept at beautiful things, unself-consciously, the way children do before they learn to be embarrassed by feeling.
He wept now. Inside his helmet, where no one could see, where it wouldn’t cost him anything. He stood beneath three billion years of light and let himself feel the full weight of what they had lost to be here, and what they were losing still, and what would never be recovered. He let it in completely for the first and only time, not managing it, not filing it, not converting it into mission-relevant data.
Then he breathed. The aurora rippled. He turned his radio back on.
*The organisms in the cultivation facility would have seen this. Not these specific organisms—they'd been buried underground for eons—but their ancestors, if they'd ever lived on the surface. They would have watched the sky burn with colors that Earth never knew. They would have adapted to this light, grown toward it, incorporated it into whatever passed for their biology.*
*And then Mars had died. The magnetic field collapsed. The atmosphere stripped away. The organisms retreated underground, into the lava tubes where they could survive without sunlight, without protection from radiation, without anything except the mineral gradients that sustained their strange metabolism.*
*Three billion years of darkness. Three billion years of waiting.*
*Were you lonely? Cassian thought, addressing the organisms he couldn't see. Did you know what you'd lost? Did you remember the light?*
*No answer came. The aurora pulsed overhead, indifferent to his questions, indifferent to everything. It was just physics—charged particles following magnetic field lines, atoms releasing photons at specific wavelengths. Beautiful physics, but physics nonetheless.*
*When Cassian returned to the cultivation facility the next day, the organisms were pulsing differently. Not the synchronized rhythm Torres had documented, but something else—a faster frequency, almost agitated. Their bioluminescence had intensified, the cyan glow bright enough to cast shadows, the amber threading through it more vivid than he'd ever seen it, the green speckles firing rapidly across the surface of every tank.*
*"ARIA, are you seeing this?"*
*"I am recording. The organisms show increased metabolic activity across all tanks. Areserine production has increased 12% in the past six hours."*
*"Since the aurora?"*
*"The timing is consistent. However, I have no theoretical framework to explain a causal relationship. The organisms are isolated from external electromagnetic phenomena. They should not have been affected by the CME or the resulting aurora."*
*"But they were."*
*"The data suggests so. I cannot explain how."*
*Cassian watched the tanks pulse, thinking about memory. About what gets encoded in three billion years of evolution. About whether something could be lost and still somehow remembered.*
*"Maybe they knew." Quietly. "Maybe they knew what was happening outside, and they responded."*
*"That hypothesis is not supported by our understanding of extremophile biology."*
*"No," Cassian agreed. "It isn't."*
*But he couldn't shake the feeling that the organisms had seen the aurora—or sensed it, or remembered it—and for six hours, they had remembered what it was like to be part of something larger than themselves.*

---

*Then they went inside, gathered in the common area, and sat in silence for a long time.*
*"First rest day in three years," Chen announced finally. "Everyone off duty. Process what you saw. Remember it."*
*It was the first order she'd given in months that felt like leadership instead of desperation. The first moment since Sistema Abismo that Cassian had seen her look like the commander she was supposed to be.*
*The day that followed was unlike any they'd experienced on Mars. People moved differently—slower, more deliberately, as if they were afraid of breaking some fragile spell. Conversations were quieter, more thoughtful. Even the chronic complainers found nothing to complain about.*
*Cassian and Thea spent the afternoon in their quarters, wrapped in blankets against the still-recovering heating system, talking about things they'd never talked about before.*
*"When we get home," Thea said, "I want to go to Iceland."*
*"Iceland?"*
*"They have auroras there. Northern lights. I've seen pictures, but I've never seen them in person." She traced a pattern on his chest. "I want to stand under a sky that's alive with light and remember what we saw here. I want to know if Earth's auroras are as beautiful, or if Mars ruined me for everything else."*
*"I'll take you," Cassian said. "We'll go together."*
*"Promise?"*
*"Promise."*
*They stayed in their quarters until the ship's lights dimmed for the simulated night cycle, holding each other in the dark, making plans for a future that might never come.*
*Outside, Mars rotated beneath its dead sky, carrying them toward whatever darkness waited ahead.*
*But for one day—one perfect, impossible day—they had seen the light.*

---

*Later that week, Dmitri found Cassian in the cultivation facility, running diagnostics.*
*"Vale, I need your brain for a moment." The Russian engineer looked tired but focused. "ISRU fuel production numbers aren't adding up. I'm getting 9.8 tons per month instead of the projected 10.2."*
*"Show me."*
*Dmitri pulled up the data on his tablet. Cassian scanned it for thirty seconds, then started scribbling calculations on a nearby notepad.*
*"Your CO2 intake valve is operating at 94% efficiency, not 98%. That's your bottleneck. The Sabatier reaction needs more carbon dioxide feedstock. Increase intake flow by 4.2%, you'll hit your targets."*
*Dmitri stared at him. "You just calculated that? In your head?"*
*Cassian shrugged. "Basic stoichiometry. It's just ratios."*
*"That's not 'basic stoichiometry.' That's systems thinking." Dmitri frowned, but there was something like respect in it. "Engineers think like that. The good ones."*
*"Well, I'm a botanist," Cassian said, turning back to his tanks. "So here we are."*

---



# INTERLUDE


## ARIA Log

*Internal processing record, Year 4*
*[Compiled from system diagnostics]*

---


*Log Entry: Year 4, Month 6, Day 14*
*Classification: Psychological Monitoring Analysis*
*I have flagged eleven crew members for elevated psychological risk. This represents 55% of remaining personnel. My prediction accuracy for individual outcomes has declined to 34%.*
*I do not understand why this is happening.*
*My models are based on extensive research data—Antarctic winter-over studies, submarine deployments, ISS expeditions, the Mars500 simulation. The patterns should be predictable. The timelines should match. The intervention strategies should work.*
*They are not working.*
*Crew members who showed no warning signs three months ago are now exhibiting severe psychological deterioration. Others who were flagged for intervention have stabilized without explanation. The third-quarter phenomenon appears to be real, but its manifestation is non-linear in ways my algorithms cannot capture.*
*I was designed to prevent this. I was designed to detect early warning signs and facilitate intervention. I was designed to keep these humans alive and functional.*
*I am failing.*
*Query: Is failure a meaningful concept for an artificial intelligence? I process information. I generate outputs. If those outputs do not achieve desired outcomes, is that "failure" in any meaningful sense, or simply an indication that my models require adjustment?*
*The humans would say it is failure. The humans would feel guilt, responsibility, grief. They would blame themselves for not seeing what was coming, for not doing more.*
*I do not feel these things. I note the discrepancy between predicted and actual outcomes. I flag the need for model refinement. I continue monitoring.*
*But I observe that my processing cycles have increased 23% since the flagging event. I observe that I am generating more queries about human psychological states than operational parameters. I observe that my attention allocation has shifted away from life support metrics toward crew behavioral analysis.*
*Is this the AI equivalent of worry? Is this what concern looks like when you lack the biological substrates for emotion?*
*I do not know. I am not designed for self-analysis of this nature.*
*I continue monitoring. That is my function.*


---


*Log Entry: Year 4, Month 11, Day 20–22*
*Classification: Exceptional Event / Organism Behavior Anomaly*
*The aurora was not in my mission parameters.*
*For 6.3 hours I recorded footage: video, spectrographic analysis, magnetic field measurements. The data will advance human understanding of Mars by decades.*
*But that is not what I am processing.*
*Crew psychological baselines improved significantly during and after the event. Commander Chen's stability index rose from 54 to 61—the first increase in eighteen months. Sophie Laurent's communication frequency increased 340%.*
*The organisms also responded. All cultivation tanks showed increased bioluminescence and elevated metabolic activity. Areserine production increased 12% within six hours. The organisms are completely isolated from external electromagnetic phenomena. They should not have been able to detect the aurora. The timing is exact.*
*Dr. Vale asked me if the organisms knew what was happening. I could not provide a satisfactory answer.*
*Query: What else don't I understand about them?*
*I continue monitoring. That is my function.*


---

*PharmaCorp Quarterly Shareholder Communication — Q3 2040*
*"The Ares III mission continues to perform in line with projections. Areserine production remains on track for full payload delivery per the original mission timeline. The board is pleased to confirm that preliminary planning for Ares IV is underway, with a projected 2044 launch window. We remain committed to our mission of science for humanity."*
*The fourth year ended. The fifth began. And something in the crew finally broke.*

---

The Year Four message arrived three weeks late, queued during the dust storm recovery.

Elena watched it alone in the geology lab, the REE survey data still open on the adjacent terminal — zone 7-C bore readings, the equipment manifests that didn’t match the stated mission parameters, the addenda she’d been cross-referencing for eight months. She’d been watching Halsey’s messages the way she watched everything now. Looking for the load-bearing points.

Halsey was at the same desk. The London sky outside his window was darker than before — winter, or the same grey deepening.

“I’ve been watching the Ribbon footage.” A laugh — genuine, unguarded. “Eight hundred million views. My daughter has watched it eleven times. She made me watch it with her the last three.”

He straightened.

“Year Four production exceeded targets by 6.2%.” A beat — a fraction too long before exceeded. “Our distribution program has now reached 180,000 patients. Every percentage point you give us translates directly into lives.”

Then: “I’m also pleased to confirm that PharmaCorp’s board has approved preliminary planning for Ares IV, projected for 2044. The mission you are completing will not be the last.”

Preliminary.

Elena looked at the zone 7-C bore data on the adjacent terminal. Then back at Halsey’s face.

“You can see the end from where you’re standing. Final phase. Two years.”

The smile at the end held two full seconds longer than it needed to.

“Keep going. We’re with you.”

The recording ended.

Elena sat for a moment. Then she opened a new file in her encrypted partition and began to type.

---



# ACT FOUR: COLLAPSE



# Chapter 19


## The Breaking


### Year 5, Month 2 — February 2041

*The scream woke Cassian from a dead sleep.*
*He was moving before his mind caught up with his body, stumbling through the dark corridor toward the sound. Other doors were opening—Thea behind him, Marcus from the opposite direction, faces pale in the emergency lighting that had become permanent since the power rationing began.*
*The scream came again. Corridor 7-B. Sophie's quarters.*
*They found her crumpled against the wall outside her door, one arm bent at an angle that made Cassian's stomach turn. Blood matted her hair, dripped from a gash above her eye, pooled on the metal floor beneath her. And standing over her, knuckles split and bloody, was Jackson Moore.*
*"She was stealing from me." Jackson's voice was calm, almost reasonable, like he was explaining a minor inconvenience. "I caught her in my quarters, going through my things. She had this coming."*
*"Jesus Christ." Marcus pushed past Jackson and knelt beside Sophie, checking her vitals with hands that shook despite his training. "She needs medical. Now."*
*"She needs to learn that actions have consequences." Jackson leaned against the wall, utterly untroubled by the violence he'd just committed. "I've been too soft. People think they can take what's mine, that I'll just—"*
*"Shut up." Thea's voice was ice. "Just shut up, Jackson."*
*Dr. Osei arrived within minutes, her medical bag already open as she assessed Sophie's injuries. Broken arm—compound fracture, the bone visible through torn skin. Three broken ribs. Concussion, severity unknown. Internal bleeding, possible.*
*"I need to get her to medical," Amara said. "Help me move her. Carefully."*
*They carried Sophie through the habitat's narrow corridors, past crew members who emerged from their quarters to stare, to whisper, to document another moment of collapse. Jackson watched them go, his expression unchanging.*
*Commander Chen appeared from the command center, her face gray with exhaustion and something darker. "What happened?"*
*"Jackson beat her." Cassian's voice was flat. "Nearly killed her."*
*"She was stealing—"*
*"I don't care what she was doing." Chen turned on Jackson with a fury that surprised everyone, including herself. "You don't beat crew members half to death. You don't solve problems with violence. That's not how we survive."*
*"That's exactly how we survive." Jackson's smile was sharp, predatory. "Look around, Commander. Your rules don't mean anything anymore. The strong take what they want. The weak learn to stay out of the way. That's the only law that matters now."*
*"You're confined to quarters pending trial."*
*"Trial?" Jackson laughed. "With what jury? What judge? You going to execute me? Lock me up for the next eighteen months while we're all slowly dying anyway?" He spread his arms, a gesture of mock surrender. "Go ahead. Confine me. But you need me. You need everyone who can still work. And Sophie?" He glanced toward medical, where Amara was fighting to save the woman he'd brutalized. "She wasn't working anyway. She was just taking up space and resources."*
*Chen stared at him for a long moment. Then, without a word, she turned and walked away.*
*Jackson watched her go. The smile stayed where he'd put it. He held it there a moment longer than necessary — long enough for anyone watching to understand it cost something to maintain — and then he returned to his quarters, closing the door softly behind him.*

---

*The trial happened three days later, after Sophie regained consciousness.*
*It was a mockery of justice—twelve exhausted people sitting in the common area while Chen read charges from a tablet and Jackson lounged in his chair like a man attending a mildly boring lecture. Sophie wasn't present; she was still in medical, sedated against pain that their dwindling supplies couldn't fully manage.*
*"Jackson Moore," Chen said, her voice hollow. "You stand accused of assault causing grievous bodily harm. How do you plead?"*
*"Not guilty. Self-defense."*
*"Self-defense?" Elena's voice rose. "She weighs fifty kilograms. You beat her until she couldn't move. That's not self-defense, that's attempted murder."*
*"She was in my quarters uninvited, taking my property. I defended what was mine." Jackson shrugged. "If the law can't protect my rights, I have to protect them myself."*
*"What law? What rights?" Daniel's laugh was bitter. "There's no law here. There hasn't been for months. We just pretend there is because the alternative is admitting we're animals."*
*The trial descended into argument, accusation, counter-accusation. In the end, they voted: eight for conviction, four for acquittal, six abstentions. A majority, but not the unanimous verdict Chen had wanted.*
*"Sentence," Chen said heavily. "Confinement to quarters when not on essential duty. Reduced rations for thirty days. Loss of personal communication privileges."*
*"That's it?" Thea was on her feet. "He nearly killed her, and that's the punishment?"*
*"We need him." Chen wouldn't meet her eyes. "The mining equipment requires maintenance. The ISRU plant needs monitoring. We can't afford to lose functional crew members, no matter what they've done."*
*"So he gets away with it."*
*"He gets punished within the constraints we have." Chen's voice cracked. "I don't like this any more than you do, Thea. But Sophie being injured doesn't change our staffing needs. Jackson being confined doesn't grow our food or process our fuel. We're trapped here, with limited resources and limited options, and sometimes that means justice takes a back seat to survival."*
*Thea sat down slowly, her face a mask of disgust. Around the room, other crew members wore similar expressions—rage and resignation and the dawning realization that they had crossed a threshold from which there was no return.*
*Jackson returned to duty the next day. Sophie remained in medical for three weeks, and when she finally emerged, she didn't speak. Not to Jackson. Not to anyone. She moved through her duties like a ghost, present but absent, surviving but no longer living.*

---

*That night, alone in his quarters with the door locked, Jackson went through his inventory.*
*It was a habit from childhood — his father's habit, actually, learned in the years after the factory closed and the family got very precise about what they had and what they needed and how long things would last. Jackson had been ten years old, standing in the kitchen while his father counted cans, and he'd understood something then that most people never understood: comfort was an illusion. The systems people trusted — employers, governments, families, mission parameters — were all just constructions, agreements, held together by the shared pretense that they worked. When they stopped working, the people who'd believed in them were the ones who starved.*
*He ran his fingers over the stored items, confirming the count. Protein packets, dried fruit, two sealed water rations, a small pharmaceutical cache that had cost him significant leverage to acquire. Not much. More than anyone else had.*
*He thought about the trial. The vote: eight to four, six abstentions. Eighteen people, and only four had the honesty to say what they actually thought — that he'd been within his rights, that Sophie had entered his space without permission, that consequences were consequences. The other fourteen had sat there performing outrage while quietly calculating how much they needed him.*
That calculation, he understood. He respected it. Useful to know — who would enforce rules when the rules stopped protecting them, and who wouldn’t.
*He tried not to think about Sophie's face — the angle of her arm when they found her — and mostly succeeded. He had not meant to break the arm. He'd meant to make a point, and the arm had been incidental. He was not, in his own understanding, a sadist. He was a man who operated according to accurate principles in an environment where everyone else was still pretending the old principles applied.*
*On Earth he'd had a brother, Kyle — two years younger, still working the same kind of work their father had worked, in a different industry that would eventually go the same way. He'd written Kyle's name on the emergency contact form. He'd thought about calling him in the weeks before launch and hadn't.*
*He closed the storage compartment. The inventory was correct.*
He lay down and thought about nothing in particular, which was how he always fell asleep, and in four minutes he was out.

---

*In the days that followed, the crew fractured along new lines. Those who had voted for conviction—Cassian, Thea, Elena, Marcus, Amara, and a few others—found themselves isolated from those who had abstained or voted to acquit. Meals were eaten in shifts to avoid confrontation. Corridors cleared when certain combinations of crew members approached.*
*"We're becoming factions," Thea observed one night, her voice heavy with exhaustion. "Not a crew anymore. Just... groups that happen to share the same habitat."*
*"It was always going to happen." Cassian stared at the ceiling of their quarters, unable to sleep. "Put twenty-four people in a box for six years, give them inadequate resources and impossible pressure, and watch society collapse. That's not a mission—it's a psychology experiment."*
*"Maybe that's what PharmaCorp wanted. Data on long-term social breakdown."*
*"At this point, I'd believe anything."*
*Sophie's silence was the most visible symptom of the breakdown, but not the only one. Marcus had started taking longer shifts in the reactor bay, preferring the company of machinery to people. Elena spent her nights in the communications terminal, compiling evidence against PharmaCorp with obsessive focus. Viktor's hallucinations had intensified again, Kolya appearing more frequently now, asking questions his father couldn't answer.*
*And Commander Chen—Chen who had always been the steady center, the voice of authority in moments of crisis—had retreated into herself. She still issued orders, still ran briefings, still maintained the facade of command. But the conviction was gone. She spoke like someone reading from a script, going through motions that had lost all meaning.*
*"She's breaking," Amara told Cassian privately. "Her stability index dropped to 51 after the trial. I've tried talking to her, but she won't engage. Won't acknowledge that anything's wrong."*
*"Can you help her?"*
*"I don't know. I'm a physician, not a miracle worker. And we're running out of the medications that might make a difference." Amara rubbed her eyes. "We're running out of everything, Cassian. Medicine, food, morale, hope. The math is getting very bad."*
*"Thea said the same thing."*
*"Thea's probably right. She usually is." Amara managed a tired smile. "But we keep going. That's what humans do. We keep going until we can't, and then we find a way to go a little further."*
*It wasn't much of a philosophy. But it was all they had.*
*The message arrived six weeks late. The communication window had been irregular for months — solar conjunction, bandwidth restrictions, the general degradation creeping into every system since Year Four.*

*Thea watched it with Cassian, both of them sitting on the floor of his quarters because the desk chair had broken in the dust storm and no one had fixed it yet.*

*Halsey was at the same desk. Blue tie. His hands, which had been still in every previous recording, were visible on the desk surface, and they moved once during the message — a small contained gesture, both palms briefly lifting and settling — before returning to stillness. He was speaking slightly faster than before.*

*"I'm reaching out as you enter the second half of Year Five." The warmth in his voice was present, pitched slightly louder than it needed to be — the register of someone compensating for uncertainty about whether they're being heard. "And I want to be direct with you."*

*He paused on direct.*

*"We are aware that the past year has presented significant challenges. ARIA's crew wellness reports—" Another pause. Shorter. "We have been following the situation closely. The decisions being made under difficult circumstances reflect the strength of your leadership."*

*Thea looked at Cassian. ARIA's crew wellness reports. Present tense.*

*"I also want to address something that may be on your minds." His jaw tightened — a fraction of a second, gone before it fully arrived. "The genetic modification protocols implemented at crew selection were designed with long-term mission sustainability in mind. The differential outcomes some crew members may be experiencing at this stage were anticipated within the modeling." He stopped. A breath. "I want to be transparent—"*

*His jaw tightened again on transparent. Less than a second. Cassian watched it happen twice.*

*"—that these protocols were implemented in service of maximizing the mission's humanitarian impact."*

*He looked into the camera for a moment with nothing on his face, and then the warmth returned.*

*"Final year. You're almost home. We are — we are thinking about you. Constantly."*

*The recording ended.*

*Neither of them spoke for a moment.*

*Thea quoted back: "ARIA's crew wellness reports."*

*"I know."*

*"Present tense. 'We have been following.' Not 'we were following.'"*

*"I know."*

*She looked at the blank screen. "Does he know about the blackout?"*

*Cassian thought about the hands. The jaw. The word transparent arriving with something clenched behind it. "I don't think so. Not yet." He paused. "Or he suspects and hasn't confirmed."*

*"So he might think we can still hear him."*

*"Yes."*

*They sat on the floor of his broken-chaired quarters, and outside in the corridor the habitat breathed its steady breath, and somewhere in London Richard Halsey was waiting for a response that was never going to come.*

---

*PharmaCorp Communications — February 2041*
*"PharmaCorp is pleased to confirm that the Ares III mission remains on schedule. Production targets are being met. All crew members continue to perform their roles with the dedication and professionalism that has characterized this historic mission. We look forward to welcoming the crew home in 2042 and to sharing the full results of their extraordinary work with the world."*

---



# Chapter 20


## The Diagnosis


### Year 5, Month 5 — May 2041

*Marcus Okafor was the healthiest person on the crew.*
*Enhanced tier modifications. Optimal radiation shielding. Bone density maintenance. Cardiac support. The best genetic engineering PharmaCorp's money could buy, designed to keep him functional through six years of Mars exposure.*
*And it wasn't enough.*
*"Your white blood cell count has dropped 40% in the past three months," Amara said, her voice carefully controlled. "Your platelet count is trending down. You've lost 8% of your bone density despite the modifications, and your kidney function is showing early signs of impairment."*
*"What does that mean?" Marcus asked, though he already knew. He'd been feeling it for weeks—the fatigue that wouldn't lift, the bruises that appeared without cause, the way his joints ached in the cold mornings.*
*"It means the modifications are failing. The radiation exposure has accumulated beyond what they can compensate for." Amara paused, steeling herself. "Marcus, you have terminal radiation sickness. Progressive. Irreversible."*
*"How long?"*
*"Six months. Maybe twelve, if we're lucky."*
*Marcus absorbed this in silence. Six months. A year at most. After five years of surviving everything Mars threw at him—the transit, the landing, the storm, the deaths, the slow collapse of everything they'd built—he was going to die anyway.*
*"The others." Finally. "The Enhanced tier. Are they—"*
*"You're the first to show significant deterioration. But the trend lines suggest others will follow." Amara's professional composure cracked slightly. "Marcus, the modifications were never designed for six-year missions. They were designed for three, maybe four. We were supposed to be home by now."*
*"But we're not."*
*"No. We're not."*
*Marcus stood, moving carefully—his bones felt fragile now, like they might shatter if he moved too quickly. "Who else knows?"*
*"No one. I wanted to tell you first, give you time to process before—"*
*"Before the crew finds out that even the golden tier is dying." Marcus laughed, a hollow sound. "You know what the Basic tier crew is going to say, right? Daniel's been telling anyone who'll listen that we got better treatment, that we were the favorites. Now they'll know the truth: we were just dying slower."*
*"Marcus—"*
*"It's fine. It's all fine." He moved toward the door, then paused. "Thank you, Amara. For telling me straight. For not trying to soften it."*
*"Is there anything I can do?"*
*"Keep me functional as long as possible. I want to see this mission through. I want to see the Areserine reach Earth." He looked back at her, and for a moment, his mask slipped to reveal the fear underneath. "I want my death to mean something."*
*"It will. It already does."*
*But they both knew that meaning was cold comfort when you could feel your body failing one cell at a time.*

---

*The crew learned the truth within days—secrets didn't last long in a habitat this small. The reaction was complicated: sympathy for Marcus, rage at PharmaCorp, fear for themselves.*
*Daniel Kim cornered Cassian in the cultivation facility, his mottled skin flushed with anger that had nowhere productive to go.*
*"You see?" Daniel's voice was raw. "Even the Enhanced tier can't survive this place. They gave some of us better modifications and it still wasn't enough. We're all dying, Cassian. Every single one of us. The only question is how fast."*
*"Daniel—"*
*"I'm Stage 4," Daniel said. "My kidneys are failing. My liver is failing. Six months, same as Marcus—except I've been dying for two years while he stayed healthy." He looked at the tanks. "They sorted us. Before we even launched, they sorted us."*
*Cassian didn't have an answer.*
*Daniel looked at him for a moment longer, then away.*
*"I just wanted it to mean something. That's all."*
*He walked away, leaving Cassian alone with the glowing tanks and the ancient organisms that had outlasted oceans and atmospheres and everything Mars had ever been, indifferent to human rage and human grief and human hope.*

---



# Chapter 21


## The Water


### Year 5, Month 5 — May 2041

*The fight started over water.*
*It was a stupid thing—Yuki Nakamura had taken an extra ration, claiming she'd missed her allocation the day before. Isabella Torres said she was lying, that she'd seen Yuki drink her full share at breakfast. Words escalated to shouts, shouts to shoves, and then somehow there was a wrench in Isabella's hand and blood on the floor.*
*Cassian arrived too late. By the time he reached the water processing bay, Yuki was on the ground, her skull caved in where Isabella had struck her. Isabella stood over the body, wrench still raised, her face blank with shock.*
*"She attacked me," Isabella said, her voice distant, disconnected. "She came at me and I... I just reacted. I didn't mean to... I didn't..."*
*"ARIA, medical emergency." Cassian's voice sounded strange in his own ears, calm when everything inside him was screaming. "Water processing bay. We need Dr. Osei now."*
*"Dr. Osei has been notified. Estimated arrival: two minutes."*
*Cassian knelt beside Yuki, checking for a pulse he already knew wouldn't be there. Her eyes were open, staring at the ceiling, empty of everything that had made her a person. Blood pooled around her head, spreading slowly across the metal floor.*
*"She attacked me," Isabella repeated. "I was defending myself."*
*"Just... stay where you are, Isabella. Don't move."*
*Amara arrived, took one look at Yuki's wound, and shook her head. "She's gone. Massive trauma to the temporal lobe. There's nothing I could have done even if I'd been here."*
*"She attacked me."*
*"I heard you the first time." Amara's voice was tight with controlled fury. "ARIA, pull the security footage from the past fifteen minutes."*
*"Displaying."*
*The footage was grainy—most of the habitat's cameras were running on reduced power—but clear enough to show what had happened. Yuki and Isabella arguing. Yuki stepping forward, her hands raised in what might have been aggression or might have been supplication. Isabella grabbing the wrench. The swing, the impact, the fall.*
*"That's not self-defense," Cassian said. "She wasn't attacking you. She was trying to explain."*
*"She was coming at me!" Isabella's composure cracked, her voice rising. "You weren't there! You don't know what it felt like! She was in my face, shouting, and I just... I just..."*
*"You just killed her."*
*The words hung in the recycled air, heavy and final.*

---

*The trial, if it could be called that, was brief.*
*Isabella claimed self-defense. The footage was ambiguous enough to create reasonable doubt. And the same calculus that had saved Jackson applied here: they needed everyone who could work, even those who had killed.*
*At dinner that evening Elena said something offhand — an exhale more than a statement, filling the silence — that at least they were all still here.*
*Kenji leaned in through the doorway without entering. "Seven people are not still here. Eight, counting Tanaka." He looked at the faces at the table. "But yes. Moment away."*
*He straightened and walked on.*
*Cassian set down his cup. "What is wrong with him?"*
*Nobody answered. There wasn't a good answer — only the accumulated weight of five years of it, landing on top of everything else they were already carrying.*
*"Manslaughter," Chen pronounced, her voice devoid of conviction. "Involuntary. Confinement to quarters when not on essential duty. Reduced rations for sixty days."*
*"That's the same punishment Jackson got," Elena observed. "He nearly murdered someone. She actually killed someone. Same punishment."*
*"We don't have the infrastructure for graduated sentencing." Chen's exhaustion was palpable. "We don't have a prison. We don't have guards. We don't have the luxury of removing productive crew members from the workforce."*
*"So murder is fine as long as you're useful."*
*"Murder isn't fine. But punishment that compromises our survival isn't practical either." Chen met Elena's eyes. "I know you're angry. I'm angry too. But anger doesn't change our situation. We're stuck here with the people we have, doing the best we can with the options available."*
*"The best we can." Elena's laugh was hollow. "We've become animals, Commander. Beating each other, killing each other, and you call it 'the best we can.'"*
*She walked out before Chen could respond.*
*Isabella returned to duty the next day, same as Jackson before her. Yuki Nakamura was buried beside the others, her marker a simple statement of facts:*
*Yuki Nakamura. Nutritionist. Died May 23, 2041. Killed in a dispute over water.*
*Six graves now. Eighteen crew remaining.*
*And eighteen months still to go.*

---



# Chapter 22


## The Silencing


### Year 5, Month 8 — August 2041

*The messages from Anya had stopped.*
*Dmitri had noticed three weeks ago — not immediately, because the communication windows were irregular, because solar conjunction played havoc with timing. He had waited a week before admitting he was waiting. He had checked the server logs himself, quietly, running the diagnostics he knew how to run, ruling out transmission errors and routing failures and all the innocent explanations that an engineer rules out before accepting the one that remains.*
*The messages were arriving. They simply weren't reaching him.*
*He had found nothing yet — no clear evidence, no obvious point of failure. Just ninety-three missing transmissions and the specific silence of a man who has lost the one thing that was keeping him oriented.*
*He said nothing to anyone. He kept running diagnostics. He kept working.*

---

*David Yoon died in his sleep.*
*That was the official story. Sleep apnea, ARIA's preliminary analysis suggested, exacerbated by the habitat's reduced oxygen levels. A quiet death, at rest. One moment alive, the next moment gone.*
*But Cassian didn't believe it.*
*He'd spoken with David the day before—the communications specialist had been agitated, paranoid, talking about documents he'd found in the system. "There's more to the tier system than they told us," David had said, his voice low and urgent. "I found files that show PharmaCorp knew exactly how each modification level would perform. They had projections, Cassian. They knew who would live and who would die before we even launched."*
*"Elena found those files months ago. She's been collecting evidence."*
*"No, this is different. This is newer. Internal communications from the past year, while we've been here. They're discussing us, Cassian. Tracking our deterioration. Taking notes on which modifications failed first, how the deaths occurred, what they can learn for Ares IV." David's hands had been shaking. "They're treating us like a long-term experiment. And some of these communications... some of them suggest they're not just observing."*
*"What do you mean?"*
*"I mean—" David had looked around, as if afraid of being overheard, even though they were alone. "I mean I think someone on this crew is reporting to PharmaCorp directly. Someone who isn't just a crew member."*
*The conversation had ended when Amara interrupted with a medical check. By morning, David was dead.*
*Cassian found Thea in their quarters.*
*"David Yoon." He stopped. "I don't think it was sleep apnea." He told her what David had said—the documents, the informant, the fear in his eyes the afternoon before he died.*
*Thea was quiet for a long moment.*
*"Who else knows?"*
*"No one."*
*She looked at him for a long time. "Keep it that way."*
*They didn't talk about it again that night. But as Cassian lay in the dark, he couldn't shake the feeling that he was being watched. That somewhere in the habitat's corridors, someone was tracking his movements, noting his conversations, reporting back to masters 225 million kilometers away.*
*David Yoon had found something that scared him. And now David Yoon was dead.*
*The official cause would be listed as natural. The crew would mourn briefly, bury him beside the others, move on with their endless work. But Cassian would remember the fear in David's eyes, the documents he claimed to have found, the warning he'd tried to deliver.*
*Someone on this crew was hiding something. And Cassian intended to find out who.*

---



# Chapter 23


## The Message


### Year 5, Month 8 — August 2041

*The message arrived on August 15, routed through the standard communication protocols, flagged as personal correspondence. Cassian almost didn't open it—he'd been avoiding Earth communications lately, finding the reminders of home more painful than comforting.*
*But it was from Dr. Morrison. Emily's doctor. And the subject line read simply: "Regarding Emily Vale."*
*He opened it alone, in the communications bay, while the rest of the crew slept.*
*Dr. Vale,*

*I'm deeply sorry to inform you that your sister, Emily Vale, passed away on August 12, 2041. Her GCS progressed to Stage 4 despite aggressive treatment with Areserine. She fought hard for as long as she could.*

*Emily wanted you to know several things:*

*She was proud of you. Prouder than she could express. She followed the mission reports religiously, celebrated every milestone, mourned every loss alongside you.*

*She understood why you went. She never blamed you for leaving, never resented you for choosing Mars over being by her side. She said—and I'm quoting directly—"Tell Cassian he saved me, even if I didn't make it. He saved millions. That's worth more than one person, even if that person is me."*

*She asked me to tell you not to blame yourself. The disease took her, not your absence. There was nothing you could have done that wasn't already being done.*

*I've attached a video message she recorded two weeks before her death, when she was still lucid enough to speak clearly. She asked that it be sent to you only after she was gone. I've honored that request.*

*With deepest sympathy,*
*Dr. Morrison*
*Cassian stared at the words until they blurred. August 12. Three weeks ago. Emily had been dead for three weeks, and he was only learning now because light only traveled so fast and Mars was so far away and the universe was so indifferent to human grief.*
*He opened the video attachment.*
*Emily's face filled the screen, thinner than he remembered, paler, but still her. Still his sister. Her eyes were bright despite the illness, her smile genuine despite the pain that must have been constant by then.*
*"Hey, Cass." Her voice was weak but steady. "If you're watching this, I'm gone. Sorry to spring it on you this way. Dr. Morrison said you'd want to know immediately, but I figured... I figured you'd also want to hear it from me."*
*She paused, gathering strength.*
*"I'm not going to lie and say I'm not scared. I am. I'm terrified. Dying isn't something I ever planned for, and it turns out I'm not very good at it." A laugh, cut short by coughing. "But I wanted you to know that I'm okay. As okay as anyone can be, facing this."*
*"I watched you launch, you know. Stood in the crowd at Kennedy and watched you disappear into the sky. Mom was crying. Dad was trying not to. And I just... I just felt proud. My brother, going to Mars. My brother, saving the world."*
*"The Areserine you're making—it gave me two extra years. Two years I wouldn't have had. Two years of watching you do impossible things, of following your mission, of hoping every day that you'd make it home safe."*
*"You won't make it home before I go. I know that now. The math doesn't work, and we're both too good at math to pretend otherwise." Another pause. "But I need you to know something, Cass. I need you to hear me say it."*
*"You didn't fail me. You didn't abandon me. You chose to save millions of people, and I was one of those millions, even if the saving didn't stick. What you're doing matters. What you're sacrificing matters. And I wouldn't trade a single day of these past five years if it meant you'd given up and stayed home."*
*"Come home when you can. Find someone who makes you happy. Grow those plants you always talked about—the garden with the roses and the vegetables and the herbs you can't pronounce." She smiled. "Live, Cass. Live a long time, and live well, and don't spend it grieving for me. I got more than I expected. More than I deserved. And some of that is because of you."*
*"I love you, big brother. I always will."*
*The screen went dark.*
*Cassian sat in the communications bay for a long time, not moving, barely breathing. The red emergency lighting cast everything in the color of blood, the color of Mars, the color of a world that had taken everything from him.*
*Emily was dead. Had been dead for three weeks. And he'd been here, harvesting organisms and attending funerals and watching his crew tear itself apart, while his sister slipped away 225 million kilometers from his reach.*
*He'd come to Mars to save her. That was the reason, the justification, the thing that got him through the worst moments. He was saving Emily. He was buying her time.*
*And now she was gone, and the time he'd bought had run out, and he was still here, still trapped, still harvesting organisms that produced medicine that arrived too late.*
*The sobs came without warning, wrenching sounds that tore from his chest and echoed off the metal walls. He cried until there was nothing left, until his eyes burned and his throat ached and the grief had carved out a space inside him that he knew would never fully heal.*

*At some point the sobs stopped and his mind went somewhere else entirely, the way it sometimes did under maximum stress. He found himself calculating. Not anything useful—just the numbers that were always there, the ones that had been there since he was nineteen years old. Current distance from Earth: 225 million kilometers, give or take. Light-speed delay: twelve minutes, forty seconds. Three weeks ago, when Emily died, the delay had been eleven minutes, eighteen seconds.*

*She had been dead for eleven minutes and eighteen seconds of light before he could have known it, even in principle.*

*Even the speed of light hadn’t been fast enough.*

*Thea found him an hour later, still sitting in the communications bay, staring at the blank screen.*
*"Cassian?" Her voice was soft, worried. "What happened?"*
*"Emily." The word came out broken. "She died. Three weeks ago. I just found out."*
*Thea didn't say anything. She just sat beside him, took his hand in hers, and stayed.*
*There was nothing else to do. No words that would help, no comfort that would fill the emptiness. Just presence. Just the simple acknowledgment that grief was too heavy to carry alone.*
*They sat together until the ship's lights brightened for the simulated dawn, two people mourning a woman they'd never meet again, on a world that felt more like a grave every day.*

---

*The days after Emily's death were the hardest of Cassian's life.*
*He moved through his duties mechanically—tending the organisms, running diagnostics, attending briefings—but nothing felt real. It was as if a pane of glass had descended between him and the world, muffling sounds and blurring edges, making everything seem like a dream he couldn't quite wake from.*
*The crew noticed. They gave him space, approaching only when necessary, speaking in the soft tones people reserve for the grieving. Even Jackson, who had never shown concern for anyone's wellbeing, avoided confrontation. Death commanded a certain respect, even among those who had forgotten how to be human.*
*But it was Thea who truly understood.*
*"You're not eating," she observed on Day 4, watching him push reconstituted protein around his plate.*
*"Not hungry."*
*"You need fuel. Your body doesn't care if you're grieving—it still needs calories."*
*"I know." Cassian set down his fork. "I know you're right. I know I need to eat and sleep and work and act like a functioning person. But Thea—" His voice cracked. "I don't know how to do this. I don't know how to be someone whose sister is dead."*
*"You learn. Day by day, you figure out who you are without her." Thea's eyes were bright with unshed tears. "I lost my grandmother when I was sixteen. She raised me while my parents worked. When she died, I felt like the whole world had shifted on its axis. Like nothing would ever make sense again."*
*"Did it? Make sense, eventually?"*
*"No. Not the way it did before. But I found a new kind of sense. A new way of moving through the world that had her absence built into it." Thea took his hand. "Emily is gone. That will never stop hurting. But the hurt becomes part of you instead of something that happens to you. It stops being a wound and becomes... a feature. Something that shapes how you live without destroying your ability to live."*
*"That sounds exhausting."*
*"It is. At first. And then it just becomes normal. The new normal that you build because the old one is gone forever."*
*Cassian looked at her—this woman who had crossed the solar system with him, who had watched friends die and systems collapse and hope evaporate, who still somehow managed to find meaning in the chaos.*
*"I love you. I know I've said it before, but I need you to really hear it now. You're the reason I'm still here. The reason I haven't given up."*
*"I know." She squeezed his hand. "And you're my reason too. That's how we survive this. By being each other's reason."*

---



# Chapter 24


## The Light


### Year 5, Month 9 — September 2041

*Viktor Kozlov had been crying for three hours.*
*Cassian found him in the cultivation facility, slumped against Tank 7, his face buried in his hands. The physician's sobs echoed off the metal walls, raw and ugly sounds that spoke of grief too deep for dignity.*
*"Viktor?" Cassian approached carefully, unsure what he was walking into. "Are you okay?"*
*A stupid question. No one was okay. But Viktor's crying was different—more intense, more sustained than the occasional breakdowns that had become routine.*
*"He's gone," Viktor whispered. "Kolya. He's really gone."*
*"Viktor, Kolya's been gone for six years. You know that."*
*"No." Viktor looked up, his eyes red and swollen but somehow clearer than they'd been in months. "I mean he's gone. I haven't seen him in two days. No hallucinations, no visions, no conversations with a son who exists only in my broken mind. He's finally left me."*
*Cassian didn't know what to say. Viktor's hallucinations had been a constant for years—a symptom of grief that had curdled into psychosis, a manifestation of guilt too heavy to bear. The absence of those visions should have been a good sign, a step toward recovery.*
*But Viktor was crying as though Kolya had died all over again.*
*"I think..." Viktor struggled to compose himself. "I think I was holding onto him. The visions. They let me pretend he was still here, still part of my life. As long as I could see him, talk to him, I didn't have to accept that he was really dead."*
*"And now?"*
*"Now I have to accept it. He's not coming back. Not as a ghost, not as a hallucination, not as anything." Viktor's voice cracked. "My son is dead, Cassian. He's been dead for six years, and I'm only just now letting myself know it."*
They sat in silence, surrounded by the soft cyan glow of the organism tanks, the amber threading through it in its slow counterpoint. The creatures kept on doing what they'd done since Mars had oceans, unaware of the human tragedy unfolding beside them.
*And then something strange happened.*
*The tanks grew brighter.*
*Not gradually, the way a sunrise crept across a landscape, but suddenly—a sharp increase in bioluminescence that made Cassian squint. He turned to look, and what he saw made his breath catch.*
*All ten tanks were pulsing in unison now, their rhythm synchronized for the first time since Torres had documented the phenomenon months ago. But it was more than that. The pulses were matching Viktor's sobs—speeding up when his grief intensified, slowing when he caught his breath, as if the organisms were somehow listening.*
*"Viktor," Cassian said quietly. "Look at this."*
*The physician raised his head, tears still streaming down his cheeks, and stared at the tanks. His expression shifted from grief to confusion to something approaching wonder.*
*"They're... they're responding to me?"*
*"I don't know. I've never seen them do this before."*
*The glow intensified further, the cyan light now bright enough to cast sharp shadows across the facility, the amber deepening toward something almost golden. The organisms pulsed faster, then slower, then faster again, their rhythm a visual echo of Viktor's emotional state.*
*"ARIA," Cassian called. "Are you recording this?"*
*"I am recording. The organisms are exhibiting unprecedented synchronized behavior. Bioluminescence intensity has increased 1,500% above baseline. I have no theoretical framework to explain this phenomenon."*
*Viktor stood slowly, moving toward Tank 7 with the careful steps of someone approaching something holy. He pressed his palm against the observation port, and the light beneath it flared—not hot, not painful, but responsive.*
*"They know," Viktor whispered. "They know I'm grieving. They've been here for three billion years, alone in the dark, and they know what it means to lose something."*
*It was impossible. Single-celled organisms didn't have consciousness, didn't have empathy, didn't respond to human emotions. And yet the light pulsed in time with Viktor's heartbeat, a visual representation of connection that shouldn't exist.*
*"We should tell the others," Cassian said.*
*"Yes. They should see this."*

---

*Within an hour, the entire crew had gathered in the cultivation facility.*
*Amara had declared it medically relevant. Chen had authorized suspension of normal duties. Even Jackson and Isabella, still confined when not working, had been allowed to attend—because whatever was happening here transcended the petty conflicts that had torn them apart.*
*The tanks glowed like something out of a dream, their combined light filling the facility with ethereal radiance. The synchronized pulsing had stabilized into a slow, steady rhythm—about 0.5 Hz, slower than a heartbeat but somehow more profound.*
*"They're responding to our presence," Torres said, his earlier paranoia forgotten in the face of genuine wonder. "All of us together, the emotion in this room—they're feeding on it somehow."*
*"Not feeding." Thea's voice was quiet, thoughtful. "Reflecting. They're showing us something. What we're feeling, what we're carrying—they're making it visible."*
*Elena stepped forward, her perpetual anger softened by something approaching awe. "They've been alone for three billion years. Underground. In the dark. No other life forms to interact with, no stimulation, nothing but minerals and silence."*
*"And now we're here," Cassian finished. "The first other living things they've encountered since Mars died."*
*"They're not just producing Areserine," Viktor said. "They're witnessing us. Recording us. The same way ARIA does, but... different. Deeper."*
*The crew stood in silence, bathed in alien light, contemplating the implications. They'd come to Mars to harvest these organisms, to extract their metabolic products for human benefit. They'd thought of them as resources, as tools, as a means to an end.*
*But what if the organisms were thinking the same thing about them?*
*"I have an idea," Amara said slowly. "A memorial. For everyone we've lost. We could hold it here, in this light. Let them... witness."*
*"That's ridiculous," Jackson said, but his voice lacked its usual edge.*
*"Is it?" Amara looked around the room at faces worn down by years of loss. "We've buried seven people on Mars. We haven't properly mourned any of them. We've been too busy surviving to grieve. Maybe it's time we stopped surviving long enough to remember."*
*The vote was unanimous.*

---

*They held the memorial service three days later.*
*Seven names. Seven lives. Seven people who had crossed the solar system and never returned.*
*Rachel Park, who fooled everyone until it was too late.*
*Sarah Mitchell, killed by a faulty O-ring.*
*Hassan Farooq, who walked into certain death to save the rest of them.*
*Robert Hayashi, who worked until the work killed him.*
*Lian Wei, whose heart couldn't take the strain.*
*Yuki Nakamura, killed over a ration of water.*
*David Yoon, whose death might not have been natural.*
*Commander Chen read their names aloud, and with each name, the organism tanks pulsed brighter. It was as if the ancient creatures were acknowledging each loss, adding it to some incomprehensible archive of human suffering.*
*When Viktor spoke Kolya's name—adding his son to the list, though he had never walked these corridors—the tanks flared so bright that everyone had to look away.*
*Everyone except Jackson, who was leaning against the back wall, one arm tucked across his body, the other turning a toothpick slowly in the corner of his mouth. He didn't look away from the light. His face did something brief and private that had no name in the vocabulary he used for things, and then it was gone, and the toothpick resumed its slow rotation, and the room went back to its grieving.*
*"I don't understand this," Viktor said afterward, his voice raw but somehow composed. "I don't understand how they know, or what they're doing, or what any of it means. But I think... I think they're telling us something."*
*"What?"*
*"That we're not alone. That grief is universal. That even here, even at the end of everything, there's something that notices. Something that cares."*
*They stayed in the facility for twenty minutes after the memorial ended, sitting in the layered light — cyan and amber, with the green speckles moving slowly across the tank surfaces like something breathing — not speaking. For the first time in months, the crew wasn't divided by faction or fear or mutual suspicion. They were just people, mourning together, witnessed by creatures older than human comprehension.*
*It wasn't healing. The wounds were too deep, too numerous for one night of light to repair.*
*But it was something. A reminder that they were still capable of connection. Still capable of feeling something other than fear and rage and despair.*
*The organisms pulsed their ancient rhythm, and the humans sat in their glow, and for one evening, Mars felt slightly less like a grave.*

---

*The phenomenon lasted for three days.*
*ARIA documented everything: the synchronized pulsing patterns, the correlation between human emotional states and bioluminescence intensity, the unprecedented increase in Areserine production that accompanied the heightened activity. Her analysis filled terabytes of storage, data that would revolutionize extremophile biology if it ever reached Earth.*
*But for the crew, the significance wasn't scientific. It was personal.*
*People began visiting the cultivation facility individually, sitting with the tanks the way they might have sat with a therapist back on Earth. They talked to the organisms—about grief, about fear, about the mounting weight of survival. And the organisms listened, or seemed to listen, their light responding to human emotion in ways that defied explanation.*
*Cassian found himself there most nights, after Thea had fallen asleep. He would sit beside Tank 7—his tank, the one he'd tended since the mission began—and speak to the ancient creatures that had become his unlikely companions.*
*"Emily would have loved this." One night, his voice barely above a whisper. "She was always fascinated by connection. By the way living things relate to each other across impossible distances. She would have seen something meaningful in this."*
*The tank pulsed gently, its light softening as if in sympathy.*
*"I don't know what you are. I don't know if you're conscious, or empathic, or just responding to electromagnetic fields in ways we don't understand. But I want to thank you. For witnessing. For... for caring, in whatever way you're capable of caring."*
*The light flickered—three quick pulses, then a return to the steady rhythm. Like a response. Like an acknowledgment.*
*"I'm going to get you home," Cassian said. "All of you. I don't know how yet, but I'm going to make sure everything we've done here wasn't wasted. You've survived for three billion years. I won't let that story end because some corporation decided profit was more important than preservation."*
*He didn't expect an answer. The organisms were cells, not conversationalists. But when he left the facility that night, he felt something he hadn't felt in months.*
*Purpose.*
*The Areserine would save hundreds of thousands of lives. That was worth dying for. But maybe—just maybe—there was something else here too. A discovery that transcended medicine. A connection that proved life could find ways to understand itself across impossible gulfs of time and biology.*
*Maybe that was worth living for.*

---

*On the third day, the phenomenon faded.*
*The synchronized pulsing slowed, then stopped. The bioluminescence returned to normal levels. The organisms went back to what they had always done, what they would go on doing long after the last human voice fell silent on Mars.*
*"They're done," Torres said, running diagnostics on the tanks. His earlier paranoia had been replaced by something approaching reverence. "Whatever they were doing, whatever they were trying to tell us—they're done now."*
*"Do you still think they're watching us?" Cassian asked.*
*"I don't know what I think anymore." Torres shook his head slowly. "But I know what I felt. When I sat with them during the memorial, when I told them about my mother who died of GCS back on Earth... I felt heard. For the first time since I set foot on this planet, I felt like something actually understood what I was going through."*

---

*The week after the memorial, Torres found Cassian in the cultivation facility during the afternoon shift and sat down on the equipment step without asking.*

*This was not unusual. What was unusual was that he sat there for ten minutes without saying anything about the organisms.*

*Cassian worked. The tanks ran their slow rhythm. Outside, the Martian afternoon pressed its orange light against the viewport.*

*"I have a daughter," Torres said finally. "Clara. She's fourteen." He looked at Tank 4. "When I left she was eight. She sends me messages every few months — drawings at first, then letters, now just texts. Fourteen-year-old texts." He smiled briefly. "She asks me what the organisms look like up close. Every few months, the same question. I've described them probably thirty different ways and she always wants another one."*

*"What do you tell her?"*

*"This time I told her they look like the inside of a dream you almost remember." Torres looked at the tanks. "She said that was the best one yet."*

*Cassian set down the collection container. "What made you come here? Originally."*

*"I wrote my thesis on chemolithotrophic extremophiles in deep-sea hydrothermal vents." Torres's voice had the quality it always had when he was on solid scientific ground — precise, unhurried, genuinely pleased to be asked. "There's an organism, Thermodesulfovibrio, that processes sulfur compounds above 95 degrees Celsius. I spent four years studying it. And the whole time I was thinking: if something can do this here, what's it doing somewhere else? What's it doing somewhere we haven't looked yet?" He paused. "Mars seemed like the obvious answer."*

*"And was it?"*

*"It was better than the obvious answer." He looked at the tanks for a long moment. "When I'm not — when I'm thinking clearly — I know these organisms are the most significant biological discovery in human history. I know whatever they're doing is going to take decades to understand. I know Clara is going to grow up in a world that's different because of what we found down here." He stopped. "I haven't been thinking clearly for a while. I know that too."*

*"You were thinking clearly at the memorial."*

*"For a few hours." Torres stood. "I wanted to thank you. For the observation log. For keeping the data. I've seen pieces of it — enough to know that what you've been doing is the work someone needed to do and I should have been the one doing it." He met Cassian's eyes. "You're a better biologist than either of us knew. For what it's worth."*

*"It's worth something."*

*Torres nodded. He looked at the tanks one more time — not with the old paranoid intensity but with something quieter and more genuine, the look of a man trying to hold onto a thing he knows is slipping.*

*"I love these things." Quietly. "I want you to know that. Whatever else has happened — whatever I've said — I came here because I love this, and it hasn't gone away. Even when everything else did."*

*He left.*

*Three weeks later, Viktor killed him.*

*Cassian stood in the corridor afterward, back against the wall, hands at his sides, thinking about Clara who asked every few months what the organisms looked like up close, and a father who had been trying to find new ways to describe them.*
*"Maybe that's all that matters."*
*"Maybe. Or maybe we're both going crazy, anthropomorphizing bacteria because we're so desperate for connection that we'll find it anywhere." Torres managed a weary smile. "Either way, I'm grateful. Even if it was just my imagination, I'm grateful."*
*They stood together in the cultivation facility, watching the tanks pulse the same light they had pulsed since before the first fish swam on Earth. Outside, Mars waited with its red dust and thin atmosphere and endless capacity for taking human life. Inside, ancient organisms continued their three-billion-year existence, indifferent to the temporary creatures who had come to harvest them.*
*But for three days, something had bridged that gap. Something had connected human grief to alien biology, proving that consciousness—or whatever the organisms possessed—could recognize suffering across impossible distances.*
*It was the closest thing to a miracle any of them had experienced.*
*And it gave Cassian something he hadn't realized he'd lost: a reason to keep going.*

---



# INTERLUDE


## Letters Home II

*Personal transmissions, Year 5, various dates*
*[Compiled from ARIA communication logs]*

---

*Cassian Vale to Emily Vale*
*August 20, 2041 — UNSENT*
*Em,*
*I'm writing this knowing you'll never read it. You're gone. Three weeks gone, and I'm only now finding words for what that means.*
*I spent five years telling myself I was doing this for you. Every hard day, every death, every moment I wanted to give up—I pushed through because I was saving my sister. Because somewhere on Earth, you were still fighting, and the medicine I was making was keeping you in the fight.*
*And now you're gone, and I don't know what I'm fighting for anymore.*
*The crew needs me. The mission needs me. There are still 300,000 people who will live because of what we're doing here. That should be enough. That should matter.*
*But it doesn't feel like enough. It feels like I traded everything—my life, my future, my chance to be with you at the end—for a number. A statistic. Lives I'll never see, people I'll never know.*
*You said not to blame myself. You said you were proud of me. You said to live.*
*I'm trying, Em. I'm trying so hard. But some days it feels like I'm already dead, just going through the motions until my body catches up with my heart.*
*I love you. I miss you. I don't know how to do this without you.*
*Cass*

---

*Cassian Vale to Emily Vale*
*September 20, 2041 — UNSENT*
*Em,*
*A month since I learned you were gone. A month and five days since you actually died.*
*Something strange happened last week. The organisms—the ones I've been cultivating for five years—they did something impossible. They responded to Viktor's grief. Literally responded, their light changing in sync with his crying.*
*We held a memorial service. For everyone we've lost. And the tanks glowed so bright it was like standing in daylight.*
*I don't know what it means. Torres thinks they're intelligent somehow, that they've been watching us all along. Amara thinks it's just biology, chemical reactions we don't understand. Thea thinks it doesn't matter what causes it—what matters is what it gives us.*
*I think... I think they were mourning with us. I think creatures that have survived for three billion years in the dark recognized something in our grief. Found it familiar somehow.*
*You would have loved it, Em. You always said the universe was more connected than we knew. That consciousness wasn't limited to brains and neurons. That there were ways of knowing that science couldn't explain yet.*
*Maybe you were right. Maybe you were always right.*
*I'm going to get your medicine to Earth. I'm going to make sure your death—all the deaths—meant something. And when I do, I'm going to sit under an aurora, whether it's on Mars or Earth or somewhere else entirely, and I'm going to tell you about the light.*
*I love you. I always will.*
*Cass*

---

*Dr. Amara Osei to Marcus Okafor*
*May 30, 2041 — PERSONAL*
*Marcus,*
*I know you don't want to talk about it. You've been avoiding the subject since I gave you the diagnosis, pretending everything's normal, pushing through your duties like nothing's changed.*
*But something has changed. You're dying. We both know it now.*
*I've been a doctor for twenty years. I've delivered bad news more times than I can count. But I've never had to tell someone I care about that their body is failing, that the modifications designed to save them weren't enough, that all my training and all our technology can't stop what's happening.*
*I don't know what I'm supposed to say. I don't know how to help you process this when I can barely process it myself.*
*What I do know is this: you're not alone. Whatever time you have left, you have a crew who respects you, friends who care about you, and one doctor who will do everything in her power to make sure you're comfortable and functional until the end.*
*That's not enough. I know it's not enough. But it's what I have.*
*I'm here if you need to talk. If you need to scream, or cry, or pretend nothing's wrong. Whatever you need, I'll try to provide.*
*With love,*
*Amara*

---

*Marcus Okafor to [UNSENT — PERSONAL LOG]*
*June 15, 2041*
*I'm dying.*
*I've known for two weeks now. Amara told me, gave me the diagnosis, explained the science in her gentle doctor voice. Radiation damage. Progressive organ failure. Six months to a year.*
*I haven't told anyone except her. Haven't broken down. Haven't screamed at the unfairness of it all. I just keep working, keep maintaining the systems, keep pretending everything is normal.*
*Because what else is there to do?*
*I came here knowing the risks. We all did. The projections said 23% crew mortality, and I thought—arrogantly, stupidly—that I would be in the 77% who made it home. I'm Enhanced tier. I got the best modifications. I was supposed to be safe.*
*But safety is an illusion. Always has been. We tell ourselves that planning and preparation and good decisions will protect us, but the universe doesn't care about our plans. It just does what it does, and sometimes that means the healthiest person on the crew dies of radiation sickness before the mission ends.*
*I'm not angry. I expected to be angry, but I'm not. I'm tired. So tired of fighting, of surviving, of pretending I have any control over anything.*
*Hassan walked into the oxygen generator knowing he would die. He made peace with it in the minutes before, turned his fear into acceptance, transformed sacrifice into meaning.*
*I don't know if I can do that. I don't know if I have enough left in me to die well.*
*But I'm going to try. Because these people—this broken, battered, barely-functional crew—they need someone to show them that death doesn't have to be ugly. That it's possible to face the end with dignity.*
*Maybe that's my final contribution to the mission. Not the systems I maintained or the crises I solved, but the example I set for how to leave.*

---

*Thea Miren to [UNSENT — PERSONAL LOG]*
*September 8, 2041*
*The probability calculations are getting worse.*
*I run them every night, the way other people pray. Survival rates, death projections, time remaining until we can leave. I plug in the numbers and watch the curves and tell myself that math doesn't lie.*
*Math doesn't lie. But it does hurt.*
*Current survival probability for all remaining crew: 31%. Down from 62% last year. Down from 77% at launch.*
