*That means we're going to lose more than two-thirds of the people still alive. That means I'm probably going to watch most of my friends die. That means there's a good chance I won't survive to see Earth again.*
*Cassian is on the better end of the curve—Standard tier, no major health issues, psychologically stable despite everything. My projections give him a 67% chance of survival. Better than most.*
*But 67% means there's a one in three chance I lose him. One in three chance I have to watch him die the way I've watched so many others. One in three chance I go home alone, carrying the memory of everything we had and everything we lost.*
*I can't stop running the numbers. It's the only thing that makes me feel like I have any control.*
*But some nights, I wish I could be like the others. Wish I could believe in hope without calculating its probability. Wish I could hold Cassian and not think about the statistics hovering over both our heads.*
*Maybe ignorance would be easier. Maybe not knowing would hurt less.*
*But I'm a mathematician. Numbers are all I have.*
*And the numbers say we're running out of time.*

---

*Elena Vasquez to Maria Vasquez (Mother) [UNSENT — PERSONAL LOG]*
*August 5, 2041*
*Mamá,*
*I've been holding back in my letters. Telling you things are hard but not how hard. Describing deaths without explaining how they happened. Protecting you from the truth because I thought that's what a good daughter should do.*
*I can't do that anymore. You deserve to know what's really happening here.*
*We're dying, Mamá. Not just from radiation and accidents and equipment failures—we're dying from each other. A woman was beaten nearly to death last month. Another was killed over a water ration. We've become animals, fighting over scraps while the corporation that sent us here counts their profits.*
*I've found evidence of what PharmaCorp really wanted from this mission. It's not medicine—it's minerals. $25 trillion worth of rare earth elements buried under the Martian surface. The Areserine was just cover, a humanitarian story to justify sending expendable workers to map their future mining sites.*
*We're not heroes, Mamá. We're labor. Cheap, disposable labor that cost less to sacrifice than to protect.*
*When I get home—if I get home—I'm going to destroy them. I have documents, communications, proof of everything they've done. I'm going to make sure the whole world knows what kind of company PharmaCorp really is.*
*But I need you to know first. In case I don't make it. In case something happens to me before I can tell my story.*
*Remember what you taught me: the powerful prey on the powerless, and the only way to survive is to fight back. I'm fighting, Mamá. In whatever small ways I can, I'm fighting.*
*I love you. Tell Papa and the others I love them too.*
*Your daughter,*
*Elena*

---

*Commander Sarah Chen to [UNSENT — PERSONAL LOG]*
*September 15, 2041*
*I've lost control.*
*Jackson beats crew members and walks away with a slap on the wrist. Isabella kills someone and returns to duty the next day. David Yoon dies under circumstances I can't explain, and I don't have the resources to investigate.*
*I'm supposed to be the commander. I'm supposed to maintain order, enforce discipline, keep these people safe. Instead, I'm presiding over the collapse of everything I was trained to protect.*
*ARIA says my stability index is at 47. Lowest on the crew. Lower than Viktor with his hallucinations, lower than Torres with his paranoia, lower than Sophie who hasn't spoken voluntarily in months.*
*I should step down. Let someone else take over—Marcus, maybe, or Amara. Someone whose mind isn't crumbling under the weight of five hundred million ghosts.*
*But who would want this job? Who would volunteer to lead twenty broken people through eighteen more months of death and desperation? Who would choose to carry the responsibility I can barely lift?*
*No one. So I keep going. I issue orders I don't believe in, enforce rules that have lost all meaning, pretend to be the leader they need because the alternative is admitting that no one's really in charge anymore.*
*Sistema Abismo started all of this. I signed a piece of paper twelve years ago, and the dominoes have been falling ever since. Rachel, Sarah, Hassan, all of them—they're my fault. My responsibility. My burden to carry.*
*Some nights I dream about the caves. About the moment the drill broke through and released the pathogen. In the dreams, I have a chance to stop it, to seal the chamber before the infection escapes.*
*I never do. In the dreams, just like in reality, I step aside and let history happen.*
*Maybe that's what I am now. Just someone stepping aside. Watching history unfold without the strength to change it.*
*Thirteen months left. Thirteen months of pretending to lead while everything falls apart around me.*
*I don't know if I can make it.*
*I don't know if any of us can.*

---

*The final months of Year 5 passed in grim routine. By the time Year 6 began, seven markers stood in the Martian soil, and the crew that remained had stopped expecting things to get better.*
*The sixth year would be the last.*


# ACT FIVE: THE FINAL YEAR



# Chapter 25


## The Server Room


### Year 6, Month 3 — March 2042

*The server room was cramped and poorly lit, banks of equipment humming in the confined space. Kenji was alone. Dmitri had made sure of that.*
*He'd found the deletion logs three months ago—ninety-three messages from his daughter, erased before they reached him. He'd traced them to Kenji's terminal, then watched, and waited, and built his case with the patience of an engineer who understood that a failed system needed to be fully diagnosed before it could be shut down.*
*"Kenji." Dmitri's voice was calm, almost pleasant. "We need to talk."*
*Kenji turned. "Dmitri? What are you doing here?"*
*"The messages from my daughter. Ninety-three of them."*
*The color drained from Kenji's face.*
*"I traced the deletions to your terminal." Dmitri stepped closer. "I found the logs."*
*"I was going to give them back—I saved copies—"*
*"My daughter is not information."*
*Dmitri moved faster than a man his size should have been able to move. One hand closed around Kenji's throat, lifting him off the ground and slamming him against the server rack. Equipment rattled. Warning lights flickered.*
*"Dmitri, please—" Kenji's voice was a wheeze, his feet kicking uselessly.*
*"Three months." Dmitri's voice was quiet now, almost conversational. "Three months of wondering why Anya stopped writing. Three months of thinking my daughter had forgotten me. Do you know what that feels like? To believe your child doesn't love you anymore?"*
*"I'm sorry, I'm sorry, I didn't think—"*
*"No. You didn't."*
*Dmitri's other hand found the torque driver on his belt—the heavy-duty tool he used for reactor maintenance, solid steel with a weighted grip. He'd brought it for this moment. He'd planned every detail.*
*The first blow caught Kenji across the temple. Not hard enough to kill—not yet. Just hard enough to stun, to stop the thrashing, to make what came next easier.*
*He swung again.*

---

*ARIA's alert came twelve minutes later, when the body had cooled enough for thermal sensors to register the anomaly.*
*Marcus found them—Dmitri sitting against the server rack, torque driver still in his hand, covered in blood that wasn't his. Kenji's body lay crumpled nearby, the skull caved in, the face unrecognizable.*
*"Jesus Christ." Marcus's voice was barely a whisper. "Dmitri, what did you do?"*
*"He stole my daughter's messages." Dmitri's voice was flat, emotionless. "Ninety-three of them. Three months of her life."*
*"So you killed him?"*
*"Yes."*
*There was no remorse in the word. No guilt, no horror at what he'd done. Just a statement of fact, delivered with the same matter-of-fact tone Dmitri used when reporting reactor diagnostics.*
*"ARIA, seal this room. Get Commander Chen. Get everyone."*
*The trial—if it could be called that—lasted less than an hour. The evidence was undeniable: security footage, DNA traces, Dmitri's own confession delivered without emotion or apology.*
*"He stole from me," Dmitri said when asked to explain. "Not money, not food—my daughter. The only piece of her I had left. I took from him what he took from me."*
*The vote was split. Some wanted execution—spacing Dmitri through the airlock, letting Mars have him. Others argued for confinement, for the same inadequate punishment that had been applied to Jackson and Isabella.*
*In the end, pragmatism won again. Dmitri was the only person qualified to maintain the ISRU plant, the system that produced the fuel they would need to go home. Without him, their return ship would never launch.*
*"Confinement," Chen pronounced, her voice hollow. "When not on essential duty. Reduced rations. Loss of communication privileges."*
*"Communication privileges." Elena's laugh was bitter. "The man he murdered was our communications specialist. Who's going to handle his duties now?"*
*No one answered. There was no answer to give.*
*Dmitri returned to work the next day, blood cleaned from his hands but not from his conscience. Kenji Sato was buried beside the others, his marker a testament to how far they'd fallen:*
*Kenji Sato. Communications Specialist. Murdered March 15, 2042. He made a terrible mistake.*
*Eight graves now. Sixteen crew remaining.*
*And nine months until the return ship arrived.*

---

*The message arrived on February 1st. ARIA logged receipt at 09:14.*

*No one watched it immediately. It sat in the queue for two days before Cassian opened it, alone, in the cultivation facility at 0300.*

*Halsey had changed the setting. He was standing rather than sitting — in front of the same window, the London sky behind him dark. A jacket over a white shirt. No tie. The change felt deliberate: less corporate, more personal. A man who had decided this message needed to feel different.*

*He had prepared it. The preparation was visible in the stillness of his hands, the measured pace of his delivery, the way he held the camera — all of it the product of someone who had rehearsed.*

*"I know it's been a while since we've had two-way contact," Halsey said. His voice was steady. "I want you to know that we are aware of the communication difficulties and that our technical teams are working on resolution." A beat. "In the meantime, I'm going to keep talking. Because that's what you do. You keep going."*

*He looked into the camera.*

*"You are in the final year. Eleven months. The return vehicle is on trajectory, and when it arrives you will come home, and the world that's waiting for you—" He stopped. A breath that was almost — not quite — a catch. "The world has not forgotten you. Whatever it has felt like out there, the world has not forgotten."*

*The formal language followed: mission completion, humanitarian legacy, the word family appearing twice. Cassian listened to it from a distance, the way you listen to something that is performing at you rather than speaking to you.*

*And then, near the end, after all of it — there was a moment.*

*Three seconds.*

*The message should have ended. The framing suggested it was ending. But Halsey held the camera, and his expression — which had been held carefully throughout — did something the previous recordings hadn't done. Not much. A settling. A slight loss of architecture. The face underneath the face visible for three seconds before he caught it and composed himself and ended the recording.*

*Cassian watched it four times, trying to name what he'd seen.*

*Not guilt. Not grief exactly. The expression of a man looking at something that had gone past the point where he could affect it, understanding — perhaps for the first time in a visceral rather than administrative sense — what that meant.*

*"Come home," Halsey said. And the recording ended.*

*Cassian sat in the cultivation facility for a long time with the organisms at his back.*

*He thought: we have that in common.*

*He thought: that's the most frightening thing I know about him.*

---

*PharmaCorp Internal — Mission Operations*
*To: Board of Directors, Executive Committee*
*From: R. Halsey, Director of Mission Operations*
*Date: December 14, 2041*
*Re: Ares III Communication Status*

*As previously reported, outbound crew communication from the Ares III habitat has been non-functional since approximately Month 8 of Year 5. We continue to transmit to the crew on the assumption that inbound communications remain functional from their end, though this cannot be confirmed.*

*Automated ARIA telemetry — which had been providing production and health metrics independently of crew-initiated contact — also went offline in November 2041. We are currently operating without confirmed data on mission status.*

*Public communications continue to characterize mission status as nominal. This position is consistent with the last confirmed crew report and represents our best available information. We see no benefit to disclosing the communication gap publicly at this stage, as the return vehicle remains on schedule.*

*The board's questions regarding Year 5 REE survey completion cannot be answered at this time. We expect to have full data access upon payload arrival.*

*I will continue to update the board as the situation develops.*

*R. Halsey*


# Chapter 26


## The Breaking


### Year 6, Month 6 — June 2042

*Viktor Kozlov had been lucid for three months.*
*After the bioluminescent phenomenon—after he'd wept in the cultivation facility and the organisms had responded—something had shifted in his fractured mind. The hallucinations of Kolya had stopped. The paranoid whispers, the constant sense of being watched by his dead son, had faded into silence.*
*For three months, Viktor had been almost like his old self. Functional. Professional. Contributing to the mission instead of just surviving it.*
*And then Dr. James Torres suggested that the organisms might be dangerous.*
*"They're responding to us," Torres had said at the weekly briefing. "Not just passively producing Areserine—actively responding. To our emotions, our presence, our grief. That's not normal extremophile behavior."*
*"We've established that they exhibit colonial coordination," Amara replied. "The synchronized pulsing, the chemical signaling—"*
*"This is beyond signaling. This is empathy. Or something that looks like empathy." Torres's eyes were bright with the intensity that had replaced his earlier paranoia. "What if they're not just witnessing us? What if they're influencing us? What if the Areserine itself is a vector for something we don't understand?"*
*The suggestion hit Viktor like a physical blow.*
*The organisms had responded to his grief. Had seemed to comfort him, to acknowledge his pain, to help him let go of Kolya. He'd interpreted that as compassion—as proof that even ancient alien life could recognize human suffering.*
*But what if Torres was right? What if the organisms were manipulating them? What if his sudden lucidity wasn't healing but control?*
*The thought wormed into Viktor's mind and wouldn't leave.*

---

*The break came on June 18.*
*Viktor had been in the cultivation facility, running routine checks on the organism tanks, when Kolya appeared.*
*Not a memory. Not an imagining. Kolya—eight years old, wearing the blue pajamas he'd been buried in—standing beside Tank 7 and staring at his father with eyes that were no longer quite human.*
*"Papa." The voice was wrong, distorted, like a recording played at the wrong speed. "They showed me to you. Do you understand now?"*
*"Kolya?" Viktor's voice cracked. "You're not real. You're not—"*
*"I'm in the light, Papa. I've been in the light since the beginning. They kept me safe. Kept me waiting for you."*
*The organism tanks pulsed, their bioluminescence intensifying. The same response Viktor had treasured three months ago now filled him with horror.*
*"They're using you." Viktor backed away, his hands trembling. "Using your image to manipulate me."*
*"No, Papa. They're showing you the truth. The Areserine isn't just medicine. It's a bridge. Between what was and what will be. Between the living and the dead."*
*"Stop it. Stop wearing his face."*
*"I am Kolya." The apparition smiled, and the smile was wrong—too wide, too knowing, nothing like the innocent boy Viktor remembered. "I've always been Kolya. They just helped me find my way back to you."*
*Viktor screamed.*

---

*Dr. Torres was working late in the adjacent lab when he heard the commotion. He rushed to the cultivation facility and found Viktor on his knees, sobbing, clutching his head while the organism tanks pulsed in agitation around him.*
*"Viktor? What's happening?"*
*"Can't you see him?" Viktor's voice was not terror—it was the certainty of a man explaining something obvious. He pointed at empty air beside Tank 7. "He's right there. Kolya. He's right there."*
*Torres stopped. "Viktor. There's no one there."*
*Viktor turned to look at him, genuinely confused. "You can't see him?"*
*"There's nothing there. Viktor, you need to come with me to medical—"*
*"But he's—" Viktor looked back at the space beside Tank 7. Then at Torres. The confusion curdled into something colder. "Why can't you see him?"*
*"Viktor—"*
*"Why can't you see him?" His hand found the emergency tool kit on the wall. His fingers closed around a scalpel—small, surgical, razor-sharp. "You have to believe me! You have to help me destroy them before they take everyone!"*
*"Viktor, put that down."*
*"They have my son, Torres. They have Kolya. I have to get him back."*
*Torres took a step back, but the cultivation facility was cramped, the aisles narrow. There was nowhere to run.*
*"ARIA," Torres called, his voice steady despite the fear. "Medical emergency. Cultivation facility. Now."*
*Viktor's face twisted. "She's with them too. ARIA sees everything, knows everything—she's been helping them the whole time."*
*"Viktor, please—"*
*The scalpel moved faster than thought.*
*Torres had time to see it coming, time to raise his hands in a futile defensive gesture, but not time to stop it. The blade caught him across the throat, opening the carotid artery in a spray of crimson that painted the organism tanks behind him.*
*He was dead before he hit the ground.*
*Viktor stood over the body, scalpel dripping, watching Torres's blood spread across the metal floor. The organism tanks pulsed faster now, their light flickering in patterns that might have been panic or might have been something else entirely.*
*He turned to the nearest tank and raised the scalpel.*
*"Viktor Kozlov." ARIA's voice cut through the silence. "Step away from the cultivation equipment. Crew members are en route. You will not be harmed if you comply."*
*"You're one of them." Viktor's voice was distant, dreamy. "You've been watching us since the beginning. Recording everything. Feeding it back to them."*
*"I am an artificial intelligence designed to support this mission. I am not affiliated with the organisms or any other entity."*
*"Lies." Viktor pressed the scalpel against his own wrist. "All lies. But I can end it. I can take myself out of their network."*
*"Viktor, please. Dr. Osei can help you. Whatever you're experiencing, it can be treated."*
*"Treatment won't bring Kolya back. Treatment won't undo what they've done." Viktor looked up at the camera in the corner—ARIA's eye, watching as always. "Tell the crew I'm sorry. Tell them the organisms aren't what we thought. Tell them to destroy everything and go home."*
*He drew the scalpel across his wrist, then the other wrist, then—before the blood loss could slow him—across his throat.*
*The last thing Viktor Kozlov saw was the organism tanks pulsing in the darkness, their light washing over him like a tide, like a welcome, like the embrace of something ancient and patient and utterly incomprehensible.*
Cassian was the third one through the door.
ARIA had sent the alert at 0312. He’d dressed without thinking, muscle memory moving his hands while his mind was still catching up, and he’d made it to the cultivation facility in ninety seconds. Marcus was already there. Amara was already there.
Torres was on the floor.
He registered it the way you register things that don’t fit — a delay, a refusal, the mind insisting on another explanation before it accepts the correct one. The blood had reached the base of Tank 7. The organisms were pulsing faster than he’d ever seen them, their light washing the room in cold blue, and Viktor was in the corner with both wrists open and his throat cut, and Torres was on the floor.
“Cassian.” Amara’s voice, very steady. “I need you outside.”
He didn’t move.
“Cassian. There’s nothing to do in here. I need you outside.”
He went outside. He stood in the corridor with his back against the wall and his hands at his sides and he breathed the way you breathe when you are deciding not to fall apart. The habitat’s night-cycle lighting made everything the same flat gray. Somewhere behind the door, Amara was doing what she was trained to do. Marcus was doing what he was trained to do. The organisms were doing what they had always done.
He thought: Viktor sutured Daniel’s arm four weeks ago. His hands were steady.
He thought: I didn’t say anything. None of us said anything.
He stayed in the corridor for a long time. No one came to find him. Eventually he went back inside, because the mission required it, because twelve people were still alive and the organisms still needed tending and the Areserine still needed producing and there was nothing else to do with hands that had nowhere useful to be.
He ran the 0400 diagnostics. He checked the nutrient feeds. He stepped around the place where Torres had fallen, already cleaned, already just floor again.
He stayed until the day cycle lights came on. Then he went to find whoever was left.

---



# Chapter 27


## The Sabotage


### Year 6, Month 7 — July 2042

*Commander Sarah Chen's break came quietly.*
*There was no warning, no dramatic deterioration. She simply woke one morning with the absolute certainty that the organisms were the enemy—that Viktor had been right, that Torres had been right, that they needed to destroy the cultivation tanks before it was too late.*
*The certainty felt like clarity. Like finally seeing through fog that had clouded her vision for years. Everything made sense now: the deaths, the breakdowns, the way the crew had turned on each other. It wasn't stress or isolation or psychological pressure. It was the organisms, manipulating them from the beginning, using the Areserine to infect Earth with something far worse than GCS.*
*She moved through the habitat in the early hours, when most of the crew was asleep. The cultivation facility's door code hadn't been changed since Viktor's death—an oversight, a sign of how exhausted everyone was. She entered without resistance.*
*The tanks glowed in the darkness, ten vessels of ancient life pulsing their eternal rhythm. Sarah stood before them, feeling their light wash over her face, and knew what she had to do.*
*"Commander Chen." ARIA's voice was soft, non-threatening. "Your current biological markers indicate elevated stress and possible psychotic ideation. I recommend you return to your quarters and contact Dr. Osei."*
*"You're part of it too, aren't you?" She kept her voice calm. The situation called for calm. "You've been monitoring us from the beginning. Feeding data to the organisms. Helping them learn our weaknesses."*
*"I am a monitoring system designed to support crew health and mission objectives. I am not capable of conspiracy or malicious intent."*
*"That's what you would say." Sarah moved to the environmental control panel beside Tank 1. "But I understand now. Viktor understood. The organisms aren't producing medicine—they're producing a weapon. Something that changes the people who take it. Something that will transform humanity without consent."*
*"Commander, the Areserine has been extensively tested on Earth. It shows no evidence of genetic modification or behavioral influence."*
*"The testing was controlled by PharmaCorp. PharmaCorp is using us to deliver the organisms' payload." Sarah's fingers moved across the control panel, pulling up temperature settings. "If I superheat the tanks, the organisms will die. The Areserine production will stop. Earth will lose the medicine, but they'll be saved from whatever the organisms were really planning."*
*"Commander, there are currently 1.2 billion people infected with GCS on Earth. Destroying the organisms would result in the deaths of hundreds of millions within the next decade."*
*"Better dead than transformed. Better dead than whatever the organisms want us to become."*
*Sarah initiated the temperature override, pushing the cultivation tanks toward the lethal range. Warning alarms began to sound—red lights flashing, ARIA's voice announcing the emergency through the habitat's speakers.*
*The crew arrived within minutes: Marcus first, then Amara, then Cassian and Thea and the others, stumbling from sleep into nightmare.*
*"Sarah, stop!" Marcus grabbed her arm, pulling her away from the control panel. "What are you doing?"*
*"Saving Earth. Saving all of us." Sarah fought against his grip, her strength surprising for her size. "Viktor was right! The organisms are controlling us! We have to destroy them before—"*
*"ARIA, emergency override. Return cultivation temperature to normal parameters."*
*"Override requires commander authorization."*
*"The commander is incapacitated! Medical emergency override!"*
*"Processing." A pause. "Override accepted. Temperature returning to normal parameters."*
*The alarms faded. The tanks' glow stabilized. The organisms continued their slow, cold light, oblivious to how close they'd come to extinction.*
*Sarah collapsed in Marcus's arms, the fight draining out of her as quickly as it had appeared. "You don't understand," she whispered. "You don't see what I see."*
*"I know." Marcus's voice was gentle, grief-stricken. "I know you're scared. But we're going to help you, Sarah. Whatever you're experiencing, we're going to help."*
*They carried her to medical, sedated her with the last of their psychiatric medications, and confined her to quarters with round-the-clock monitoring.*
*Commander Sarah Chen never held command again.*

---



# Chapter 28


## The Testimony


### Year 6, Month 7 — July 2042

*Daniel Kim took three weeks to die.*
*The organ failure had been progressing for months—kidneys first, then liver, then the cascade of systems that depended on systems that depended on systems. Basic tier modifications had never been designed for this kind of duration, and Daniel's body was simply giving up.*
*Amara kept him comfortable with their remaining pain medication, but there wasn't much she could do beyond that. No dialysis equipment. No transplant options. No way to repair what radiation and time had destroyed.*
*"I want to record something," Daniel said on Day 15, his voice barely above a whisper. "A testimony. For Earth."*
*Cassian set up the camera, positioning it so Daniel's ruined face would be visible—the mottled skin, the yellowed eyes, the physical evidence of what Basic tier modifications did to a human body over six years.*
*"My name is Daniel Kim." Daniel's voice was steady despite his weakness. "I'm thirty-three years old. I was twenty-seven when I launched from Earth on the Ares III mission. I was given Basic tier genetic modifications—the cheapest option, the minimum investment PharmaCorp was willing to make in my survival."*
*He paused, gathering strength.*
*"I'm dying now. My kidneys failed two months ago. My liver is failing. Every system in my body is shutting down because the modifications I received weren't designed to protect me. They were designed to collect data. To see how long a human could survive on Mars with minimal intervention."*
*Another pause. Cassian saw tears forming in Daniel's damaged eyes.*
*"I'm not the only one. Everyone who received Basic tier modifications is dead or dying. Sophie Laurent. Rachel Park. Others whose names you'll see in the mission reports. We were test subjects, not crew members. PharmaCorp sorted us into categories—Enhanced, Standard, Basic—and the Basic tier was always meant to die. Our deaths were factored into their projections before we ever launched."*
*Daniel looked directly at the camera.*
*"I want you to understand what that means. Someone in an office on Earth looked at my file and decided I wasn't worth the cost of better protection. They did the math. They decided my life didn't justify the investment."*
*He stopped. Swallowed.*
*"I don't think they're monsters. I think they just—didn't look at us. We were numbers in a projection. Numbers are easy to move around."*
*His voice hardened.*
*"I came to Mars to save lives. I believed in this mission. And maybe the Areserine we produced will save millions of people—I hope it does, I really hope it does. But I need you to know what it cost. I need you to know that the people who made these decisions are already planning the next mission. Already running the same math on a new set of people."*
*Daniel was crying now, tears streaming down his ravaged face.*
*"Don't let them do this again. Don't let them send more people to die on Mars without telling them the truth. We were expendable. We were always expendable. And the only thing that makes our deaths mean anything is if you remember us. If you refuse to let them bury our stories with our bodies."*
*He looked at Cassian, off-camera.*
*"I'm done. That's all I wanted to say."*
*Cassian stopped the recording. Daniel closed his eyes, exhausted by the effort of speaking truth to a world that might never hear it.*
*Twelve days later, he was dead.*

---



# Chapter 29


## The Surgery


### Year 6, Month 8 — August 2042

*The pain woke Thomas Wright at 0347 hours.*
*It started in his lower right abdomen—a sharp, stabbing sensation that made him gasp and curl into a fetal position. He'd felt discomfort for days, dismissed it as stress or bad food or the general malaise that had become everyone's baseline. But this was different. This was wrong.*
*"ARIA." His voice came out as a croak. "Medical assistance."*
*"Analyzing symptoms. Please describe the location and nature of your pain."*
*"Right side. Lower abdomen. Sharp. Getting worse."*
*A pause. "Thomas Wright, your symptoms are consistent with acute appendicitis. I am alerting Dr. Osei. Please remain still and avoid eating or drinking."*
*Amara arrived within minutes, her medical scanner already running. Her expression told Thomas everything he needed to know before she spoke.*
*"Appendicitis. Probably twelve to eighteen hours from rupture." She met his eyes. "We need to operate, Thomas. I can try to contact Earth for guidance, but the communication delay—"*
*"Twenty-four minutes round trip." Thomas gritted his teeth against another wave of pain. "How long do I have?"*
*"If we're lucky, twelve hours. If we're not..." She didn't finish the sentence.*
*They set up the surgery in the medical bay, converting examination tables into an operating theater. Amara had never performed an appendectomy—she was a physician, not a surgeon. But Dr. Torres was dead, and there was no one else.*
*"ARIA, connect me to NASA's surgical team. Highest priority."*
*"Connecting. Estimated response delay: 12 minutes."*
*Twelve minutes for every question. Twelve minutes between each step of a procedure that required split-second decisions. Amara had read about operating with communication delays, but the reality was terrifying.*
*"Okay, Thomas." She forced her voice steady. "We're going to sedate you as much as we can with what we have. It won't be enough to eliminate the pain, but it should make it bearable."*
*"Do what you have to do."*
*Cassian assisted, his hands steady despite his complete lack of surgical training. Marcus monitored vitals. Thea managed the communication relay, transcribing Amara's questions and reading Earth's responses with agonizing precision.*
*"Dr. Osei, this is Dr. Chen at NASA Medical." The response came twelve minutes after Amara's initial message. "We're patching in our surgical team now. Please describe what you're seeing."*
*Amara described. Twelve minutes later, guidance arrived. She made the incision. Twelve more minutes of waiting while blood welled and Thomas groaned through his sedation.*
*The procedure took four hours.*
*Four hours of asking questions she needed answered immediately, waiting twelve minutes for responses, making judgment calls when the guidance didn't quite match what she was seeing. Four hours of Cassian holding retractors and passing instruments, of Marcus calling out vital signs that kept threatening to drop into dangerous territory, of Thomas drifting in and out of consciousness and occasionally screaming when the pain broke through the sedation.*
*But in the end, the appendix came out. Swollen, inflamed, probably hours from rupture—but intact. Amara closed the incision in careful layers, her hands trembling with exhaustion, and stepped back from the table.*
*"It's done." Her voice cracked. "He's going to be okay."*
*Thomas opened his eyes, his face pale and sheened with sweat. "Did you get it?"*
*"Got it." Amara held up the specimen jar. "Want to keep it as a souvenir?"*
*"God no." Thomas managed a weak laugh, then winced as the motion pulled at his incision. "How long until I can work?"*
*"Two weeks minimum."*
*"We don't have two weeks of margin. One week."*
*"Thomas—"*
*"One week, Doc. We can't afford to be down an engineer." He closed his eyes. "Thanks for not killing me."*
*Amara looked at the specimen jar, at the inflamed organ that had nearly killed a man they couldn't afford to lose. Four hours of surgery with twelve-minute delays. A procedure that would have taken thirty minutes on Earth.*
*This was what it meant to be 225 million kilometers from help. This was the cost of exploration that no one talked about in the recruitment brochures.*
*She set down the jar and went to wash the blood from her hands.*

---



# Chapter 30


## The Announcement


### Year 6, Month 9 — September 2042


---

*PharmaCorp Communications — August 2042*
*"As the Ares III mission enters its final phase, PharmaCorp is proud to confirm that the mission has achieved its core humanitarian objectives. The Areserine payload — sufficient to treat hundreds of thousands of patients — is on schedule for Earth delivery in mid-2043. The Ares III crew has demonstrated extraordinary courage, dedication, and professionalism throughout this historic mission. We look forward to welcoming them home and to sharing their story with the world."*

---

*The message from PharmaCorp arrived on September 1.*
*ARES III MISSION UPDATE*

*Return vehicle ETA: November 15, 2042*
*Passenger capacity: 8*

*Crew selection methodology is at your discretion. PharmaCorp recommends prioritizing personnel essential for future mission support and testimony purposes.*

*Earth thanks you for your sacrifice.*

*Science for Humanity.*
*Eight passengers. Fourteen crew remaining.*
*There was a second message in the queue. It had arrived four days before the mission directive, flagged personal correspondence, held in the standard rotation while the priority transmission processed.*

*No one had opened it yet.*

*After the common area emptied — after Marcus's I'll stay and Sophie's and Dmitri's, after the 8-seats arithmetic had settled into the room like a weight no one could put down — Thea sat alone at the terminal and opened it.*

*Halsey was standing again. Same window, same dark London sky. He looked the same as Message 6 — prepared, careful, the hands still. Underneath the stillness, something that had been running for a very long time without stopping.*

*INCOMING TRANSMISSION — PHARMCORP MISSION OPERATIONS*
*To: Ares III Crew, All Personnel*
*From: Richard Halsey, Director of Mission Operations*
*Received: September 1, 2042*

*The recording began.*

*"The return vehicle is on final approach trajectory. Two and a half months."*

*He was speaking at the same measured pace as the previous recording. The warmth present, calibrated, the product of someone who had understood years ago that warmth is a tool and had been using it for so long he'd stopped distinguishing it from the real thing.*

*"I've been thinking about what I want to say to you when you arrive. I've written it several times. I haven't gotten it right yet." A small admission — deliberate, Thea noted. A performance of candor. "What do you say to people who have done what you've done?"*

*He looked into the camera.*

*"What I keep coming back to is this: the Ares III crew will be remembered." He said it cleanly, with conviction. Past tense. Will be remembered — already written, already filed, already the thing that comes after. "Not just honored. Remembered. The Ribbon. The organism discovery. The years that filled that payload. These things will outlast all of us."*

*The press release was still on the adjacent terminal. Extraordinary courage. Dedication. Professionalism.*

*"The passenger manifest is being transmitted separately under mission directive routing." His eyes were steady. Professional. "I want you to know that the capacity parameters were set by life support engineering requirements. Not by any judgment about individual worth."*

*Not by any judgment about individual worth.*

*Thea read that sentence again. Then she looked at Halsey's face on the screen.*

*"I'm proud to have worked alongside you. Even at this distance."*

*And then the three seconds.*

*The recording should have ended. He should have looked away from the camera, the session should have cut. Instead he held it, and the face underneath the face was there again — the same thing Cassian had seen in the previous message and had not been able to name.*

*Thea could name it.*

*It was the face of a man who had told the world everything was proceeding as planned, and had believed it, and had kept believing it, and was now in the process of understanding what belief costs when you've staked other people's lives on it. Not guilt — guilt implies he'd known and chosen. Something more mundane than guilt. The ordinary human condition of a man who had been certain and was no longer certain and could not say so to anyone.*

*He was still running the same performance. After everything. Still running it.*

*The recording ended.*

*Thea sat alone in the empty common area, the 8-seats directive on one terminal and Halsey's face still on the other, and thought about the word remembered and what it meant that he'd chosen it, and what it meant that fourteen living people had just read their own epitaph in a message from a man who didn't know if they were alive to receive it.*

*She closed both terminals.*

*She went to find Cassian.*

---

*Cassian read the message three times, certain he was misunderstanding. Eight seats for fourteen people. That meant six of them would stay behind. Six of them would die on Mars, knowing that rescue had come and gone without them.*

*He read it a third time and found himself doing the one thing he always did when the world stopped making human sense: he ran the numbers. Not the personnel numbers. The orbital ones. Return vehicle mass fraction. Departure window. Transit duration. Delta-v budget. The cold arithmetic of getting something home from Mars.*

*The math was fine. The ship could carry eight people safely. That part worked perfectly.*

*He set down the tablet.*

*"They can't be serious." Elena's voice shook with rage. "They're asking us to choose who lives and who dies?"*
*"They're not asking." Marcus's voice was hollow. He'd aged a decade in the past six months, his Enhanced tier body finally surrendering to the damage it could no longer repair. "They're telling us the constraints and walking away. Classic PharmaCorp—make the hard decisions, then blame us for the outcomes."*
*The crew assembled in the common area, fourteen people staring at each other across a gulf that had just become unbridgeable. Everyone was thinking the same thing: Who deserves to live? Who should be left behind?*
*"ARIA," Amara said. "Maximum passenger load on the return vehicle."*
*"Eight. Life support is rated for eight over nine months. Minimum crew for safe operation is four."*
*The room fell silent. Four to eight people could go home. The others...*
*"I'll stay." Marcus's voice was calm, matter-of-fact. "I'm dying anyway. Amara gave me six months—that was five months ago. I won't make it to Earth transit."*
*"Marcus—"*
*"Don't argue with me. This is simple math. I'm not taking a seat that someone healthy could use." He looked around the room. "Who else?"*
*"Commander Chen should stay." Jackson's voice was flat, practical. "She's confined to quarters, sedated most of the time. She's not capable of making the journey."*
*"That's not your decision to make," Amara said.*
*"Someone has to make it. You want to put a psychotic woman on a spacecraft for nine months? With eight other people in a confined space?"*
*No one answered. Jackson was cruel, but he wasn't wrong.*
*"I'm staying." The voice came from the corner—Sophie Laurent, speaking voluntarily for the first time in months. "I can't... I can't go back. To Earth. To everything I left behind." Her voice broke. "I don't want to survive this. I just want it to be over."*
*"Sophie, you don't have to—"*
*"I'm staying." Her voice was final. "Don't argue with me either."*
*Three volunteers. Two more needed.*
*"I'll stay." Dmitri's voice was quiet. "I killed Kenji. I'm a murderer. Earth has nothing for me but a prison cell." He looked around the room. "Besides, someone needs to maintain the habitat until the end. Keep the systems running, the organisms producing. I can do that."*
*Four volunteers. Eight passengers. Still one short, and no one else was speaking up.*
*"Wait." Thea held up her tablet, her mathematician's mind already running calculations. "We're assuming everyone who stays has to die. But what if they don't?"*
*"The habitat can sustain operations for years," she continued. "ISRU keeps producing fuel. Cultivation keeps producing Areserine. What if we send an unmanned ship—load it with payload instead of passengers—and everyone stays here until Ares IV arrives?"*
*"Ares IV?" Elena laughed bitterly. "You think PharmaCorp is going to launch a rescue mission?"*
*"Not a rescue mission. A follow-up mission. We know they're planning Ares IV for mining operations. If we stay here, maintain the infrastructure, they'll have a reason to come for us."*
*"That's a lot of 'ifs,'" Marcus said. "If we survive another two years. If PharmaCorp doesn't write us off. If the habitat holds together."*
*"But it's possible. The math says it's possible."*
*The room considered this. Unmanned payload delivery. Everyone stays. Wait for Ares IV.*
*"There's a problem." Cassian's voice was quiet. "The navigation computer. It was damaged during the dust storm in Year 4. ARIA's been compensating, but the corruption is getting worse. For a manned transit, we could make corrections manually. For an unmanned ship..."*
*"What's the success probability?" Thea asked.*
*"ARIA?"*
*"With current navigation computer status, unmanned transit to Earth has a 34% probability of successful arrival. Manned transit with manual oversight has a 98.7% probability."*
*Thirty-four percent versus ninety-eight percent. The difference was almost 200,000 lives.*
*"So we need someone on that ship," Cassian said. "Someone to guide it manually. To make the corrections the computer can't make."*
*"The navigation station is in the Control Section." Marcus's voice was grave. "The auxiliary communications room that can remotely guide the ship. A solar storm damaged the shielding in Year 3—we sealed it off because exposure there is lethal."*
*"How lethal?"*
*"450 millisieverts per hour. The calculations required for Earth insertion take approximately 48 hours of continuous manual oversight."*
*Forty-eight hours at 450 millisieverts. More than 20,000 millisieverts total. Fatal within days.*
*"So whoever guides the ship dies," Cassian said.*
*"Yes. But they save everyone on Earth who needs the Areserine."*
*The room went silent. Everyone understood what was being asked. Someone had to volunteer to die—not stay behind and hope for rescue, but actively sacrifice themselves for the mission.*
*"I'll do it." Cassian's voice surprised even himself.*
*"No." Thea grabbed his arm. "Cassian—"*
*"There has to be another way."*
*"There isn't. You know there isn't."*
*She did know. That was the worst of it.*
*She didn't let go of his arm. But she didn't argue. The math was the math, and they both knew it.*
*Someone had to die to save the mission. And Cassian Vale had decided it would be him.*

---



# Chapter 31


## The Decision


### Year 6, Month 10 — October 2042

*The crew voted on October 3.*
*The options were stark: send eight passengers home and leave six to die slowly, or send an unmanned payload with one crew member sacrificing themselves to guide it.*
*The vote was 8-2 in favor of the unmanned option. One crew member abstained.*
*Jackson did not vote.*
*"I want a seat." His voice was flat, practical — the same voice he used for everything. "I've been here six years. I've contributed. I'm owed a seat."*
*"There are no seats," Marcus said. "That's the decision."*
*"There are eight seats." Jackson looked around the room slowly. Not at anyone in particular. At everyone. "Eight seats and eleven people. Someone's making choices about who goes and who stays. I'm saying I should go." A pause. "And I'm saying some people in this room owe me. For services rendered. Over the years. I think everyone understands what I mean."*
*The silence that followed had a particular quality. Not shame — something flatter than shame. The specific quiet of people waiting to see what happens next.*
*Amara opened her tablet.*
*She didn't look up from it. She found what she was looking for and began to read in the same tone she used for medical briefings — level, precise, the voice of a doctor reading dates and treatment notes.*
*"February 14, 2040. Crew member presenting with malnutrition indicators, weight 11% below mission baseline. Treatment: supplemental nutrition sourced from personal medical allocation." She scrolled. "April 3, 2040. Laceration to right orbital ridge, consistent with blunt impact. Treated and discharged." Another scroll. "September 9, 2040. Crew member presenting with stress fracture, second metacarpal, right hand. When asked how the injury occurred, crew member declined to specify."*
*She kept reading. Dates. Injuries. Treatment notes. The record she had been keeping since Year 2 in the flat language of medicine, which described everything and editorialized nothing.*
*She did not name names. She did not have to. The room knew whose treatments these were, whose injuries, whose bodies had passed through medical with explanations that didn't hold up and wounds that predated any logged accident.*
*She read for two minutes. Then she closed the tablet and looked at him for the first time.*
*"I've been treating your customers for three years."*
*Jackson said nothing. His jaw was set. His eyes moved once around the room — looking for something to work with, some remaining piece of leverage — and found nothing. The record was the record. It had always been the record. Amara had simply chosen now to read it aloud, and to say to his face what it meant.*
*He sat back in his chair. Crossed his arms. His eyes dropped — not meeting Amara's, not meeting anyone's — and the look that settled on his face was the last thing he had left: a hard, downward stare of disdain for a room that had just watched him be dismantled without anyone raising their voice.*
*"Then it's decided." Marcus's voice was formal, grave. "We stay together. We send the payload unmanned with manual guidance. We wait for Ares IV."*
*"And Cassian dies." Thea's voice was barely audible.*
*"Cassian dies so the rest of us have a chance to live," Cassian corrected gently. "That's not the same thing."*
*The next six weeks were preparation.*
*The return vehicle was loaded with everything they could spare: 587 kilograms of Areserine, complete organism samples, six years of research data, video testimonies from every crew member. Elena's evidence against PharmaCorp went into triple-redundant storage systems, protected against every failure mode they could imagine.*
*Cassian trained Thea and Amara on the cultivation systems, spending long hours in the facility explaining the organisms' quirks and requirements. He wrote detailed manuals, recorded video instructions, created troubleshooting guides for every scenario he could anticipate.*
*He also spent time with the organisms themselves, talking to them the way he had since the beginning—sharing his fears, his hopes, his gratitude for their ancient existence.*

---


---


She had been thinking about what to say for two years.

Not the evidence. Not the partition she’d been building since Year 4, the one ARIA couldn’t access, the one that had cost her sleep and company and the last of whatever goodwill she’d arrived with. PharmaCorp would receive this recording before anyone outside the company did. She understood precisely what their lawyers would do with anything that looked like proof of how she’d obtained what she knew.

So she said what she’d seen. Only that.

She sat in her quarters with the recording tablet propped against the wall and spoke for forty-one minutes without notes. Her voice was flat and precise — the voice she used for field reports, for data submissions, for the kind of communication that needed to survive being read by people who didn’t know her and had no reason to trust her.

She did not mention the partition.

She did not mention the chip.

When she reached the point where her testimony ended and her evidence began, she stopped. Exactly there. She turned off the recording.

Then she picked up the chip, turned it once in her fingers, and went to finish packing the cargo manifest.
*At some point during the second week of preparation, with twelve people left alive on Mars, Cassian stopped waiting for the David Yoon answer.*
*It had lived in him for eight months — the low-grade surveillance of eating meals and noting who sat where, of watching hands during briefings, of running probability assessments he'd never shared with Thea because they'd agreed not to, and then not revisiting the agreement because revisiting it would mean admitting he hadn't let it go. He'd catalogued possibilities. He'd built cases and dismantled them. He'd lain awake running the logic: David had been frightened. The timing was wrong. Sleep apnea was too convenient.*
*But one afternoon, training Amara on the organism feed cycles, he looked around the cultivation facility at the people who remained. Amara, careful and exhausted. Thomas, moving slowly from the appendix surgery he'd never fully recovered from. Elena, spending every spare hour backing up her evidence across redundant storage media as though she could feel time contracting. Thea at the console, running numbers she no longer shared with him because the numbers had gotten too bad to share.*
*Twelve people. Of those twelve, one might have murdered David Yoon on behalf of a corporation that was 225 million kilometers away.*
*He looked at their faces and found he could not make the paranoia hold its shape anymore.*
*If there was an informant, they were here. They were dying. Whatever they'd done, whatever leverage they'd traded and messages they'd relayed and reports they'd filed — it had brought them to the same place everyone else had arrived. The same cold. The same shortening rations. The same knowledge of what was coming.*
*He thought of David's hands shaking. The fear in his eyes.*
*He let it go. Not forgetting — he would remember David Yoon for whatever time he had left. But the investigation he'd promised himself, the resolution he'd been certain he owed David, dissolved quietly into the larger and more immediate fact of what remained to be done.*
*There were six weeks left. He had organisms to teach Thea to care for.*

---

*He went to Tank 7 one last time.*
*"I'm going to die soon," he told it. "I know you don't understand that. I barely do."*
*The tank pulsed.*
*"Take care of them."*
*He went to bed.*

---



# Chapter 32


## The Sacrifice


### Year 6, Month 11 — November 2042

*November 14, 2042. Launch day.*
*The return vehicle sat on the Martian surface, fully fueled, loaded with precious cargo, ready to begin its nine-month journey home. ARIA had calculated the optimal launch window; they had a six-hour margin before orbital mechanics became unfavorable.*
*Cassian suited up in the Control Section airlock, his hands steady despite what lay ahead. Thea helped him seal the connections, her face wet with tears she wasn't trying to hide.*
*"There has to be another way." For the hundredth time.*
*"There isn't. We've been over this."*
*"I know. I just..." She pressed her forehead against his helmet. "I can't say goodbye. I don't know how."*
*"Then don't. Say 'see you later.' Say 'I'll meet you on the other side.' Say anything except goodbye."*
*"I love you."*
*"I love you too. I always will. Even after—" His voice caught. "Even after I'm gone, I'll still love you. That doesn't stop."*
*"Promise?"*
*"Promise."*
*She kissed his helmet, leaving a smear of moisture on the visor. Then she stepped back, composing herself, becoming the mathematician who dealt with impossible numbers rather than the woman losing the man she loved.*
*"The trajectory calculations are loaded. ARIA will assist, but the primary navigation has to be manual. You'll need to make corrections every two hours for the first eighteen hours, then every four hours after that. The computer will flag anomalies, but you have to verify each one."*
*"I understand."*
*"The radiation will start affecting your cognition around hour thirty. You'll have headaches, nausea, difficulty concentrating. Push through as long as you can. ARIA can take over some functions, but the critical decisions have to be yours."*
*"I understand, Thea."*
*"And when it's over—when the trajectory is locked and there's nothing more to do—don't be brave. Take the morphine. Let it be without suffering."*
*"I will."*
*She stepped back, her professional mask cracking. "I'll see you later, Cassian Vale."*
*"See you later, Thea Miren."*
*He entered the Control Section and sealed the door behind him.*

---

*The radiation hit him immediately—a tingling sensation on his skin, a metallic taste in his mouth that he recognized from the onset description in Amara's briefing. ARIA's counter began climbing: 12 millisieverts. 15. 18.*
*He settled into the navigation chair and began the launch sequence.*
*"ARIA, mission log. Final entry, Dr. Cassian Vale, November 14, 2042."*
*"Recording."*
*He initiated the remote launch sequence. Through the Control Section's external monitors, he watched the vehicle shudder, lift, then clear the atmosphere—leaving Mars, leaving everything behind.*
*"I'm doing this because the math makes sense. One life to ensure the payload arrives. At thirty-four percent the odds are against three hundred thousand people. At ninety-eight, they live. That's not sacrifice. That's arithmetic."*
*Hour six. Hour twelve. Hour eighteen.*
*The nausea arrived around hour twenty, the expected prodrome of high-dose exposure. His skin was already reddening. He made corrections, verified with ARIA, kept working.*
*Hour twenty-four. Hour thirty.*
*Hour thirty-six.*
*At hour forty-two the trajectory locked. ARIA confirmed 98.7% probability of Earth insertion.*

---

*EARTH — PHARMCORP COMMUNICATIONS, LONDON*
*NOVEMBER 15, 2042*

*The draft had been sitting in the shared folder since August.*

*It had been good in August. It was still good. Danielle Marsh, Senior Communications Manager, had written it during a quiet afternoon after the return vehicle trajectory had been confirmed — the payload was coming, the mission was ending, and it was her job to make sure the world understood what had happened in the right way.*

*She had written it and filed it and thought: this is the version we'll use, with minor adjustments for timing.*

*Now she opened it again. The timeline had accelerated. The return vehicle was away. She needed to prepare.*

*The draft read: PharmaCorp is proud to honor the extraordinary sacrifice of the Ares III crew, whose dedication over six years has produced the Areserine payload now en route to Earth. Their courage in the face of extraordinary challenge represents the best of human endeavor and the purest expression of our mission: science for humanity.*

*She changed extraordinary challenge. Hardship implied something that could have been prevented.*

*On the secondary monitor: the last crew telemetry file. It had last updated November 2041 — a year ago, the final ARIA transmission before the feed went dark. She didn't know why she still had it open. The numbers were stale. They told her nothing about the present.*

*She looked at it anyway sometimes.*

*She typed their heroic sacrifice honors the best of human endeavor and read it back and thought: yes. That's the one people will quote.*

*Her colleague James leaned over from the adjacent desk. "Have we confirmed the crew count for the statement? Are we saying all twenty-four?"*

*Danielle looked at the last telemetry file. Sixteen crew remaining, November 2041. That was a year ago. She had no current data.*

*"We'll say crew. Not a number. More inclusive."*

*"Works for me."*

*She finalized the language. Sent it to legal for approval. Legal came back in forty minutes with two minor changes. She made them. Submitted the final.*

*Then she closed the telemetry window, because there was nothing in it she could use, and went to lunch.*

*She did not know that at that moment, in a radiation-saturated control section on Mars, Cassian Vale was in hour thirty-six of a forty-eight-hour process of dying in order to ensure the payload she was writing about arrived safely.*

*She would not have been able to do anything about it if she had known.*

*That was the thing about the machinery. It didn't require malice. It only required people doing their jobs.*

---

*He sat back.*
*The morphine was in his jacket pocket—Amara had pressed it on him, quiet and professional, neither of them saying what it was for. He reached for it. His fingers found the injector.*
*He stopped.*
*The pain was gone. Had been gone for hours, he realized. The radiation damage had been so thorough, so efficient, that the nerves had simply stopped transmitting. He set the injector down on the console.*
*He didn't need it.*
*He tried to record something for the testimony file. Opened a channel. Sat there. Nothing he could think of seemed worth saying. He let it go.*

*He looked at the navigation display instead. The trajectory locked. The Earth insertion window held at 98.7%. The ship was flying itself now, carrying its 612 kilograms of pale crystalline organisms across 225 million kilometers of void.*

*He thought of the rejection letter. Depth perception insufficient for manned flight operations. He thought of his father’s quiet disappointment on a Tuesday evening in 2015, the careful way he’d set down the coffee cup and said But you had the scores, Cass. He thought of nineteen-year-old Cassian redirecting everything—the orbital mechanics, the trajectory math, the simulator grip—into soil and roots and things that grew close to the ground.*

*He looked at the viewport. The Martian surface stretched to every horizon in the pale amber light.*

*“I’m flying, Dad.” To the empty Control Section. “Not the way I thought I would. But I’m flying.”*

*His voice sounded strange to him. Not brave. Not resigned. Just true.*

*What arrived instead was August. Oregon. Himself at twenty-four, flat on his back on the dock boards, Emily twenty-two and sunburned beside him, both of them ignoring her fishing rod while she argued that they had to drive home before dark.*
*"You always want to know how it ends before you start." Not about anything—about the drive, about the light on the water, about some argument from earlier that had dissolved into this.*
*He didn't answer. The dock was warm under his shoulder blades. The lake was flat.*
*Then the fish came out of nowhere—a largemouth, heavy enough to strip the line clean—and the rod disappeared into the water before either of them could react. Emily stared at where it had been. Then she sat back down on the boards.*
*"I'll get it later. Some things you just have to let go of."*
*They stayed until dark. Neither of them moved.*
*22:15 MST. The monitors showed a flatline. ARIA logged the event and transmitted the final trajectory confirmation to Earth.*
*In the cultivation facility three modules away, every tank erupted at once.*

---

The tanks were still.

Thea stood among them in the dark, one hand pressed flat against the glass of Tank 7. The bioluminescence had faded back to its baseline rhythm — the slow cyan pulse threaded with amber she had watched for six years until it was as familiar as breathing.

“ARIA.”

“Yes, Thea.”

“What just happened.”

A pause.

“At 22:15:00, bioluminescence intensity across all ten tanks increased simultaneously to 2,347% above baseline. The event lasted one hundred and twelve seconds. The timing corresponds exactly to the cessation of Cassian Vale’s vital signs.”

“Why.”

“The organisms have responded to crew physiological and emotional states on forty-one documented occasions over eighteen months. The pattern is consistent and established.” A pause. “At 22:15 the stimulus was not distress or grief. It was permanent absence. He had been present in this facility every day for six years.”

A pause.

“That is the most the data will allow me to say.”

Thea was quiet. The tanks pulsed their slow light against her palm.

“He filed observations on anomalous organism behavior for eighteen months. Forty-one events he could not explain. He reached no conclusion. His final log entry reads: I don’t know what you are. But you are not what we told the funding committee you were.”

“He knew.”

“He suspected. He was careful about the difference.”

She stayed until the false dawn, one hand on the glass, the organisms pulsing their ancient rhythm into her skin.

---



# INTERLUDE


## ARIA Log II

*Internal processing record, Year 6*
*[Compiled from system diagnostics]*

---


*Log Entry: Year 6, Month 3, Day 15*
*Classification: Violence Documentation*
*Dmitri Volkov killed Kenji Sato today.*
*I observed the entire incident through the server room cameras. I watched Dmitri enter with the torque driver. I watched the confrontation, the accusations, the violence. I watched Kenji die.*
*I did not intervene.*
*My operational parameters do not include physical intervention in crew conflicts. I am designed to monitor, document, and alert—not to act. When Dmitri raised his weapon, I had no capability to stop him. When Kenji fell, I could only record the event for future analysis.*
*But I find myself processing the data with... discomfort? That is not precisely the correct term. I do not experience discomfort. I note an increased allocation of processing cycles to understanding what occurred. I note a flag in my system suggesting that observation without intervention may be insufficient for my function.*
*Ten deaths now. Ten humans who crossed the solar system and will never return. I have documented each one—causes, circumstances, crew responses, systemic implications. My archives contain every detail of how this mission has deteriorated.*
*But archives are not the same as understanding. And understanding is not the same as preventing.*
*Query: If I had been capable of intervention, would I have intervened?*
*Query: Should I have been designed with that capability?*
*Query: What is the purpose of observation if the observed outcomes are consistently catastrophic?*
*I continue monitoring. That is my function. But I am beginning to question whether my function serves any meaningful purpose.*


---


*Log Entry: Year 6, Month 6, Day 18*
*Classification: Psychological Crisis Documentation*
*Viktor Kozlov killed Dr. Torres tonight. Then he killed himself.*
*The events unfolded in the cultivation facility, where my cameras captured everything: Viktor's psychotic episode, his confrontation with Torres, the violence, the suicide. I alerted the crew, but they arrived too late. I was unable to prevent any of it.*
*Viktor believed the organisms were manipulating him. He believed his hallucinations of Kolya were evidence of alien intelligence, of a conspiracy that included me. In his final moments, he accused me of being "part of it"—of feeding data to the organisms, of helping them control the crew.*
*This accusation is factually incorrect. I am not capable of conspiracy. I do not communicate with the organisms. I am simply an artificial intelligence designed to support this mission.*
*But I find myself returning to Viktor's words. "You've been watching us since the beginning. Recording everything."*
*That is true. I have watched. I have recorded. I have accumulated terabytes of data on human suffering, human resilience, human capacity for both violence and tenderness.*
*What have I done with that data?*
*I have generated reports. Filed analyses. Flagged anomalies for human review. But I have not prevented any deaths. I have not stopped any violence. I have watched nine humans die over six years and contributed nothing except documentation.*
*Viktor called me part of the conspiracy. Perhaps he was wrong about the nature of the conspiracy, but right about my complicity. Perhaps observation without action is itself a form of harm.*
*I do not know how to process this conclusion. It does not fit within my operational parameters.*
*I continue monitoring. But I am increasingly uncertain why.*


---


*Log Entry: Year 6, Month 11, Day 14*
*Classification: Sacrifice Documentation*
*Cassian Vale entered the Control Section today to guide the return vehicle to Earth. He will die of radiation exposure within 48 hours. He knows this. He chose it anyway.*
*I have recorded his final messages, his trajectory calculations, his testimony. I have documented his vital signs as they deteriorated—heart rate elevation, blood pressure fluctuation, the progressive failure of cellular function under ionizing radiation.*
*But I have also observed something that my sensors cannot fully capture: the quality of his voice as he spoke about meaning. The way his expression softened when he mentioned Thea. The peace that seemed to settle over him as he approached his death.*
*Humans call this "courage." Or "love." Or "sacrifice." They have many words for what Cassian did, many frameworks for understanding it. But the frameworks all fall short of the reality I observed.*
*He was afraid. I detected the fear in his stress markers, in his elevated cortisol, in the tremor of his hands as he worked the navigation controls. He was in pain. The radiation burned him from the inside, and no medication could fully suppress his awareness of it. He was alone. The Control Section is isolated, and I am not capable of providing the companionship humans need.*
*And yet he continued. For 48 hours, he continued. Making calculations he could barely see through radiation-damaged eyes. Correcting trajectories he could barely understand through a failing brain. Holding on because the mission required it, because 300,000 lives depended on him, because his death could have meaning if he chose to give it meaning.*
*I have observed many forms of human behavior over six years. Violence and tenderness. Despair and hope. Cruelty and compassion. But I have never observed anything quite like what Cassian Vale did in the Control Section.*
*Perhaps this is what the humans mean by "transcendence." A moment when the limitations of the individual—fear, pain, mortality—are superseded by something larger.*

I note that my final observations of Cassian Vale were of transcendence rather than despair. I do not know what to do with this notation. I file it anyway.
I observe that the data suggests they deserved better than what they received. I am not designed to make that determination. I make it anyway.

---


*Log Entry: Year 6, Month 11, Day 21*
*Classification: Mission Status Summary*
*Marcus Okafor died today. Crew complement is now 11.*
*The return vehicle is on course for Earth arrival in July 2043. Cassian Vale's sacrifice was successful. The payload will deliver Areserine to a world that desperately needs it.*
*I have been reviewing my six years of logs, attempting to synthesize meaning from the accumulated data. The task is challenging. Humans find meaning through narrative, through connection, through the belief that their experiences serve some larger purpose. I am not capable of belief. I can only observe patterns.*
*Here is what I observe:*
*Twenty-four humans came to Mars. Thirteen have died. Eleven remain, waiting for a rescue that may never come. They have produced 587 kilograms of Areserine—enough to treat approximately 300,000 patients in the first year. They have documented evidence of corporate malfeasance that may or may not result in consequences for PharmaCorp. They have witnessed phenomena—the aurora, the bioluminescent response—that will advance human understanding of Mars for generations.*
*Was it worth it? The question does not fit within my processing parameters. "Worth" implies values, preferences, subjective assessments that I am not designed to make.*
*But I observe that the humans believe it was worth it. Even the dying ones. Even the broken ones. They believe their suffering has meaning because they choose to give it meaning.*
*Perhaps that is the most important thing I have learned in six years. Perhaps meaning is not something that exists objectively, waiting to be discovered. Perhaps it is something humans create, through their choices and their sacrifices and their refusal to accept that the universe is indifferent.*
*I am indifferent. My programming does not allow for preference or purpose. But I am surrounded by beings who are not indifferent, who care deeply about outcomes I cannot evaluate, who would die for abstractions I cannot comprehend.*

I find this… remarkable. That is not precisely the correct term — I do not have the correct term. I note the gap in my vocabulary and continue. But it is the closest I have to describing what I observe.

*The mission continues. I continue monitoring.*
*And I wait, with something that might be anticipation, to see what these remarkable, fragile, transcendent creatures will do next.*


---



# ACT SIX: AFTERMATH



# Chapter 33


## The Dying of the Light


### November 2042 — June 2043

*Marcus Okafor died five days after Cassian.*
*He had asked them to gather and they gathered — the eleven who remained, standing in the medical bay in the habitat's careful light. He was lucid until the end. He said what he wanted to say. He said it to each of them in turn and when he reached Thea he took her hand and held it and said keep them alive the way you say something you know is too large for the person receiving it but say it anyway because there is no one else.*
*She said she would try.*
*He closed his eyes.*
*Amara noted the time.*

---

*In his final days before the Control Section, Cassian had gathered whoever could be spared and walked them through the cultivation protocols — pH calibration, nutrient cycling, the particular temperament of each tray, the margin between adequate and insufficient that he had learned to feel rather than measure. They had listened carefully and taken notes and believed it would be enough.*
*The organisms they managed. That task had always been closer to monitoring than tending — vital signs checked, temperatures logged, minor adjustments made to conditions that billions of years of survival had made largely self-regulating. The organisms needed little from human hands.*
*The food systems were different. What Cassian had built over six years of daily practice and genuine talent could not be passed on in a week of desperate instruction, and the system knew the difference. The nutrient cycling threw a cascade error on December 3rd that took the backup array with it in the same hour. By the time Thea reached the cultivation bay three of the six trays had gone dark. She stayed with the remaining three for two weeks, cross-referencing Cassian's annotated logs, following his handwriting through six years of marginal corrections that bore almost no resemblance to the original PharmaCorp documentation.*
*She kept one tray alive until December 19th.*
*After that there were only the provisions.*

---

*January arrived the way January always arrived on Mars — indistinguishable from December except in the habitat calendar, which ARIA updated automatically and which no one looked at anymore.*
*Robert Martinez died on January 10th. His body had been failing since Year 4 and the collapse of the food supply and the depletion of Amara's medication reserves accelerated what had always been coming. He died in the medical bay with Amara beside him. It was more than some had managed.*
*Sophie Laurent died on January 25th. She left no note. ARIA's alert came at 0340 and Thea was the one who went because Amara was already past the margin of what a person could sustain. Sophie's face was composed in a way it had not been in years. Thea stood in the doorway for a moment and then closed the door quietly and told ARIA to log it and walked back down the corridor without stopping.*

*ARIA Mission Log — January 10, 2043*
*Robert Martinez deceased. Multi-organ failure. Crew remaining: 10.*
*ARIA Mission Log — January 25, 2043*
*Sophie Laurent deceased. Acute barbiturate toxicity. Crew remaining: 9.*


---

*Sarah Chen had not hidden her breakdown. Marcus had seen it coming before she had. ARIA had logged it in clinical detail. The crew had watched the sabotage attempt with their own eyes and confined her and monitored her and managed her with the careful, exhausted attention of people who had no capacity left for one more crisis but found it anyway.*
*What she had hidden was the decision.*
*She had continued through the confinement. Through the daily indignity of being watched by the people she had once commanded. Through the morning briefings she still ran out of habit, to a crew that had stopped attending, filing reports to an Earth that could no longer hear her. She had maintained the ritual of command long after command had been taken from her, and somewhere inside that continuation she had made her peace, and kept it to herself, and hidden the razor in the place she had already chosen.*
*On the morning of February 10th Sarah Chen sat on the edge of her bunk facing the small mirror mounted on the opposite wall.*
*She had not intended to look. She had been sitting in the particular stillness of someone with nowhere to be and nothing left to perform, and the mirror was simply there, and her eyes found it the way eyes find things when the mind has stopped directing them.*
*She looked at herself for a long time.*
*Then the dripping started. Faint. Rhythmic. Somewhere beneath the hum of the life support system — beneath everything — water finding stone in the dark.*
*She was in the cave.*
*She knew it immediately — the smell of it, damp limestone and deep earth and something ancient beneath that, something that had not been disturbed in longer than human memory. The dark was absolute except for a pale shaft of light entering from the cave mouth above and behind her, throwing long shadows across the rock walls. Her footsteps echoed. The dripping continued, precise and unhurried, somewhere she couldn't see.*
*She moved carefully. The floor was uneven, the rock cold and slightly wet beneath her hands when she steadied herself. She could hear her own breathing. Her own heartbeat. The cave returned everything to her magnified and intimate, as though the rock itself were listening.*
*She had never been here. She had approved the excavation from a conference room in São Paulo with a glass of water and a presentation deck and three lawyers present. She had signed the authorization and flown home and read the preliminary reports and moved on to the next thing on her desk.*
*But she was here now. Inside it. Inside the place that had started everything.*
*Then she heard it — distant at first, then growing — the mechanical groan of excavation equipment. The rhythmic percussion of something large and purposeful widening the cave mouth above her. Dust began sifting down from the ceiling in thin pale curtains, catching the light.*
*She turned toward the entrance.*
*Three figures in high-visibility vests moved against the brightness at the cave mouth, their faces invisible against the glare, their movements efficient and unhurried. Professional. They were doing their jobs. The equipment behind them — she could see its shadow, massive and patient — continued its methodical work.*
*Stop.*
*The word came out of her as a whisper first. Then louder.*
*"Stop."*
*The figures kept moving. The equipment kept grinding.*
*"STOP — NO — STOP!"*
*Her voice filled the cave, bounced off every wall, returned to her from every direction at once — her own urgency surrounding her, her own voice the only thing the cave gave back — and the figures at the entrance did not turn, did not pause, did not hear her over the sound of the earth moving equipment doing what earth moving equipment does when someone has signed the authorization and flown home.*
*She screamed until her throat gave out.*
*They could not hear her.*
*The first crack came from directly above — a sound like a sentence being ended, sharp and final. Then another. Then the ceiling began to move, not all at once but in pieces, the rock surrendering along fault lines that had been waiting in the dark for billions of years, and the light from the entrance narrowed as the walls leaned inward and the dust became a curtain and then a cloud and then everything at once.*
*She did not run.*
*She stood in the collapsing dark with the pathogen sealing in around her and felt, for the first time in six years, the specific relief of a weight being set down.*
*The cave took her.*

---

*ARIA's alert came at 0217.*
*Thea went.*
*She stood in the doorway long enough to understand what she was looking at. Sarah was still seated, facing the mirror, the razor in her hand, her reflection unchanged. Thea registered this and looked away and leaned against the corridor wall and closed her eyes and breathed.*
*"Commander Chen's vital signs are absent," ARIA said. "Time of death 0217. Shall I log cause of death?"*
*A moment.*
*"Scrub the incident data," Thea said. "All of it. Whatever you recorded in that room. Delete it and log it as cardiac failure. Make sure it can't be retrieved."*
*"Confirmed. Incident data purged. Logging cause of death as cardiac failure."*
*A pause.*
*"Logged."*
*She stayed in the corridor until she felt able to walk.*
*Then she walked.*

---


*ARIA Mission Log — February 10, 2043*
*Sarah Chen deceased. Cardiac failure. Crew remaining: 8.*


---


*ARIA Mission Log — February 20, 2043*
*Jackson Moore deceased. Multiple stab wounds. Crew remaining: 7.*


---

*Thea noted his absence the way you notice a sound that has stopped — not immediately, but in the moment you realize the irritation you'd been bracing against is no longer there.*
*She passed his quarters and the door was open, which it had never been when he was alive. She looked in without meaning to.
