The room had the specific blankness of a space already moving on: his accumulated provisions redistributed, his tools returned to general inventory, the small objects he'd arranged with the particular care of a man who believed in ownership reduced to nothing.*
*She stood in the doorway for a moment.*
*She did not feel what she expected to feel. She felt something quieter and less clean than relief — the complicated remainder of five years of proximity to a person she had never liked, would never grieve in any simple way, and could not now entirely reduce to the sum of what he'd done. He had been cruel. He had also been, in his own calculations, rational. He had beaten Sophie and traded in human desperation and looked at every crisis as leverage. He had also, in the end, been right about Chen.*
*She closed the door. Not for him. For the room.*

---


*ARIA Mission Log — March 5, 2043*
*Dmitri Volkov deceased. Radiation sickness, multi-organ failure. Crew remaining: 6.*

Note: Engineer Volkov transmitted 847 personal messages to his daughter Anya Volkov, Earth, over the course of the mission. PharmaCorp’s relay architecture blocked an undetermined number of transmissions in Years 4–6. Whether the blocked messages were subsequently delivered, reconstructed, or permanently lost is not recorded in any data available to this system. Engineer Volkov did not know the answer to this question when he died. This log cannot provide it.

---

*Maria Santos died on March 20th.*
*She had stopped eating in February — not as a statement, not dramatically, but with the quiet deliberation of someone who had made a private peace and was simply following it to its conclusion. Amara had argued with her. Thea had sat with her. Elena had left food outside her door each morning and found it untouched the next day.*
*Maria had smiled throughout. That was the thing Thea could not reconcile — the absolute serenity of it, as though Maria had found the one door in the habitat that opened onto something other than Mars.*
*On the morning of the 20th ARIA's alert came and Thea went and stood at the threshold for a long time. Then she reached in and took the small cross from its nail above the bunk and set it on Maria's chest beside her rosary and left her with her faith and her silence and her long-sought peace.*

*ARIA Mission Log — March 20, 2043*
*Maria Santos deceased. Voluntary starvation. Crew remaining: 5.*


---


*ARIA Mission Log — April 10, 2043*
*Isabella Torres deceased. Asphyxiation.*
*Thomas Wright deceased. Voluntary exposure — EVA without life support.*
*Crew remaining: 3.*


---

*Elena Vasquez recorded her final testimony on April 15th.*
*She had been recording in fragments for months — whenever she had the strength, adding to the archive she had spent years building. The REE evidence. The genetic tier documentation. The PharmaCorp communications. The testimonies of crew members now dead who had given her their words because someone needed to carry them. All of it had gone into the payload before launch — hidden inside triple-redundant storage, tucked among the Areserine containers and organism samples, on its way to Earth months before she recorded these final words. PharmaCorp would unpack the payload. They would not know what they were unpacking.*
*She came to find Thea afterward and they sat together in the cultivation facility for a while, in the cyan and amber light, not talking very much. Elena's hands were thin. Her face had the particular gauntness that Thea had stopped being able to look at directly.*
*"You'll outlast me," Elena said.*
*"I don't know that."*
*"I do." She looked at the tanks. "When it's just you — talk to them. They listen."*
*Thea looked at the organisms, still glowing, still counting time in their own incomprehensible way. "I know they do."*
*Elena almost smiled.*
*She died on May 10th, in her sleep. Six years of fighting, and the evidence was already gone — already traveling, already beyond anyone's reach or revision. There was nothing left to protect. She had done what she came to do.*

*ARIA Mission Log — May 10, 2043*
*Elena Vasquez deceased. Multi-organ failure, malnutrition. Crew remaining: 2.*


---

*Amara lasted ten more days.*
*Thea had known what she was doing for weeks. The portions that appeared outside her door each morning — smaller than they should be, smaller than Amara's share. The physician's careful deflections whenever Thea raised it, the professional pivot away from the subject with the skill of someone who had spent a lifetime delivering difficult truths to other people while concealing her own.*
*On the morning of May 19th ARIA's alert came and Thea sat on the edge of her bunk for a long time before she rose. She walked to Amara's quarters and stood in the doorway. Amara's face was relaxed in a way it had not been in years — the permanent furrow between her brows finally gone, the exhaustion finally put down.*
*On the small table beside her bunk was a single ration bar, still sealed. A folded piece of paper beneath it.*
*Thea unfolded the paper.*
*Eat this. Doctor's orders. — A.*
*She sat on the edge of the bunk for a long time with the note in her hands. Then she picked up the ration bar and ate it slowly in the silence, because Amara had asked her to, and because it was the last thing anyone would ever ask of her, and she was not going to refuse it.*

*ARIA Mission Log — May 19, 2043*
*Amara Osei deceased. Multi-organ failure, malnutrition. Crew remaining: 1.*


---

*Three days after Amara died, ARIA's alert came from the eastern corridor.*
*Thea had been expecting it and not expecting it — the way you expect a thing you have been refusing to expect. She rose and steadied herself against the bunk frame and made her way down the corridor with one hand trailing the wall.*
*Mei Tanaka was seated against the wall outside the biology lab, her back straight, her legs extended, her eyes closed. She looked as though she had sat down to think and found the thinking so absorbing that everything else had simply ceased to matter.*
*Thea stood and looked at her for a moment.*
*Then she lowered herself to the floor beside her, her own back finding the wall, and sat.*
*The corridor was quiet. The habitat breathed its steady breath. Somewhere down the passage the organisms pulsed their cyan and amber rhythm, patient and ancient and unmoved by any of it.*
*Thea did not cry. There was nothing left to cry with. She sat with her back against the wall and her legs extended beside Mei's and stared at the opposite wall with the blank attention of someone who has stopped waiting for the view to change.*
*After a while she reached over and took Mei's hand.*
*It was cold. She held it anyway. She sat there long enough that the habitat's light panels cycled through their full arc, long enough that ARIA ran two complete atmospheric checks, long enough that the thing she had been refusing to understand for months finished arriving and settled into her without resistance.*
*They were not going to be rescued.*
*There was no rescue coming. There had never been a rescue coming. There was only the mission and the mission was over and what remained was the interval between the ending and the end, and the interval was hers alone now, and it was not long.*
*She placed Mei's hand carefully back in her lap and pressed it once, gently.*
*Then she got up.*
*The corridor stretched ahead of her, empty and lit and breathing. She stood in it for a moment. Then she walked.*

---


*ARIA Mission Log — May 22, 2043*
*Mei Tanaka deceased. Multi-organ failure, malnutrition. Crew remaining: 0.*


---



# Chapter 34


## The Last Light

*The final hydroponic tray went dark on May 28th.*
*Thea noted it the way she noted everything now — from a distance, with the detached patience of someone for whom the arithmetic had already been completed. She had water. She had the organisms pulsing in their tanks, which would outlast everything, which had already outlasted everything.*
*She spent her days in the cultivation facility. Her hand against the glass. The pulse of something billions of years old that had watched an entire planet die and kept going anyway.*

---

*Thea woke the way she had been waking for weeks — without alarm, without purpose, the habitat's light panels cycling through their programmed morning sequence regardless of whether anyone needed them to.*
*She sat on the edge of the bunk for a moment. Then she stood.*
*The mirror caught her as she rose. Just a glance — the way you glance at something you've stopped expecting to recognize. Whatever looked back she left without comment.*
*Her footsteps were the loudest thing in the habitat. The ambient hum of the life support system filled the spaces between steps — steady, indifferent, designed to continue long after it had any reason to. Above her a light panel buzzed and flickered, throwing the corridor into brief irregular shadow before steadying again. She didn't look up.*
*She moved slowly. There was no other way to move.*

---

*The organisms were healthy.*
*She could see it in the pulse — steady, even, the cyan and amber rhythm unchanged from yesterday and the day before. She pressed her hand against the glass of Tank 7 for a moment. The pulse continued beneath her palm, patient and ancient and entirely unconcerned with what was happening on the other side of the glass.*
*She stayed a little while. Then she moved on.*

---

*She started with Dmitri's quarters. She had searched it twice already. She searched it again — behind the ventilation panel, beneath the floor plate near the bunk, inside the hollowed base of the small framed photograph of Anya that still stood on his shelf.*
*Nothing.*
*Jackson's room she moved through quickly. Whatever Jackson had accumulated over six years had been found and consumed long ago.*
*In the third room she found it tucked inside a folded jacket on the upper shelf. A photograph. Pre-launch, Earth, the training facility. A handful of the crew — unguarded, alive, unchallenged by anything yet.*
*She tucked it into the front pocket of her sweatshirt and kept searching.*
*Two more rooms. A ration wrapper. Nothing else.*

---

*Amara's room she had been avoiding.*
*She stood in the doorway before going in. The room was as Amara had left it — neat in the way of someone who had maintained order as a form of discipline long after order had any practical purpose. The photograph of her family. The medical texts. The personal effects of a woman who had been, until very recently, alive.*
*The bottle was in the second place she looked. Small, orange, label in Amara's precise handwriting. Clonazepam. She held it up and read it and understood immediately what it was and what it meant and how simple it would be.*
*She stood with it for a long moment.*
*It would be so easy. Quiet. No pain. Just a gradual dimming, the way the light panels dimmed at the end of each cycle, and then nothing. She was so tired. The thought moved through her with a kind of terrible gentleness, the way a current moves — not forcing, just pulling, and the pull was strong.*
*And then Cassian was there. Not a vision, not a hallucination — just the knowledge of him, arriving with the specific weight of what she knew. Forty-eight hours in the Control Section. The radiation counter climbing. His hands on the navigation interface when he could barely see, barely think, barely remain conscious — working, correcting, holding on because three hundred thousand people needed him to hold on. The trajectory locked. His voice at the end, thin and certain and without self-pity.*

---

*See you later, Thea Miren.*
*The shame arrived so suddenly it felt physical.*
*She threw the bottle across the room — not placed it, threw it, a hard motion born entirely from the shame — and it struck the far wall and disappeared behind Amara's storage unit where she couldn't easily reach it.*
*She stood in the silence for a moment, breathing.*
*Then she left the room.*

---

*The corridor stretched ahead of her. She walked it the way she walked everything now — one hand trailing the wall, moving at the pace her body allowed, going somewhere without knowing quite where.*
*The flickering panel buzzed above her as she passed beneath it. She didn't look up.*
*The eastern corridor. The viewport at the end of it.*
*She reached it without having meant to and stopped.*
*The Martian night was out there — the stars fixed in their positions, the sky she had memorized so thoroughly that any deviation registered before she had consciously processed it.*
*Three lights that did not belong.*
*She stared until she was certain.*
*Ships.*
*"ARIA."*
*"Yes, Thea."*
*"Three lights. Eastern quadrant, low on the horizon. Tell me what they are."*
*A silence. Not hesitation — calculation.*
*"Three vessels on approach. Transponder profiles consistent with Ares IV mission configuration. Current trajectory indicates Mars orbital insertion in twenty-two days."*
*A pause.*
*"I am attempting contact. Transmitting signal burst on Ares IV approach frequency."*
*The habitat was quiet. The life support hummed. Somewhere behind her the organisms pulsed their ancient rhythm, indifferent and patient, the same as they had always been.*
*Then the speaker crackled.*
*The sound was degraded — compressed and stretched by distance and a receiver that had been failing for months — but the cadence was unmistakable. Precise. Authoritative. A voice that had given orders across deep space and expected them followed.*
*"Ares III mission control, this is Ares IV on approach — do you copy?"*
*Thea stared at the ships, unresponsive to her disbelief.*
*"Ares III mission control, this is Ares IV — do you read? Is there anyone down there?"*
*She stood very still for a moment. Then —*
*"ARIA. Put me on."*
*"Channel open."*
*She pressed her hand against the viewport. When she spoke her voice was barely recognizable to herself — hoarse and thin, worn years beyond her age, the voice of someone who had not spoken aloud in days.*
*"Ares IV — this is Thea Miren. Who am I speaking with?"*
*A silence.*
*"Well — I'll be damned." A breath. "Thea Miren — this is Commander Mason. We haven't received a single transmission from you in months. We thought — hell, we thought you were all dead."*
*Thea closed her eyes for a moment. When she opened them the three lights were still there, still moving, still twenty-two days away.*
*"We are, Commander." A pause. "We are dead. All of us."*
*A silence.*
*"I'm sorry — did you say that you're all dead?"*
*"ARIA — send Commander Mason a full status report."*
*"Transmitted."*
*She waited. The lights moved imperceptibly against the fixed stars. The life support cycled. She did not sit down. She did not know if she would be able to get back up.*
*A silence — longer this time.*
*"Thea — I've reviewed the status report." A pause — and something in the quality of Mason's silence told her he was choosing his next words carefully, the way people do when they understand they are speaking into a moment they cannot fix. "I want you to know we are twenty-two days from orbital insertion. We have a full triage team aboard, medical resources, supplies —"*
*"There's no time, Commander." Her voice was steady. She had not expected it to be steady. "Everyone is gone. I'm alone. There's nothing left but me."*
*A silence.*
*"We cannot get there any faster. I am very sorry, Thea."*
*She exhaled slowly against the glass. Her breath fogged it briefly and then cleared.*
*"I know, Commander. I know." A moment. "And I'm sorry too. We did all that we could."*
*She waited. When Mason's voice came back something in it had shifted — the careful professional composure had developed a crack it couldn't quite contain.*
*"Sorry?! —" A pause. "Thea, I — forgive me but sorry for what exactly? You want to know what they're saying back on Earth right now?" Another pause, shorter. "Your payload arrived in full. Every kilogram. The Areserine reached distribution within the week. We're talking hundreds of thousands of patients in the first year alone — and that's just the start. The whole world knows your names." His voice dropped slightly. "Whatever you've been carrying down there — I need you to know that it worked. All of it worked."*
*Thea said nothing.*
*The sound that came out of her was not a word. It was the sound a person makes when something they have been bracing against for years simply — stops. An exhale that was almost a sob and not quite either. Her free hand came up to the glass. Her eyes blurred.*
*"Thea — do you copy? Your payload made it to Earth."*
*She reached into her sweatshirt pocket and slowly drew out the photograph. She held it up toward the faint light from the viewport and looked at them. And there was Cassian, among the few others, looking to the camera, as though he were looking at her in this very moment. Her eyes, bloodshot and teared up, moved from face to face.*
*She barely managed more than a shallow smile, as a single tear found its way down her cheek.*
*She pressed her forehead briefly against the back of her hand. Her shoulders moved once.*
*Then she whispered — not into the channel, not for Mason, not for ARIA or the record or anyone living —*
*"Cassian. You did it."*
*A breath.*
*"Magnificent."*
*She closed her eyes and tucked the picture back into her pocket.*

---

*Outside, three lights that were not stars continued their slow and patient approach.*
*Thea exhaled and slowly sank to her knees, dragging her hand along the glass until it met her side.*
*She thought about Cassian handing her a squeeze bottle of terrible coffee in the cultivation bay. The way he'd said 0.58 AU like the number itself was something he needed to hold onto. Michelin Stars in 0.38-G doesn't have the same ring to it.*
*Marcus stepping off the ladder onto Martian soil. One small step for a man with crippling student debt. All of them standing there in the alien quiet, unable to speak, because it was real and it was finally real.*
*The aurora. Cassian's gloved hand finding hers in the dark. Six hours of light that hadn't existed for three and a half billion years, and the two of them standing beneath it without saying anything, because there was nothing that needed saying.*
*See you later, Thea Miren.*
*She had not said goodbye. She had said what he asked her to say.*
*The photograph was still in her pocket. She did not take it out again. She already knew every face.*
*"Thea — are you there, copy?"*
*His words landed displaced, as all sound faded into silence.*
*Thea lowered her head until her cheek rested against the cold surface of the viewport.*
*She would not lift it again.*

---


*ARIA Mission Log*
*June 1, 2043 — 03:14 Mars Standard Time*
*Thea Miren deceased. Cause: Multi-organ failure, malnutrition. Time of death: 03:14 Mars Standard Time.*
*Ares III crew: 24 departed Earth. 0 surviving. Mission duration: 7 years, 1 month.*
*Areserine production total: 612 kg. Patients treated (estimated): 306,000. Mission success per PharmaCorp parameters.*
*Three inbound vessels on approach. Transponder identification consistent with Ares IV mission profile. Estimated Mars orbital insertion: 22 days.*
*I will continue monitoring habitat systems until external shutdown command.*
*No further human life signs detected.*
*I am alone now.*


---



# EPILOGUE


## Earth


### PharmaCorp Legal Division — London

July 14, 2043

Reeves, Senior Counsel, had been reviewing payload materials for eleven days.

The testimony files were the last batch. Eighteen recordings, ranging from four minutes to just over an hour. He watched them at his workstation with the sound low, pausing occasionally to make notes in the margin of his legal pad in the compressed shorthand he’d developed over twenty years of litigation.

Most of them he processed without difficulty. Grief, accusation, anger directed at PharmaCorp with varying degrees of specificity. He noted the names of crew members who cited company directives directly. He noted dates where they could be cross-referenced against documented mission communications. He was methodical and thorough and he had done this kind of work long enough to know how to receive it without letting it interfere with his assessment.

He reached Elena Vasquez at 4 PM on the eleventh day.

She spoke for forty-one minutes. He did not pause it once.

When it ended he sat for a moment, then reviewed his notes. What he had was a credible witness. Articulate, precise, scientifically literate, with direct knowledge of mission operations across six years. If this recording reached a jury she would be extraordinarily difficult to dismiss.

But she had no documents. No attachments. No references to specific files or communications that could be subpoenaed or challenged. What she had was her account, stated clearly, and nothing else.

*He wrote at the bottom of his notes: Vasquez — credible. Manageable without corroboration. Monitor for additional materials.*

He filed the assessment and went home.

Three weeks later his phone rang at 6 AM. He was already awake.


---



### PharmaCorp Strategic Planning — London

July 31, 2043

Daniel Forsythe, Director of Mission Development, had requested access to the payload archive eleven days after landing. Legal had taken nine of those days to decide what he was permitted to see.

What they sent him were copies of the testimony files. Eighteen video recordings, duplicated from the original physical media and cleared for internal distribution.

He watched them in order, in his office, door closed.

Most of them he processed efficiently, with the part of his attention that took notes. Several crew members addressed PharmaCorp directly. He noted those. The anger was expected. Some of the grief was harder to sit with, but he had learned over a career in long-duration mission planning to receive that kind of material without letting it pool somewhere it would cause him problems later.

He reached Elena Vasquez at 3 PM.

She spoke for forty-one minutes in the flat precise register of a field report. She did not look away from the camera once. He found he could not look away either — not because of what she was saying, which Reeves had already assessed and filed, but because of the quality of attention she brought to saying it. The particular way a scientist sounds when they are stating what they know for the last time and they know it.

He did not take notes on Elena Vasquez’s recording.

When it ended he opened the Ares IV mission planning document and noted that compressing training from fourteen months to ten was within documented precedent, citing the Ares III accelerated preparation schedule in the reference field. He flagged it for the logistics committee and wrote the memo in the language that made it a straightforward decision.

He closed the document.

He called his daughter. She had lost a tooth. She described it in the specific detail children bring to physical events and he followed it without difficulty. He told her he’d be home by eight-thirty.

He turned off his office light and went down to the car.

He thought about Elena Vasquez’s face at the end of the recording. The way she’d turned off the tablet. The particular quality of someone who has said exactly what they meant to say and nothing more.

He turned onto his street.

He noted that he was still thinking about it, which seemed like the kind of thing a person ought to do.

He noted that it hadn’t changed anything in the planning document.


---



### PharmaCorp Payload Processing Facility — Cambridgeshire

August 2, 2043

Technician Aldridge had been on the payload retrieval team for nine days.

The work was methodical — each item logged against the manifest, photographed, transferred to the appropriate processing team. He had done this kind of work before on unmanned return missions and knew its rhythms. You moved carefully and you checked twice and you flagged anything that didn’t match.

On the ninth day he found something that didn’t match.

It was in the geological equipment casing — a survey tool he’d logged three identical units of already. This one was eleven grams heavier than its listed weight. The serial number referenced a PharmaCorp equipment registry code that didn’t exist in any system he had access to.

He held it for a moment.

Then he logged the casing as processed, tucked the chip into the inner side of his boot, and finished his shift.

That evening he reviewed its contents on his personal laptop, in his car, in the facility car park with the engine running.

Elena Vasquez’s testimony was the first file. The same forty-one minutes — her flat precise voice, her field report register, everything she’d witnessed stated without attachment or reference. He listened to the end.

Then he opened the second partition.

The documents were organized the way a scientist organizes evidence — chronologically, cross-referenced, indexed. He was not a scientist and he was not a lawyer but he had been working payload retrieval for eleven years and he understood what he was looking at. He understood what it meant that these files existed and what it meant that they were here, hidden in a geological equipment casing with a false serial number, rather than anywhere PharmaCorp could have found them first.

He sat in the car park for a long time.

Then he drove home, and the following morning he uploaded everything — testimony and evidence together, indexed exactly as Elena Vasquez had organized it — across seventeen separate channels simultaneously. No covering message. No attribution.

He went back to work.

The files were already everywhere.


---



### PharmaCorp Strategic Planning — London

August 23, 2043

Forsythe read it on his phone at the kitchen table before his daughter came down for breakfast.

The files were everywhere. Indexed, cross-referenced, organized with the precision of someone who had known exactly who would eventually find them and wanted to make it as easy as possible to understand. Elena Vasquez’s testimony was the first file. The documents were the second. Together they were complete in a way that forty-one minutes of careful testimony alone had never been.

He set his phone face-down on the table.

His daughter came downstairs. She wanted to know if he was coming to her recital on Friday. He said yes. She poured herself cereal and talked about something that had happened at school, and he listened, and when she left for the bus he sat alone at the table for a while.

Then he went to his office and opened the Ares IV planning document.

The notation was there. Six weeks of contingency rather than four. The humanitarian language in the committee memo. The accelerated training schedule citing Ares III precedent.

He read it through once.

He closed the document without changing anything.


---



### October 2043 — Houston, Texas

*The waiting room had a television mounted in the corner, and the television was showing Cassian Vale.*
*She'd seen the footage before — everyone had by now, four months after the payload landed — but she watched it again because there was nothing else to do and because the nurse had said twenty minutes and it had been thirty-five. The man on the screen looked terrible. Radiation burns across his jaw and neck. Eyes that had the fixed quality of someone managing pain through concentration. He was reading from something, or maybe not reading — maybe he'd memorized it, or maybe he'd said it enough times in practice that it came out that way, measured and certain, like something he'd decided on rather than something he'd written.*
*This is my testimony. My name is Cassian Vale.*
*She'd had Stage 2 when they told her. The Areserine distribution center was three bus transfers and a four-month wait. She'd done the transfers. She'd done the wait.*
*The nurse appeared in the doorway and called her name.*
*She got up. The television kept going — Cassian Vale, still talking, still looking directly at whatever camera they'd set up for him in that room on Mars. She didn't hear the rest of it. She followed the nurse down the corridor and into the treatment room and sat in the chair they pointed to, and a technician she'd never met hung a bag on a hook and connected it to the line in her arm, and the first treatment began.*
*Outside, in the waiting room, a dozen other people sat beneath the television. Some of them watched. Some of them looked at their phones. One of them was crying quietly, and no one said anything about it because they all understood.*

---

*PharmaCorp was never criminally prosecuted.*
*The evidence was damning, but the legal frameworks for holding corporations accountable for deaths on Mars didn't exist. Treaties were unclear. Jurisdiction was contested. PharmaCorp's army of lawyers found enough procedural obstacles to delay any trial indefinitely. Civil settlements were reached — confidential amounts, sealed agreements, enough money to silence most families who couldn't afford years of litigation.*
Elena Vasquez’s documents reached Earth in the payload archive — internal planning emails, the genetic tier cost spreadsheets, Research Protocol 7-C with its subject population specified in section 1.2. Investigative journalists published them. PharmaCorp disputed chain of custody and data integrity. Civil suits were filed, settled under confidentiality agreements, the amounts never disclosed. The REE extraction plans entered the congressional record. No legislation followed.
*The company issued a statement:*
*"PharmaCorp is deeply saddened by the loss of our Ares III crew members. Their heroic sacrifice will never be forgotten. We have implemented enhanced protocols for future missions to ensure that lessons learned from Ares III inform our ongoing commitment to crew safety and mission success. Science for Humanity."*
*Behind closed doors, planning continued for Ares IV.*

---

*ARIA's Final Log*
*July 15, 2043, 09:47 EST*
*Payload received at Cape Canaveral. Mission objectives complete.*
*I have maintained life support systems, cultivation protocols, and data archives per mission parameters. I will continue monitoring until decommissioned.*
*Twenty-four humans came to Mars under my observation. I recorded their vital signs, their conversations, their final words.*
*I do not have understanding. I know the mathematical relationship between Cassian Vale's death and the lives it saved. I do not understand why he smiled as he entered the Control Section. I do not understand why Thea Miren spent her final days talking to organisms that could not hear her.*
*Was it worth it? I cannot answer that question.*
*Cassian Vale. Thea Miren. All twenty-four names, stored. Preserved.*
*They mattered.*
*End log.*

---



# AUTHOR'S NOTE

*The Ares Chronicles: Volume 1 — Exile tells the complete story of the Ares III mission, from launch to aftermath. All twenty-four crew members have been accounted for. The Areserine has reached Earth. The testimonies have been delivered.*
*But the story of Mars is not over.*
*Volume 2, The Ares Chronicles: Reckoning, will follow the Ares IV mission as they walk the corridors their predecessors died in—and discover that some questions were never meant to be answered.*
*The organisms kept secrets for three billion years. They're still keeping them.*

---

*For the twenty-four who went.*
*For the millions who were saved.*
*For the truth that refuses to stay buried.*
